The Influence of Natural Science on Divine Science
Ghazali discusses how natural science influences divine science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the study of natural science, with its vast and complex cosmic secrets, affects theological investigations.

Core Idea
Natural science's secrets shape the course of divine inquiry.

In this passage, Imam Ghazali explains that natural science and divine science are deeply intertwined, making it hard to separate them. He argues that the secrets of the universe—too vast, subtle, and complex for full human comprehension—nevertheless influence theological investigations, causing them to expand or contract, speed up or slow down, depending on what is revealed. This sets the stage for identifying specific natural issues that have serious implications for divine science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author describes the interconnectedness of the sciences.
The author uses a metaphor of things interlinked like rings of a chain.
2
Identify the claim about natural science's influence
Find the sentence where the author states that natural science has an influence on divine science in its advance and retreat. This is the main thesis of this section.
3
Understand the characteristics of cosmic secrets
The author lists three characteristics: too great, too subtle, too complex. Reflect on why these qualities would cause a reaction in divine investigations.
This explains why divine science is not static.
4
Consider the overall effect
The author says divine investigations proceed according to what is revealed of cosmic secrets, in terms of breadth, speed, and ease. Think about how this challenges the idea that theology is purely deductive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آخذ بعضها برقاب بعض ، حتى ما يكاد القارىء يستبين نقطة الفصل بينهما ضمنها كتابه ( الإشارات والتنبيهات » وعسانا بعد كل هذا لا نجد صعوبة في أن نسلم أن لحركة « العلم الطبيعي » في تقدمه وتأخره - تأثيراً على العلم الإلهى ، في تقدمه وتأخره ، كذلك والعلم الطبيعى هو علم الأسرار الكونية ، وهذه الأسرار يبدو أعظم من أن تستوعبها كلها طاقة إنسان · أنها وأدق من أن يتكشف له منها ما يتكشف ، في سرعة خاطفة وأعقد من أن تفهم على وجهها الصحيح في سهولة ويسر . ولهذا كله رد فعل على البحوث الإلهية ، فهى تسير في مدار ما تكشف لنا ، ويتكشف ، من أسرار هذا الكون : سعة وضيقاً · وسرعة وبطئاً وسهولة وصعوبة · ولقد كان لمسألتين من مسائل العلم الطبيعي ، وما يزال ، أثرهما الخطير في محيط العلم الإلهى تانكم هما : أولا : قدم العالم وأبديته . ثانياً : استحقاق المادة للوجود ، هل تستحقه بذاتها ؟ أم تستحقه بمعاونة من غيرها ؟ ويتصل بهاتين المسألتين ( الطبيعيتين» مسائل من العلم الإلهى : منها : مدى سلطة الإله ، وحدود إرادته · وتفسير الجنة والنار ، والثواب والعقاب الأخرويين .
It interlinks some with others, so much so that the reader can hardly discern the point of separation between them. He included it in his book 'al-Isharat wa al-Tanbihat'. And perhaps after all this we will find no difficulty in acknowledging that the movement of 'natural science' in its advance and retreat has an influence on divine science, in its advance and retreat as well. And natural science is the science of cosmic secrets, and these secrets appear too great for all of a man's capacity to encompass them, and too subtle for him to have revealed to him what is revealed, in a fleeting speed, and too complex to be understood correctly with ease and facility. For all this, there is a reaction upon divine investigations, for they proceed in the orbit of what is revealed to us, and what is revealed, of the secrets of this universe: in breadth and narrowness, in speed and slowness, in ease and difficulty. And there were, and still are, two issues from the issues of natural science that have a serious impact in the realm of divine science: first: the eternity of the world and its perpetuity; second: the matter's deserving existence—does it deserve it by itself? Or does it deserve it with the assistance of something else? And connected to these two (natural) issues are issues from divine science, among them: the extent of the authority of God and the limits of His will; and the interpretation of Paradise and Hellfire, and the rewards and punishments of the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is an introductory classification and argument about the relationship between natural science (al-ʿilm al-ṭabīʿī) and divine science (al-ʿilm al-ilāhī). The author begins by noting how intertwined these fields are, making it difficult to separate them. He then states that natural science—the study of cosmic secrets—influences divine science in its progress and regress. The secrets of the universe are vast, subtle, and complex, and thus they cause a reaction in theological inquiries, which move according to what is revealed of these secrets. The author then identifies two specific natural-science issues that have a serious impact on divine science: (1) the eternity of the world and its perpetuity, and (2) whether matter deserves existence by itself or only with external assistance. He further notes that connected to these two natural issues are several divine issues: the extent of God's authority and the limits of His will, and the interpretation of Paradise, Hellfire, and eschatological rewards and punishments. This sets up the context for the subsequent critique of certain claims about self-evident principles.

Key Terms
علم طبيعي
علم إلهي
أسرار كونية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the relationship between natural science and divine science?
A) They are completely separate.
B) Natural science has no effect on divine science.
C) Natural science influences divine science in its advance and retreat.
D) Divine science determines natural science.

Key Takeaway
The secrets of natural science shape the trajectory of divine theological inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the complexity of the natural world, do you feel overwhelmed and turn away from God, or does it drive you to deeper inquiry? This lesson shows that what we learn from nature shapes our divine understanding. What specific natural phenomenon has affected your view of God? How will you intentionally seek cosmic secrets to draw closer to Him, not away?
?
This passage warns that divine investigations are limited by what is revealed of cosmic secrets. But how much effort do you invest in uncovering those secrets through study and contemplation? Are you satisfied with a surface-level faith, or will you commit to the rigorous pursuit of knowledge—both natural and divine—even when it is difficult, slow, and narrow? What will you change starting today?

Two Natural Science Issues and Their Divine Implications
Ghazali enumerates two natural issues affecting divine science and lists connected divine issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two specific natural science issues that Ghazali identifies as having serious impact on divine science, and the divine issues connected to them.

Core Idea
The eternity of the world and matter's self-sufficiency are natural issues that challenge divine teachings.

Having established the general influence of natural science on divine science, Ghazali now names two particular natural issues that have historically had a serious impact: whether the world is eternal and whether matter deserves existence by itself or with assistance. These seemingly natural questions lead to profound divine issues such as the extent of God's authority, the limits of His will, and the interpretation of Paradise and Hell. This lesson unpacks these connections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation. Note the two natural issues and the divine issues connected to them.
2
Identify the two natural issues
The first issue is the eternity of the world (قدم العالم). The second is whether matter deserves existence by itself or with assistance.
These are fundamental questions in natural philosophy.
3
Understand the divine implications
The author lists three divine issues connected to these natural issues: the extent of God's authority, limits of His will, and interpretation of afterlife.
This shows how natural assumptions can affect theology.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these particular natural issues would have such a serious impact. Think about how answering them differently would change theological conclusions.

Source Text

According to the passage, which natural issue concerns whether matter deserves existence by itself?
A) The eternity of the world
B) The matter's deserving existence

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that scientific theories about the universe's origins have no bearing on your faith? How does Ghazali's warning about the 'serious impact' of natural issues on divine science force you to reexamine that assumption? Commit to spending five minutes today identifying one assumption you hold about the world that might be shaping your view of God without you realizing it.
?
When you think about Paradise and Hellfire, do you sincerely believe that your actions in this life have eternal weight, or does a hidden assumption about matter's self-sufficiency make you doubt the reality of the afterlife? Ask yourself: 'Do I live today as if my reward or punishment depends entirely on God's will, or do I act as if the world runs on its own?' Write down one change in behavior this awareness demands.
?
The passage ties the limits of God's will to natural assumptions about the world. Do you, in moments of difficulty, truly believe that God's will is unlimited—that He can intervene in every detail of your life—or do you unconsciously accept natural limitations that shrink His authority? Pray now for the conviction to trust His absolute power and for the courage to submit to His will completely.

The Two Impossibilities and the Critique of Claiming Self-Evidence
Ghazali states the two propositions and critiques the claim of self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the claim that the two impossibilities are self-evident to be a diversion from true examination.

Core Idea
Self-evidence is not a valid argument; it can be a shortcut to avoid thinking.

Ghazali opens by stating two propositions that philosophers often take as self-evident: that creation ex nihilo and annihilation are impossible. He then directly challenges the reader, arguing that labeling these as self-evident underestimates their complexity and discourages critical thought. This lesson examines the initial statement and Ghazali's warning about the misuse of 'self-evidence'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Ghazali first states the two propositions and then immediately criticizes the claim of self-evidence.
2
Identify the two propositions
The two propositions are 'the impossibility of creating something from nothing' and 'the impossibility of rendering something into nothing'. Find them in the Arabic.
They are the first two phrases.
3
Understand Ghazali's critique
Ghazali argues that calling these self-evident is an underestimation that diverts the intellect. Why does he think that? Consider that self-evident claims are not subject to examination.
He uses strong language: 'استهانة' (underestimation) and 'صرف للعقل' (diversion of intellect).
4
Reflect on the implication
What is the danger of claiming something is self-evident? According to Ghazali, it leads to complacency and stops inquiry. Do you agree?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عدم إمكان خلق شيء من لا شيء وعدم إمكان تصيير شيء إلى لا شيء .
أمور أولية ، تفصح عن أوليتيهما ضرورة العقل و بداهته
وأصارحك أيها القارئ أن دعوى البداهة في هاتين المسألتين ، استهانة بشأنهما ، وصرف للعقل عن فحصهما وتمعنهما ، إنها دعوة إلى الاستنامة والاطمئنان ، وما أظن أن العقل اليقظ يشعر بالرضى والاطمئنان حيال دعوى خطيرة كدعوى بداهة هاتين المسألتين فأين كانت البداهة يوم كان يظن أن ما تحرقه النار ، تهلكه وتفنيه ؟ هل تولدت في النفوس وتقررت في الأذهان منذ اختراع الجهاز الذي أثبت أن المتحلل من الشمعة المحترقة ، يساوي وزنها قبل الاحتراق ؟ والذى أثبت ، تبعاً لذلك ، أن الاحتراق ليس إفناء ، وأن المحترق لا يفنى ! ! هل تولدت البداهة فى النفوس، وتقررت فى الأذهان ، منذ ذلك الوقت فقط ؟ وعن هذا الطريق فقط ؟
وهل يمكن أن تعتمد البداهة على تجربة ، أو على دليل ؟
ثم إذا كانت النار لا تفنى ما تحرقه ، فهل انحصرت وسائل الإفناء في الإحراق ؟
وهل إذا كان العلم البشرى ، لم يقف إلى الآن ، على وسائل أعظم من النار خطراً ، وأشد منها بأساً ، يكون معنى ذلك أن مثل هذه الوسائل لم توجد ، ولا يمكن أن توجد ؟
وهل عدم الوجدان يدل على عدم الوجود ؟
وهل أخضع قدرة الإله - من يعتقد أن هناك إلهاً ـ للتجربة ، فقاس حدودها ، وعرف ما يدخل في هذه الحدود ، وما يخرج عنها ، وحكم بناء على ذلك بأن تصيير الشيء عدماً صرفاً، خارج عن حدود المقدور ؟
The impossibility of creating something from nothing and the impossibility of rendering something into nothing. They are primary things, revealing their primacy by the necessity of intellect and its self-evidence. And I declare to you, O reader, that claiming self-evidence (badāhah) in these two issues is an underestimation of their importance, and a diversion of the intellect from examining and pondering them. It is a call to complacency and reassurance. I do not think that the vigilant intellect feels satisfaction and reassurance regarding a serious claim like the claim of self-evidence of these two issues. So where was self-evidence when it was thought that what fire burns is destroyed and annihilated? Did it arise in souls and become established in minds only since the invention of the device that proved that the decomposed substance of a burnt candle equals its weight before burning? And which proved, accordingly, that burning is not annihilation, and that what is burnt does not perish!! Did self-evidence arise in souls and become established in minds only from that time and only by that path? And can self-evidence rely on experience or on a proof? Then, if fire does not annihilate what it burns, are the means of annihilation restricted to burning? And if human science has not yet known means more dangerous than fire and more severe, does that mean that such means do not exist and cannot exist? Does the absence of finding indicate the absence of existence? And does one who believes there is a God subject God's power to experience, measuring its limits, knowing what enters these limits and what goes out, and thus judge that rendering a thing into pure nothing is beyond the limits of the possible?
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the claim that the impossibility of creation ex nihilo and the impossibility of annihilation are self-evident (badīhī). Ghazali begins by stating the two propositions: the impossibility of creating something from nothing and the impossibility of turning something into nothing. He notes that some consider these to be primary truths disclosed by the necessity of reason and its self-evidence. However, he then addresses the reader directly, arguing that claiming self-evidence for these issues is an underestimation and a way to divert the intellect from proper examination. He asks where this self-evidence was when people believed that fire destroys what it burns. Only after the invention of a device showing that the weight of the burnt candle equals its original weight did people realize that burning is not annihilation. Thus, self-evidence cannot be based on historical change or empirical discovery. He questions whether self-evidence can rely on experience or proof at all. Then, even if fire does not annihilate, are there other means of annihilation? Does our lack of knowledge of them prove they don't exist? Finally, he criticizes those who limit God's power based on empirical observation, asking whether they have subjected divine power to experimentation to declare that rendering something into nothing is beyond God's capability. The passage thus challenges the foundation of the claim, showing that self-evidence is not a valid basis for these metaphysical assertions.

Key Terms
بداهة
أمور أولية
خلق شيء من لا شيء
تصيير شيء إلى لا شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a proposition is self-evident, it requires no proof or examination.
Correction: Ghazali shows that claiming self-evidence can be a rhetorical device to avoid scrutiny, and what seems self-evident may change with knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say about the claim of self-evidence in these two issues?
A) It is a strong argument.
B) It is an underestimation and diversion.
C) It is the only way to understand them.
D) It is supported by empirical evidence.

Key Takeaway
Claiming self-evidence can be a way to avoid critical examination, and the two impossibilities are not simply self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What beliefs do you hold that you have never truly examined because they seem 'obvious'? Will you commit to investigating one this week, no matter how uncomfortable it makes you?
?
When someone tells you something is self-evident, do you feel a sense of relief or do you, like Ghazali's vigilant intellect, feel a warning? How will you train yourself to resist complacency?
?
Is there an area of your life—faith, ethics, daily habits—where you have accepted a claim without questioning it, simply because it felt reassuring? What would it cost you to honestly scrutinize it?

The Candle Example: Challenging Self-Evidence with Empirical Discovery
Ghazali uses the candle example and rhetorical questions to further critique self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the example of the burning candle to argue that what is considered self-evident can change with new knowledge.

Core Idea
Self-evidence is not eternal; it can be overturned by empirical discovery.

In this passage, Ghazali continues his critique by asking where self-evidence was when people believed fire annihilates matter. He recounts the discovery that the mass of a burnt candle remains, showing that what was once thought obvious turned out to be false. He then questions whether self-evidence can ever rely on experience or proof, and challenges the attempt to limit God's power based on human knowledge. This lesson explores these powerful arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the rhetorical questions Ghazali poses.
2
Analyze the candle example
Ghazali points out that before a scientific device showed that the mass of a burnt candle is conserved, people thought fire annihilates matter. This shows that self-evidence can be wrong.
The example demonstrates that empirical discovery can overturn self-evident beliefs.
3
Examine the questions
Ghazali asks a series of questions: Can self-evidence rely on experience? Are means of annihilation limited to fire? Does lack of knowledge prove non-existence? These questions undermine the claim that the impossibility of annihilation is self-evident.
Each question exposes a flaw in the assumption.
4
Reflect on the final question
Ghazali ends by asking whether anyone who believes in God would subject divine power to empirical limits. This is a powerful theological point: God's power cannot be confined by human experiments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أمور أولية ، تفصح عن أوليتيهما ضرورة العقل و بداهته

The impossibility of creating something from nothing and the impossibility of rendering something into nothing. They are primary things, revealing their primacy by the necessity of intellect and its self-evidence. And I declare to you, O reader, that claiming self-evidence (badāhah) in these two issues is an underestimation of their importance, and a diversion of the intellect from examining and pondering them. It is a call to complacency and reassurance. I do not think that the vigilant intellect feels satisfaction and reassurance regarding a serious claim like the claim of self-evidence of these two issues. So where was self-evidence when it was thought that what fire burns is destroyed and annihilated? Did it arise in souls and become established in minds only since the invention of the device that proved that the decomposed substance of a burnt candle equals its weight before burning? And which proved, accordingly, that burning is not annihilation, and that what is burnt does not perish!! Did self-evidence arise in souls and become established in minds only from that time and only by that path? And can self-evidence rely on experience or on a proof? Then, if fire does not annihilate what it burns, are the means of annihilation restricted to burning? And if human science has not yet known means more dangerous than fire and more severe, does that mean that such means do not exist and cannot exist? Does the absence of finding indicate the absence of existence? And does one who believes there is a God subject God's power to experience, measuring its limits, knowing what enters these limits and what goes out, and thus judge that rendering a thing into pure nothing is beyond the limits of the possible?
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the claim that the impossibility of creation ex nihilo and the impossibility of annihilation are self-evident (badīhī). Ghazali begins by stating the two propositions: the impossibility of creating something from nothing and the impossibility of turning something into nothing. He notes that some consider these to be primary truths disclosed by the necessity of reason and its self-evidence. However, he then addresses the reader directly, arguing that claiming self-evidence for these issues is an underestimation and a way to divert the intellect from proper examination. He asks where this self-evidence was when people believed that fire destroys what it burns. Only after the invention of a device showing that the weight of the burnt candle equals its original weight did people realize that burning is not annihilation. Thus, self-evidence cannot be based on historical change or empirical discovery. He questions whether self-evidence can rely on experience or proof at all. Then, even if fire does not annihilate, are there other means of annihilation? Does our lack of knowledge of them prove they don't exist? Finally, he criticizes those who limit God's power based on empirical observation, asking whether they have subjected divine power to experimentation to declare that rendering something into nothing is beyond God's capability. The passage thus challenges the foundation of the claim, showing that self-evidence is not a valid basis for these metaphysical assertions.

Key Terms
بداهة
تجربة
إفناء
احتراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident truths are permanent and cannot be contradicted by experience.
Correction: Ghazali shows that empirical discovery can overturn what was once considered self-evident, so self-evidence is not a reliable criterion.

Checkpoint
What does the example of the burning candle demonstrate according to Ghazali?
A) Fire annihilates matter.
B) Self-evidence can change with new knowledge.
C) The impossibility of annihilation is proven.
D) Empirical evidence is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali uses the candle example to show that self-evidence is not fixed and can be overturned by empirical discovery, and he questions the use of self-evidence as an argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about yourself, others, or Allah that felt so obvious you never questioned it, only to later discover it was limited or wrong? What would it take for you to examine your current assumptions with the same boldness Ghazali uses here?
?
Ghazali challenges us not to limit God's power by our own lack of experience. In what area of your life have you, perhaps unknowingly, assumed that Allah cannot act because you have not seen it happen? What would change if you truly believed that His power is beyond all human measurement?
?
The candle experiment showed that what seemed like destruction was actually transformation. Is there a trial or loss in your life that you have interpreted as annihilation, but which might be a transformation in ways you cannot yet see? How can you trust Allah's wisdom in that situation starting today?

The Claim of Obviousness is an Underestimation
Ghazali concludes that claiming obviousness is an underestimation and encourages intellectual laziness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the claim of obviousness regarding the impossibility of creation ex nihilo and annihilation to be a methodological error that leads to intellectual laziness.

Core Idea
Claiming obviousness is not proof; it is a shortcut that avoids real inquiry.

Ghazali wraps up his critique of the philosophers' position by focusing on their method. Rather than providing rigorous arguments, they assert that the impossibility of creation from nothing and annihilation is self-evident. Ghazali warns that this attitude underestimates the complexity of the issues and discourages genuine investigation. This lesson examines that warning and its implications for theological method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read Ghazali's concluding words carefully, noting his strong language ('underestimation', 'enticement for the intellect to be lazy').
The passage is cut off at the end; focus on the complete thought before that.
2
Identify the two aspects of his critique
Understand that Ghazali criticizes both the underestimation of the subject and the resulting intellectual laziness.
Underestimation (استهانة) and enticement to laziness (إغراء للعقل بالكسل).
3
Reflect on the methodological significance
Consider why Ghazali insists on thorough examination rather than appeals to obviousness. How does this connect to the broader purpose of kalam?

Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This is Ghazali's concluding reflection on the preceding arguments. He states that the philosophers' claim that the impossibility of creation ex nihilo and annihilation are self-evident truths is actually an underestimation of the complexity of these issues. Such a claim encourages intellectual laziness instead of rigorous investigation. He then begins a new thought about metaphysicians living in a purely intellectual environment (the sentence is cut off), suggesting that even they might need to reconsider. The passage emphasizes the need for careful scrutiny rather than reliance on alleged obviousness.

Key Takeaway
Claiming that a theological position is 'obvious' is not a valid argument; it underestimates the issue and discourages the rigorous examination needed for certainty.

The Mutual Dependence of Metaphysics and Natural Science
Ghazali states that metaphysicians and naturalists each benefit from the other's research.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's view that metaphysics and natural science are interdependent disciplines, each enriching the other.

Core Idea
Metaphysics and natural science need each other—neither is self-sufficient.

This brief passage from the Tahafut makes a crucial methodological point. Ghazali asserts that metaphysicians, despite their purely rational approach, cannot ignore the findings of natural scientists. Conversely, naturalists require the insights of metaphysicians. He then signals that his critique of Galen will illustrate this interdependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall meaning. Note the key terms: ميتافيزيقي, طبيعي, استئناس.
2
Identify the core claim
What does Ghazali say about the relationship between metaphysicians and naturalists? He states each needs to 'benefit from' or 'seek enlightenment from' the other's research.
The word يستأنسوا implies seeking illumination, not mere borrowing.
3
Connect to the example
Ghazali references his stance on Galen as a demonstration of this interdependence. Consider how a concrete case (like Galen's arguments) can illustrate a methodological principle.
This prepares for the subsequent discussion on Galen's views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجعلنى أيضاً أقول : إنه إذا كان الميتافيزيقيون ، الذين يعيشون في محيط عقلى صرف ، بحاجة إلى أن يستأنسوا ببحوث الطبيعيين ؛ فإن الطبيعيين بحاجة إلى أن يستأنسوا أيضاً ببحوث الميتافيزيقيين . وفى موقف للغزالى من جالينوس ، توضيح و بيان لهذه الحاجة

And in the stance of al-Ghazali towards Galen, there is clarification and explanation of this need.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This short passage is a methodological statement. Ghazali asserts a reciprocal relationship between metaphysics and natural science. Metaphysicians, despite their purely rational approach, are not self-sufficient; they require insights from natural science. Conversely, naturalists need the perspective of metaphysicians. He then indicates that his own treatment of Galen's arguments will serve as an example illustrating this interdependence.

Misconception: Metaphysics and natural science are completely independent fields that do not inform each other.

Examine your academic or spiritual life: where have you acted as if you are self-sufficient like the metaphysicians or naturalists Ghazali describes? What is one concrete step you will take this week to seek insight from a source you have ignored?

Galen's Empirical Argument and Ghazali's Rational Response
Describes Galen's argument for the eternity of the world and Ghazali's purely rational stance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's observational argument for the eternity of the world and why Ghazali shifts the debate to a purely logical foundation.

Core Idea
Observation alone cannot settle the deepest questions; logic must rule.

This lesson presents a key moment in the Tahafut: Galen's argument that the sun's apparent stability proves the world is eternal, and Ghazali's refusal to rely on empirical data. Instead, Ghazali insists on a rational critique of the logical form of such arguments. Understanding this sets the stage for the detailed logical refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note both the content (Galen's argument) and the method (empirical vs rational).
2
Identify Galen's argument structure
Find the premise: the sun hasn't changed for millennia. Conclusion: therefore it will not perish. Notice the implied conditional: if it were perishable, we'd see decay.
This is a classic argument from induction.
3
Contrast Ghazali's approach
Ghazali does not dispute the observation but rejects the reasoning. He insists on a purely rational analysis—examining the logical necessity of the connection.
This is the core of Ghazali's critique: the conditional syllogism is incomplete.
4
Reflect on the methodological difference
Consider why Ghazali thinks natural science needs metaphysics. How does this passage illustrate the relationship between empirical observation and logical proof?
This connects to the larger theme of the Tahafut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى أن يستأنسوا ببحوث الطبيعيين ؛ فإن الطبيعيين بحاجة إلى أن يستأنسوا أيضاً ببحوث الميتافيزيقيين. وفي موقف للغزالي من جالينوس، توضيح وبيان لهذه الحاجة. فلقد ذهب جالينوس - مع من ذهبوا - إلى أن العالم أبدي، وقدم بين يدي دعواه هذه، قوله: (إن فساد العالم يكون بتحوله من حال إلى حال، وبانتقاله من شأن إلى شأن. ولكنا نرى الشمس على حالها التي كانت عليها منذ آلاف السنين، لم يعتورها ذبول، ولم يطرأ عليها ضمور. ومعنى هذا أنها لن تفنى، إذ لو كانت في طريقها إلى الفناء، لوجب تعرضها، خلال آلاف السنين التي مرت بها، إلى التحلل والذبول. أما وهي لم تذبل ولم تتحلل، خلال هذه الآماد المديدة، فليس لذلك إلا تفسير واحد، هو أنها ثابتة لا تفنى، باقية لا تزول (۲). هذا ما يقوله «جالينوس» بصدد نظرية «أبدية العالم». ولقد عول «جالينوس»، في حكمه بعدم تعرض الشمس للذبول، خلال عمرها الطويل، على آلات الرصد التي كانت معروفة لعهده. ولكن الغزالي لم يعول على الأرصاد الجوية، ولم يقف من المسألة موقفاً يستمد معلوماته عنها، من العلم الطبيعي، وإنما وقف منها موقفاً عقليا صرفاً، وقف منها موقف المهيمن على البحوث الطبيعية يقومها ويسدد خطاها.
So that they may seek comfort from the researches of the natural scientists; for the natural scientists also need to seek comfort from the researches of the metaphysicians. And in al-Ghazali's position vis-à-vis Galen, there is a clarification and explanation of this need. For Galen — among those who held it — held that the world is eternal, and he presented before this claim of his, his statement: 'The corruption of the world occurs by its transformation from state to state, and by its passing from condition to condition. But we see the sun in the same state it has been for thousands of years, no withering has afflicted it, and no atrophy has occurred to it. And the meaning of this is that it will not perish, for if it were on the path to perdition, it would necessarily have undergone dissolution and withering during the thousands of years that have passed over it. But since it has not withered nor dissolved throughout these extended ages, there is no explanation for this except one: that it is stable, does not perish, everlasting, does not pass away (2).' This is what Galen says regarding the theory of the 'eternity of the world'. And Galen relied, in his judgment that the sun is not subject to withering over its long life, on the observational instruments that were known in his time. But al-Ghazali did not rely on astronomical observations, nor did he take a position on the issue that derives his information about it from natural science. Rather, he took a purely rational position, a position of one who has authority over natural researches, correcting them and rectifying their steps.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage provides background: first, the general principle that natural scientists and metaphysicians need each other's findings. Then it presents Galen's argument for the eternity of the world, which is based on observational evidence: the sun has not changed over millennia, so it cannot be subject to decay, hence it is eternal. Finally, it states that al-Ghazali does not rely on such empirical observations but adopts a purely rational, critical stance. The contrast sets up the logical objections that follow.

Key Terms
طبيعي
ميتافيزيقي
أبدي
الفساد
الذبول
الرصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali rejects all empirical evidence.
Correction: Ghazali does not dispute the observations themselves; he challenges the logical inference from observation to conclusion. He insists that empirical data alone cannot prove metaphysical necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is Ghazali's response to Galen's argument?
A) He relies on more accurate astronomical instruments.
B) He accepts the eternity of the world but with a different reasoning.
C) He takes a purely rational position, not based on natural science.
D) He argues that the sun actually changes.

Key Takeaway
Galen's empirical argument for the world's eternity is met by Ghazali's purely rational critique, emphasizing that logical analysis supersedes observational data in metaphysical matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often trust your own observations to make decisions about faith and life. But when have you confidently concluded something based on what you saw, only to discover later that your logic was flawed? How does Galen's mistake warn you to question your own reasoning?
?
Ghazali did not rely on better instruments; he relied on logic. In what area of your life are you relying on 'observational instruments' (your own experiences, what you see) to draw conclusions about God or truth, while neglecting the logical framework of revelation? Will you submit your observations to rational and scriptural correction today?
?
You may think that because you have 'seen' something consistently, it will always be that way. But Galen's argument about the sun was proven wrong in its implications. What belief about yourself or your circumstances have you assumed is permanent simply because it has been true for a long time? Are you willing to let God's logic break that assumption?

The Paradox of Tahafut as Philosophy
The editor presents the paradox of the Tahafut being treated as philosophy while aiming to destroy it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central paradox: how a book that aims to demolish philosophy can be considered a philosophical work.

Core Idea
Can a book that demolishes philosophy itself be philosophy?

The editor opens by noting that the Tahafut's placement in the philosophy curriculum suggests it is a philosophy book. Yet al-Ghazali explicitly states his purpose is to expose philosophy as nonsense and demolish it. This creates a puzzle that the rest of the passage seeks to resolve.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening paradox
Read the first part of the passage where the editor contrasts the curriculum placement with the book's stated aim. Notice the two rhetorical questions he raises.
These questions set up the need to define philosophy.
2
Identify the two questions
The editor asks: (1) Can philosophy judge itself as nonsense? (2) Can a book proving philosophy futile be philosophy? Consider why these are problematic.
Both questions challenge the assumption that the Tahafut is philosophical.
3
Reflect on the underlying issue
Think about what the editor implies: the answer depends on the definition of philosophy. This prepares for the subsequent analysis.
The definition will determine the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولاً : صلة كتاب تهافت الفلاسفة ، بالفلسفة ، وبالفلسفة الإسلامية على وجه خاص
ولقد يشير إليها وضعه - بجانب كتاب الإشارات لابن سينا - ضمن منهاج الفلسفة للدراسات العالية
--
فإن هذا يعنى - في نظر واضعى البرنامج على الأقل – أن التهافت كالإشارات_كتاب فلسفة .
ولكن كتاب التهافت صريح فى أنه ـ من أوله إلى آخره - بيان لما تنطوي عليه الفلسفة من سخف وهذيان ، وهل يمكن أن تحكم الفلسفة على نفسها بأنها سخف وهذيان ؟
ثم إن مؤلف كتاب التهافت
يصرح في مواضع كثيرة (1) . بأن غرضه من تأليف كتابه هذا هدم الفلسفة وزعزعة ثقة الناس فيها و بيان أنها عمل فاشل غير ناجح ، وهل يكون فلسفة ما يثبت أن الفلسفة عمل فاشل ؟
――
هذان سؤالان يثيرهما اعتبار كتاب » تهافت الفلاسفة » – الذي يحاول هدم الفلسفة - داخلاً ضمن دائرة الفلسفة ، والإجابة عليهما بالسلب أو بالإيجاب ، تتطلب قبلا تحديد معنى الفلسفة فى نظر المسلمين . ففى ضوء هذا التحديد يمكن القول بأن كتاب التهافت داخل ضمن - أو خارج عن – دائرة الفلسفة . فلو اتسع معناها لشمول ما يكون بحثاً فيها وحكماً عليها ، ولو بأنها سخف وهذيان صح اعتبار كتاب التهافت ، كتاب فلسفة ، وإن ضاق معناها عن قبول مثل هذه المحاولة ، لم يصح اعتبار كتاب التهافت كتاب فلسفة
فما هي الفلسفة الإسلامية إذن ؟
أهى مجموعة الآراء التي ارتاها الفارابي وابن سينا ومن نحا نحوهما ، في الله والنفس الإنسانية مثلاً، دون نظر إلى الطريق التى تأدوا منها إلى هذه الآراء ؟
إن كان ذلك هو ما اصطلح على تسميته بالفلسفة الإسلامية ، فكتاب التهافت ليس منها ؛ لأنه محاولة لهدم هذه الآراء والتشنيع عليها . إلا أنه – في هذه الحال لا بد من التساؤل عن مبررات تخصيص ما لابن سينا والفارابى من آراء - دون ما لغيرهما - باسم الفلسفة ؛ فإن قصر اسم الفلسفة على طائفة الأفكار دون طائفة أخرى - إذا لم يدخل في اعتبار المسمى رعاية طريق خاص لاستمداد الأفكار منه ، يكون تحكماً صرفاً .
و إذ لا مبرر يدعو لذلك ، فلابد من البحث لاسم الفلسفة عن معنى آخر . وأظهر ما يقال في هذا الصدد هو أن الفلسفة : العمليات الفكرية ، والمحاولات العقلية التي يراد بها التوصل إلى الحق والاهتداء إلى الصواب
(1) خذ مثلا قوله : « ليعلم أن المقصود تنبيه من حسن اعتقاده في الفلاسفة ، وظن أن مسالكهم نقية عن التناقض ، ببيان وجوه تهافتهم »
وقوله : « ونحن لم نلتزم فى هذا الكتاب إلا تكذيب مذهبهم ، وأما إثبات المذهب الحق فسنصف فيه كتاباً بعد الفراغ من هذا »
First: The connection of the book Tahafut al-Falasifa to philosophy, and to Islamic philosophy in particular.
Its placement - alongside Ibn Sina's Isharat - within the philosophy curriculum for higher studies indicates this.
For this means - in the view of the curriculum designers at least - that the Tahafut, like the Isharat, is a philosophy book.
But the book Tahafut explicitly states that it is - from its beginning to its end - an exposition of the nonsense and drivel contained in philosophy. Can philosophy judge itself to be nonsense and drivel?
Furthermore, the author of the Tahafut declares in many places that his purpose in writing this book is to demolish philosophy, shake people's confidence in it, and show that it is a futile, unsuccessful enterprise. Can something that proves philosophy to be futile be philosophy?
These are two questions raised by considering the book Tahafut al-Falasifa - which attempts to demolish philosophy - as falling within the sphere of philosophy. Answering them negatively or affirmatively requires first determining the meaning of philosophy in the view of Muslims. In light of this determination, it can be said that the Tahafut is either inside or outside the sphere of philosophy. If its meaning is broad enough to include investigation into philosophy and judgment upon it, even if it is nonsense and drivel, then the Tahafut may be considered a philosophy book. But if its meaning is too narrow to accept such an attempt, then the Tahafut cannot be considered a philosophy book.
So what is Islamic philosophy then?
Is it the collection of opinions adopted by al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and those who followed their approach, concerning God and the human soul, for example, without consideration of the method by which they arrived at these opinions? If that is what is conventionally called Islamic philosophy, then the Tahafut is not part of it, because it is an attempt to demolish these opinions and denounce them. However, in this case, one must question the justification for singling out the opinions of Ibn Sina and al-Farabi - to the exclusion of others - under the name philosophy. For restricting the name philosophy to one group of ideas to the exclusion of another, without taking into account a particular method for deriving ideas, is pure arbitrariness.
And since there is no justification for that, one must search for another meaning for the name philosophy. The most obvious thing that can be said in this regard is that philosophy is: the intellectual processes and rational attempts intended to arrive at truth and to be guided to what is correct.
(1) Take, for example, his statement: 'Let it be known that the purpose is to caution those who have a good opinion of the philosophers and think that their methods are free from contradiction, by exposing the aspects of their incoherence.'
And his statement: 'We have not committed ourselves in this book except to refuting their doctrine. As for establishing the true doctrine, we will describe a book on it after finishing this one.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is an argument about whether the Tahafut should be considered a work of philosophy. The editor begins by noting that the Tahafut's inclusion alongside Ibn Sina's Isharat in the philosophy curriculum suggests it is a philosophy book. However, al-Ghazali's own text states that it exposes the nonsense of philosophy and aims to demolish it. This raises two questions: can philosophy judge itself as nonsense, and can a book that proves philosophy futile be considered philosophy? The editor argues that answering these requires defining philosophy from a Muslim perspective. If philosophy is defined broadly to include any inquiry into it, then the Tahafut qualifies. But if it is narrowly defined as the specific opinions of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, then the Tahafut is excluded. The editor criticizes this narrow definition as arbitrary, since it ignores method and merely selects a set of ideas. He then proposes a more universal definition: philosophy is the intellectual and rational processes aimed at attaining truth and right guidance. This definition would encompass the Tahafut as a philosophical work. The footnote provides two quotes from al-Ghazali confirming his intent: to alert those who admire the philosophers and to refute their doctrines, while reserving the positive exposition for another book.

تهافت الفلاسفة
الفلسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the editor say the Tahafut explicitly states about philosophy?
A) It is a perfect system
B) It contains nonsense and drivel
C) It should be taught in universities
D) It is compatible with Islam

Key Takeaway
The Tahafut presents a paradox: it is treated as philosophy yet aims to demolish philosophy, forcing us to examine what philosophy really means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a idea or worldview you strongly disagree with, do you genuinely study it from the inside to understand its claims, or do you dismiss it based on surface-level judgments? What would it cost you to really learn what you oppose before rejecting it?
?
The Tahafut is placed in the philosophy curriculum despite aiming to destroy philosophy. Is there an area in your life where you use the very tools you claim to reject? How honest are you about that contradiction?
?
If proving that philosophy is futile is itself a form of philosophy, then what does that say about your own attempts to argue against something? Are you willing to accept that your critique might also be a form of engagement with the very thing you reject—and what accountability does that place on you to argue with integrity?

Critique of Narrow Definitions of Islamic Philosophy
The editor examines and rejects the narrow definition of Islamic philosophy as the opinions of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the editor rejects the narrow definition of Islamic philosophy as merely the opinions of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
Restricting philosophy to a set of opinions is arbitrary without considering method.

The editor now turns to the question: What is Islamic philosophy? He first considers a narrow definition: the specific opinions of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. He then argues that this definition is arbitrary because it ignores how those opinions were derived. This critique opens the door for a more meaningful definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrow definition
The editor asks: Is Islamic philosophy just the opinions of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina about God and the soul, without regard to method? Read this part carefully.
This is the definition he will reject.
2
Understand the implication for Tahafut
If that definition is accepted, the Tahafut is not philosophy because it attacks those opinions. But the editor then asks: why only these two thinkers?
This reveals the arbitrariness.
3
Grasp the critique of arbitrariness
The editor argues that naming 'philosophy' for a selected set of ideas without considering the method of deriving them is pure arbitrariness.
Method is essential for a proper definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولاً : صلة كتاب تهافت الفلاسفة ، بالفلسفة ، وبالفلسفة الإسلامية على وجه خاص
ولقد يشير إليها وضعه - بجانب كتاب الإشارات لابن سينا - ضمن منهاج الفلسفة للدراسات العالية
--
فإن هذا يعنى - في نظر واضعى البرنامج على الأقل – أن التهافت كالإشارات_كتاب فلسفة .
ولكن كتاب التهافت صريح فى أنه ـ من أوله إلى آخره - بيان لما تنطوي عليه الفلسفة من سخف وهذيان ، وهل يمكن أن تحكم الفلسفة على نفسها بأنها سخف وهذيان ؟
ثم إن مؤلف كتاب التهافت
يصرح في مواضع كثيرة (1) . بأن غرضه من تأليف كتابه هذا هدم الفلسفة وزعزعة ثقة الناس فيها و بيان أنها عمل فاشل غير ناجح ، وهل يكون فلسفة ما يثبت أن الفلسفة عمل فاشل ؟
――
هذان سؤالان يثيرهما اعتبار كتاب » تهافت الفلاسفة » – الذي يحاول هدم الفلسفة - داخلاً ضمن دائرة الفلسفة ، والإجابة عليهما بالسلب أو بالإيجاب ، تتطلب قبلا تحديد معنى الفلسفة فى نظر المسلمين . ففى ضوء هذا التحديد يمكن القول بأن كتاب التهافت داخل ضمن - أو خارج عن – دائرة الفلسفة . فلو اتسع معناها لشمول ما يكون بحثاً فيها وحكماً عليها ، ولو بأنها سخف وهذيان صح اعتبار كتاب التهافت ، كتاب فلسفة ، وإن ضاق معناها عن قبول مثل هذه المحاولة ، لم يصح اعتبار كتاب التهافت كتاب فلسفة
فما هي الفلسفة الإسلامية إذن ؟
أهى مجموعة الآراء التي ارتاها الفارابي وابن سينا ومن نحا نحوهما ، في الله والنفس الإنسانية مثلاً، دون نظر إلى الطريق التى تأدوا منها إلى هذه الآراء ؟
إن كان ذلك هو ما اصطلح على تسميته بالفلسفة الإسلامية ، فكتاب التهافت ليس منها ؛ لأنه محاولة لهدم هذه الآراء والتشنيع عليها . إلا أنه – في هذه الحال لا بد من التساؤل عن مبررات تخصيص ما لابن سينا والفارابى من آراء - دون ما لغيرهما - باسم الفلسفة ؛ فإن قصر اسم الفلسفة على طائفة الأفكار دون طائفة أخرى - إذا لم يدخل في اعتبار المسمى رعاية طريق خاص لاستمداد الأفكار منه ، يكون تحكماً صرفاً .
و إذ لا مبرر يدعو لذلك ، فلابد من البحث لاسم الفلسفة عن معنى آخر . وأظهر ما يقال في هذا الصدد هو أن الفلسفة : العمليات الفكرية ، والمحاولات العقلية التي يراد بها التوصل إلى الحق والاهتداء إلى الصواب
(1) خذ مثلا قوله : « ليعلم أن المقصود تنبيه من حسن اعتقاده في الفلاسفة ، وظن أن مسالكهم نقية عن التناقض ، ببيان وجوه تهافتهم »
وقوله : « ونحن لم نلتزم فى هذا الكتاب إلا تكذيب مذهبهم ، وأما إثبات المذهب الحق فسنصف فيه كتاباً بعد الفراغ من هذا »
First: The connection of the book Tahafut al-Falasifa to philosophy, and to Islamic philosophy in particular.
Its placement - alongside Ibn Sina's Isharat - within the philosophy curriculum for higher studies indicates this.
For this means - in the view of the curriculum designers at least - that the Tahafut, like the Isharat, is a philosophy book.
But the book Tahafut explicitly states that it is - from its beginning to its end - an exposition of the nonsense and drivel contained in philosophy. Can philosophy judge itself to be nonsense and drivel?
Furthermore, the author of the Tahafut declares in many places that his purpose in writing this book is to demolish philosophy, shake people's confidence in it, and show that it is a futile, unsuccessful enterprise. Can something that proves philosophy to be futile be philosophy?
These are two questions raised by considering the book Tahafut al-Falasifa - which attempts to demolish philosophy - as falling within the sphere of philosophy. Answering them negatively or affirmatively requires first determining the meaning of philosophy in the view of Muslims. In light of this determination, it can be said that the Tahafut is either inside or outside the sphere of philosophy. If its meaning is broad enough to include investigation into philosophy and judgment upon it, even if it is nonsense and drivel, then the Tahafut may be considered a philosophy book. But if its meaning is too narrow to accept such an attempt, then the Tahafut cannot be considered a philosophy book.
So what is Islamic philosophy then?
Is it the collection of opinions adopted by al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and those who followed their approach, concerning God and the human soul, for example, without consideration of the method by which they arrived at these opinions? If that is what is conventionally called Islamic philosophy, then the Tahafut is not part of it, because it is an attempt to demolish these opinions and denounce them. However, in this case, one must question the justification for singling out the opinions of Ibn Sina and al-Farabi - to the exclusion of others - under the name philosophy. For restricting the name philosophy to one group of ideas to the exclusion of another, without taking into account a particular method for deriving ideas, is pure arbitrariness.
And since there is no justification for that, one must search for another meaning for the name philosophy. The most obvious thing that can be said in this regard is that philosophy is: the intellectual processes and rational attempts intended to arrive at truth and to be guided to what is correct.
(1) Take, for example, his statement: 'Let it be known that the purpose is to caution those who have a good opinion of the philosophers and think that their methods are free from contradiction, by exposing the aspects of their incoherence.'
And his statement: 'We have not committed ourselves in this book except to refuting their doctrine. As for establishing the true doctrine, we will describe a book on it after finishing this one.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is an argument about whether the Tahafut should be considered a work of philosophy. The editor begins by noting that the Tahafut's inclusion alongside Ibn Sina's Isharat in the philosophy curriculum suggests it is a philosophy book. However, al-Ghazali's own text states that it exposes the nonsense of philosophy and aims to demolish it. This raises two questions: can philosophy judge itself as nonsense, and can a book that proves philosophy futile be considered philosophy? The editor argues that answering these requires defining philosophy from a Muslim perspective. If philosophy is defined broadly to include any inquiry into it, then the Tahafut qualifies. The footnote provides two quotes from al-Ghazali confirming his intent: to alert those who admire the philosophers and to refute their doctrines, while reserving the positive exposition for another book.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The editor warns against arbitrary labeling based on authority. In your own learning, are you prone to accepting certain scholars' views simply because they are famous, without examining their methods? What steps can you take to avoid this bias?
?
If defining Islamic philosophy requires method, then what does that imply for how you should engage with philosophical texts? Will you shift from asking 'what did they say?' to 'how did they think?'? What might that cost you in terms of effort?

A Broader Definition of Philosophy
The editor proposes a broader definition of philosophy and supports it with al-Ghazali's own statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's proposed definition of philosophy as intellectual processes aimed at truth, and how it includes the Tahafut.

Core Idea
Philosophy is the pursuit of truth, even through criticism.

Having rejected the narrow definition, the editor now proposes a better one: philosophy as the intellectual and rational processes aimed at arriving at truth. He supports this with al-Ghazali's own statements from the Tahafut, which show that al-Ghazali's intent was to expose incoherence and clear the way for truth. This definition naturally includes the Tahafut as a philosophical work.

Guided Path
1
Read the proposed definition
Read where the editor says 'the most obvious thing that can be said is that philosophy is: the intellectual processes and rational attempts intended to arrive at truth and to be guided to what is correct.'
This is the positive definition.
2
Examine the footnote evidence
Read the two quotes from al-Ghazali. They show his purpose: to expose errors of philosophers and to refute wrong doctrines, not to reject rationality itself.
These quotes support the broader definition.
3
Connect to the Tahafut's status
Think: if philosophy is the pursuit of truth through rational inquiry, then the Tahafut's critical engagement with philosophical arguments qualifies as philosophy.
Thus the Tahafut is a philosophical work.

?

?

Defining Islamic Philosophy: Is the Tahafut Philosophical?
The author defines Islamic philosophy and argues for the Tahafut's inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of Islamic philosophy presented in the passage and why Ghazali's Tahafut qualifies as philosophy under the broader definition.

Core Idea
Philosophy is defined by its rational method, not by its conclusions.

This passage from a modern commentary on Ghazali's Tahafut addresses a crucial meta-question: Is a book that aims to demolish philosophy itself a work of philosophy? The answer depends on how we define 'Islamic philosophy'. Let's examine the two definitions presented and see where Ghazali's project fits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
Read the opening lines to spot the core dilemma: can a book that criticizes philosophy be considered philosophy? The author uses this question to frame the entire discussion.
2
Examine the narrow definition
Find where the author presents the conventional view that Islamic philosophy is just the opinions of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. Notice why he rejects this as arbitrary.
The criticism: restricting philosophy to a set of doctrines ignores the method by which those doctrines are reached.
3
Understand the broader definition
Locate the alternative definition: philosophy as any rational attempt to reach truth. See how this definition includes Ghazali's work because he uses rational argumentation, as shown by the excerpt from Ibn Sina.
The longer quotation from Ibn Sina illustrates the rational method both thinkers share.

هو هدم الفلسفة يصرح في مواضع كثيرة (1) بأن غرضه من تأليف كتابه هذا وزعزعة ثقة الناس فيها وبيان أنها عمل فاشل غير ناجح، وهل يكون فلسفة ما يثبت أن الفلسفة عمل فاشل؟ هذان سؤالان يثيرهما اعتبار كتاب «تهافت الفلاسفة» – الذي يحاول هدم الفلسفة – داخلاً ضمن دائرة الفلسفة، والإجابة عليهما بالسلب أو بالإيجاب، تتطلب قبلا تحديد معنى الفلسفة في نظر المسلمين. ففي ضوء هذا التحديد يمكن القول بأن كتاب التهافت داخل ضمن – أو خارج عن – دائرة الفلسفة. فلو اتسع معناها لشمول ما يكون بحثاً فيها وحكماً عليها، ولو بأنها سخف وهذيان صح اعتبار كتاب التهافت كتاب فلسفة، وإن ضاق معناها عن قبول مثل هذه المحاولة، لم يصح اعتبار كتاب التهافت كتاب فلسفة. فما هي الفلسفة الإسلامية إذن؟ أهي مجموعة الآراء التي ارتاها الفارابي وابن سينا ومن نحا نحوهما، في الله والنفس الإنسانية مثلاً، دون نظر إلى الطريق التي تأدوا منها إلى هذه الآراء؟ إن كان ذلك هو ما اصطلح على تسميته بالفلسفة الإسلامية، فكتاب التهافت ليس منها؛ لأنه محاولة لهدم هذه الآراء والتشنيع عليها. إلا أنه – في هذه الحال – التساؤل عن مبررات تخصيص ما لابن سينا والفارابي من آراء دون ما لغيرهما باسم الفلسفة؛ فإن قصر اسم الفلسفة على طائفة الأفكار دون طائفة أخرى – إذا لم يدخل في اعتبار المسمى رعاية طريق خاص لاستمداد الأفكار منه – يكون تحكماً صرفاً. وإذ لا مبرر يدعو لذلك، فلابد من البحث لاسم الفلسفة عن معنى آخر. وأظهر ما يقال في هذا الصدد هو أن الفلسفة: العمليات الفكرية والمحاولات العقلية التي يراد بها التوصل إلى الحق والاهتداء إلى الصواب. وفي دائرة هذا المعنى يقع العمل الذي قام به الغزالي في كتابه التهافت؛ فإنه: إذا كان ابن سينا قد عول على العقل واستلهمه، فكذلك صنع الغزالي. وإذا كان ابن سينا يسلك مسلكاً عقلياً صرفاً يبين به وجوب أن يكون العالم قديماً فيقول: وجود المعلول متعلق بالعلة من حيث هي على الحال التي بها تكون علة: من طبيعة، أو إرادة، أو غير ذلك من أمور يحتاج إلى أن تكون من خارج، ولها مدخل في تتميم كون العلة علة بالفعل. وعدم المعلول متعلق بعدم كون العلة على الحال التي هي بها علة بالفعل سواء كانت ذاتها موجودة لا على تلك الحال، أو لم تكن موجودة أصلاً. فإذا لم يكن شيء معوقاً من خارج، وكان الفاعل بذاته موجوداً، ولكنه ليس لذاته علة، توقف وجود المعلول على وجود الحالة المذكورة، فإذا وجدت كانت طبيعة، أو إرادة جازمة، أو غير ذلك - وجب وجود المعلول؛ وإن لم توجد، وجب عدمه. وأيهما فرض أبداً، كان ما بإزائه أبداً، أو وقتاً ما، كان ما بإزائه وقتاً ما. وإذا جاز أن يكون شيء متشابه الحال في كل شيء، وله معلول، لم يبعد أن يجب عنه سرمداً، فإذا لم يسم هذا مفعولاً – بسبب أنه لم يتقدمه عدم زماني – فلا مضايقة في الأسماء بعد ظهور المعنى (1). ويقول: فإن كان الداعي إلى تعطيل واجب الوجود عن إفاضة الخير والجود، هو كون المعلول مسبوق العدم لا محالة، فهذا الداعي ضعيف قد انكشف لذوي الإنصاف ضعفه (2).
It is the demolition of philosophy. He states in many places (1) that his purpose in composing this book is to shake people's confidence in it and to show that it is a failed, unsuccessful work. And can that which proves that philosophy is a failure be philosophy? These are two questions raised by considering the book 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers' – which attempts to demolish philosophy – as falling within the domain of philosophy. Answering them negatively or affirmatively requires first determining the meaning of philosophy in the view of Muslims. In light of this determination, it can be said that the book of the Incoherence is either inside or outside the domain of philosophy. If its meaning is broad enough to include discussion about it and judgment upon it, even if it is nonsense and raving, then it is correct to consider the Incoherence a book of philosophy. But if its meaning is too narrow to admit such an attempt, then it is not correct to consider it a book of philosophy. So what is Islamic philosophy then? Is it the set of opinions that al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and those who followed their path arrived at, concerning God and the human soul, for example, without regard to the path by which they arrived at these opinions? If that is what is conventionally called Islamic philosophy, then the Incoherence is not part of it, because it is an attempt to demolish these opinions and disparage them. However, in this case, one must question the justifications for singling out the opinions of Ibn Sina and al-Farabi, excluding those of others, with the name 'philosophy.' For restricting the name of philosophy to one set of ideas rather than another – if no consideration is given to a particular method for deriving ideas – is pure arbitrariness. And since there is no justification for that, one must seek another meaning for the name philosophy. The most apparent thing said in this regard is that philosophy is: intellectual operations and rational attempts intended to reach truth and be guided to correctness. And within the scope of this meaning falls the work that al-Ghazali undertook in his book the Incoherence. For if Ibn Sina relied on reason and drew inspiration from it, so too did al-Ghazali. And if Ibn Sina followed a purely rational method to demonstrate the necessity of the world's pre-eternity, saying: The existence of the effect is related to the cause insofar as it is in the state by which it is a cause: whether by nature, will, or other matters that need to be from outside, and they have a role in completing the cause's being an actual cause. And the non-existence of the effect is related to the cause not being in the state by which it is an actual cause, whether its essence exists not in that state, or does not exist at all. If there is no hindrance from outside, and the agent exists in itself, but it is not a cause by itself, the existence of the effect depends on the existence of the mentioned state. So if it exists – whether nature, decisive will, or other – the existence of the effect is necessary; and if it does not exist, its non-existence is necessary. And whichever is assumed forever, what corresponds to it is forever; or for a time, what corresponds to it is for a time. And if it is possible that something is uniform in all respects and has an effect, it is not unlikely that it should necessitate it perpetually. So if one does not call this 'effected' because it was not preceded by temporal non-existence, there is no harm in names after the meaning becomes clear (1). And he says: If the reason for preventing the Necessary of Existence from pouring forth good and generosity is that the effect is necessarily preceded by non-existence, then this reason is weak, and its weakness has become clear to those who are fair (2).
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a meta-discussion about the nature of the book 'Tahafut al-Falasifa' and its relationship to the discipline of philosophy. The author begins by posing a central question: can a book that aims to demolish philosophy itself be considered a work of philosophy? He then argues that answering this requires first defining what 'Islamic philosophy' means. 

The author presents two possible definitions. The first is a narrow, conventional definition: Islamic philosophy is the set of opinions held by al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and their followers regarding God and the soul, regardless of the method by which they arrived at these opinions. Under this definition, the Tahafut is not philosophy because its purpose is to refute and disparage those very opinions. However, the author points out that restricting the name 'philosophy' to one set of ideas without considering method is arbitrary and unjustified. 

Therefore, a second, broader definition is proposed: philosophy is any intellectual operation or rational attempt aimed at reaching truth and correctness. Under this definition, the Tahafut qualifies as philosophy because, like Ibn Sina, al-Ghazali uses rational methods. The author then provides a lengthy quotation from Ibn Sina's argument for the pre-eternity of the world, demonstrating his rational method. The quotation shows Ibn Sina reasoning about the relationship between cause and effect, necessity, and temporality. By including this, the author illustrates that both Ibn Sina and al-Ghazali employ rational inquiry, thus placing the Tahafut within the broader definition of philosophy. The passage concludes with a second quotation from Ibn Sina dismissing a potential objection about the effect being preceded by non-existence. 

In essence, the passage defends the Tahafut as a philosophical work by adopting a definition of philosophy centered on rational methodology rather than a fixed set of doctrines.

Key Terms
تهافت الفلاسفة
الفلسفة الإسلامية
العقل
المعلول
العلة
وجوب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Tahafut cannot be philosophy because it attacks philosophy.
Correction: The passage argues that philosophy is defined by its rational method, not by its conclusions. Since Ghazali employs rational argumentation, the Tahafut is a work of philosophy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the broader definition of philosophy that includes Ghazali's Tahafut?
A) The set of opinions of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina
B) The study of ancient Greek texts
C) Intellectual operations and rational attempts to reach truth
D) The denial of causality

Key Takeaway
The Tahafut is philosophy because it employs rational inquiry to seek truth, not because it affirms or denies specific doctrines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge, do you define it by its content (what is taught) or by its method (how it is sought)? Which approach are you currently using, and could the other lead you to a deeper understanding?
?
The passage shows that al-Ghazali used the same rational method as Ibn Sina but reached opposite conclusions. Are you willing to let your intellect challenge your most comfortable beliefs, even if it leads to conclusions you did not expect?
?
The author calls the narrow definition of philosophy 'pure arbitrariness.' In your own life, are there areas where you arbitrarily limit what counts as valid inquiry? What might you be excluding from your quest for truth?

Ghazali's Method vs Ibn Sina's: A Comparison
Editor compares Ghazali and Ibn Sina's methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's view that Ghazali's method is clearer and more precise than Ibn Sina's, and the challenge to the definition of philosophy.

Core Idea
Editor argues Ghazali's method is clearer and questions if only one approach is philosophy.

This short editorial passage offers a direct comparison between Ghazali and Ibn Sina's methods of rational inquiry. The editor praises Ghazali's thoroughness and suggests his approach may be superior. The passage ends with a provocative question that challenges us to reconsider what counts as philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the editor's tone and key terms.
Pay attention to the words 'يسرف' and 'يمعن'.
2
Identify key descriptive terms
The editor describes Ghazali's method as 'excessive' (يسرف) and 'deep' (يمعن). Consider how these words may carry both praise and critique.
3
Understand the comparison
The editor explicitly states that Ghazali's method is 'أوضح وأدق' (clearer and more precise) than Ibn Sina's. This is a direct comparative claim.
Note that the editor says 'عساه لا يبعد عن الحق' (perhaps he is not far from the truth).
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage ends by asking if there is any justification for calling one approach philosophy but not the other. Consider what this implies about the editor's view of philosophy.
This question challenges the reader to think about the boundaries of philosophical inquiry.

هكذا يسرف الغزالى فى البحث العقلى حول هذه المسألة إسرافاً ، ويمعن فيه إمعاناً . وعساه لا يبعد عن الحق من يقول : إن منهج الغزالي في بحث هذه المسألة أوضح وأدق من بحث ابن سينا . فهل يوجد – إذن ما يبرر اعتبار أحد المسلكين فلسفة دون الآخر؟
Thus Ghazali is excessive in rational inquiry on this issue, excessively, and delves deep into it. And perhaps he is not far from the truth who says: The method of Ghazali in investigating this issue is clearer and more precise than the investigation of Ibn Sina. So is there then anything that justifies considering one of the two approaches as philosophy without the other?
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is an editorial commentary comparing Ghazali and Ibn Sina. The editor begins by remarking on Ghazali's thoroughness in rational inquiry, describing it as excessive (يسرف) and deep (يمعن). Then he suggests that it may be correct to say that Ghazali's method on this particular issue is clearer and more precise than Ibn Sina's. The editor ends with a rhetorical question: is there any justification for considering one of these two approaches (Ghazali's or Ibn Sina's) as philosophy to the exclusion of the other? This question challenges the assumption that only one method can be truly philosophical.

Correction: The editor suggests that despite being excessive, Ghazali's method may be clearer and more precise, and questions whether such a difference excludes it from being philosophy.

B) Ghazali's method is clearer and more precise.

The editor argues that Ghazali's method is clearer and more precise, and challenges the notion that only one approach can be called philosophy.

?

?
The editor challenges whether one approach can be called philosophy and not the other. What cherished assumption about knowledge do you hold that this question forces you to defend or abandon? Are you ready to do so?

Skepticism as Constructive Criticism
Editor defends skepticism as constructive criticism using analogy and historical example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why skepticism and criticism are considered constructive philosophical tools, using Ghazali's Tahafut as a case study.

Core Idea
Skepticism is not destruction—it clears the ground for truth.

This passage defends Ghazali's critical method in the Tahafut. The editor argues that skepticism and criticism are not merely negative; they serve a constructive role by pointing out errors and opening the path to better theories. You'll see how this perspective reframes Ghazali's work as a positive contribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the tone: defensive yet persuasive.
2
Identify the main argument
The editor claims skepticism and criticism are constructive. Find the sentence that states this directly.
Look for key phrase: 'أعمال تساعد على بناء وتشييد'.
3
Analyze the analogy of the path
The editor uses an analogy of a person believing a certain path leads to truth. How does showing faults help?
This illustrates the constructive function of criticism.
4
Examine Aristotle's critique of Plato
Aristotle's falsification of Plato's theory of Forms is presented as a positive step. Why?
His criticism paved the way for his own theory.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this defend Ghazali's method in the Tahafut?
The editor suggests Ghazali's skepticism is similarly constructive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم إن مسلك الغزالى فى كتاب التهافت قائم فى معظمه على التشكيك والنقد ، ولكن التشكيك عمل علمى له قيمته ؛ فإن بعض الفلاسفة يذهب إلى القول بأن : 1 وظيفة الفلسفة لا تقوم فى وضع حلول للمشاكل ، بل تقوم في تفنيد الحلول الموضوعة للمشاكل (١)] ومن عدم الإنصاف أن يعتبر التشكيك والنقد أعمالا سلبية عديمة الفائدة ، إنهما - فما أعتقد - أعمال تساعد على بناء وتشييد من نوع آخر .. فلو أن شخصاً اعتقد أن طريقاً معينة يمكن أن توصل إلى الحق ، فكشف له إنسان عن نقص في هذه الطريق وأظهر له مثالبها وعيوبها ، فإن ذلك الإنسان يكون قد صرف ذلك الشخص عن باطل ، ونبهه إلى ضرورة البحث عن طريق أخرى عساها تكون أليق بالغرض المطلوب إن أرسطو حينما زيف نظرية المثل الأفلاطونية ، لم يكن عمله هذا ـ وهو عملاً سلبيا ؛ إن عمله هذا كان خطوة تمهيدية فلسفة = هدم لشيء يسمى لا بد منها ، للوصول إلى نظريته الجديدة التي ملأ بها فراغ النظرية التى استبعدها . إذ لو لم ينقد نظرية المثل الأفلاطونية - بل آمن بها – لم يكن هناك سبيل لكشف نظرية أخرى تحل محلها (1) أسس الفلسفة للدكتور توفيق الطويل ص ١٢٦ الطبعة الثانية
Yes, indeed the method of Ghazali in the book Tahafut is based mostly on skepticism and criticism, but skepticism is a scientific work that has its value; for some philosophers go to say that the function of philosophy does not consist in providing solutions to problems, but rather consists in refuting the solutions offered to problems. And it is unjust to consider skepticism and criticism as negative, useless actions; they are — as I believe — actions that help in building and constructing of another kind. For if a person believed that a certain path can lead to truth, and another person uncovered to him a deficiency in this path and showed him its faults and defects, then that person would have turned that individual away from falsehood and alerted him to the necessity of searching for another path that might be more suitable for the desired goal. Aristotle, when he falsified Plato's theory of Forms, his work was not — and it was not negative; his work was a preparatory step — a necessary demolition of something called philosophy, to reach his new theory with which he filled the void of the theory he excluded. For if he had not criticized Plato's theory of Forms — rather believed in it — there would be no way to discover another theory to replace it. (1) Foundations of Philosophy by Dr. Tawfiq al-Tawil, p. 126, second edition.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a defense of skepticism and criticism as constructive philosophical activities, using Ghazali's method in the Tahafut as an example. The editor begins by affirming that Ghazali's approach is based on skepticism and criticism, but argues that this is scientifically valuable. He cites the view of some philosophers that philosophy's role is not to solve problems but to refute proposed solutions. The editor asserts that it is unjust to label such work as merely negative; rather, it helps build something new. He illustrates with an analogy: if someone believes a certain path leads to truth, and another shows its faults, the latter has redirected the seeker away from falsehood and toward a better path. The editor then gives Aristotle's critique of Plato's theory of Forms as a historical example: Aristotle's criticism was not negative but a necessary demolition that paved the way for his own theory. Without criticizing Plato, Aristotle could not have developed an alternative. The passage ends with a footnote citation. The argument is that criticism is a fundamental part of philosophical progress, and thus Ghazali's work should be seen as constructive.

Key Terms
تشكيك
نقد
فلسفة
تفنيد
بناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Skepticism and criticism are purely negative and destroy knowledge.
Correction: The passage argues that criticism clears away falsehood and paves the way for better theories, as shown by Aristotle's refutation of Plato.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one function of philosophy according to some philosophers?
A) To provide definitive solutions to problems.
B) To refute solutions offered to problems.
C) To avoid criticism altogether.
D) To accept all theories as equally valid.

Key Takeaway
The passage shows that skepticism and criticism are essential for philosophical progress by clearing away falsehoods and opening the path to better theories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a deeply held belief you have about your faith, yourself, or others. When was the last time you allowed yourself to honestly examine its weaknesses? If you haven't, what fears stop you? Commit today to writing one question that challenges that belief and sit with it without fleeing.
?
The editor argues that Aristotle's criticism of Plato was not negative but paved the way for a better theory. Is there a criticism you have received or seen that you dismissed as destructive? Could it have been clearing ground for a deeper truth? Resolve to re-read that criticism with an open heart and see what it might be pointing you toward.
?
You are reading this lesson to learn the method of seeking truth. But have you ever actually preferred comfort over truth—assuming that because something is familiar, it must be correct? Where in your life are you clinging to a 'path' because it's easy, even if it might be false? Choose one area today and actively seek out a critique of your position.
