The Tahafut as a Philosophical Work
The author argues that Ghazali's Tahafut is philosophical in method despite its anti-philosophical aim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali's Tahafut is considered philosophical in subject despite its anti-philosophical goal, and how the use of reason to critique reason creates a philosophical paradox.

Core Idea
Criticizing philosophy with reason makes the critique itself philosophical.

This passage presents a key argument about the nature of Ghazali's Tahafut. The author contends that although Ghazali aims to undermine reason's ability to grasp divine truths, his method is thoroughly rational. This creates a fascinating philosophical paradox: using reason to prove reason's limits. As we read, pay attention to how the author supports this claim with historical and contemporary examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the argument. Notice the key terms like 'التهافت', 'العقل', 'الفلسفة'.
The passage begins with a conditional statement.
2
Understand the central paradox
Identify Ghazali's goal and his method. The goal is to show reason's inadequacy, but the method relies on reason. This is the core paradox.
3
Examine the supporting quotes
Look at the quotes from Aristotle and the modern philosopher. How do they reinforce the idea that denying philosophy is itself a philosophical act?
The quote 'He who denies metaphysics philosophizes metaphysically' is key.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The author concludes that the Tahafut is philosophical in subject if not in goal. Understand why 'subject' refers to the method and content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان النقد داخلا هكذا ، في نطاق الفلسفة ، فكتاب التهافت - إذن – فلسفة
نعم إن هدف كتاب التهافت هو إظهار العقل بمظهر العاجز عن اقتناص الحقائق الإلهية ، ولهذا ، يحاول الغزالي فيه أن ينتزع ثقة الناس من العقل كمصدر تتعرف منه المسائل الإلهية ، ولكن الغزالى إذ يحاول ، تقييد سلطة العقل ، يتخذ من العقل نفسه مطية للوصول إلى هذه الغاية . فإذن عمله هذا هو محاولة عقلية لإثبات قصور العقل في ميدان الإلهيات ، وشهادة عقلية بأن للعقل حدا يجب
الوقوف عنده .
فمن نظر إلى الغاية من كتاب التهافت - تلك التى يصورها الغزالى نفسه ، بأنها انتزاع الثقة من الفلسفة - و رآه و رآه ، لهذا ، بعيداً من نطاق الفلسفة ، فهو مضطر إلى اعتبار وسيلته ، واستخدام العقل وحده للكشف عن قصورها وعجزها ، وضعفها وأدلتهم و ركتها - عملا داخلا في الفلسفة . إنه عمل يمكن تصويره بأنه بحث في صميم طاقة العقل ، وهل يمكن أن يكون عمل كهذا بعيداً عن مجال الفلسفة ؟ ولقد قال أرسطو قديماً :
[ إن من ينكر الميتافيزيقا ، يتفلسف ميتافيزيقيا ]
وقال :
[ فلنتفلسف إذا اقتضى الأمر أن نتفلسف ، فإذا لم يقتض الأمر التفلسف وجب أن نتفلسف لنثبت أن التفلسف لا ضرورة له].
ولقد قال حديثاً بعض الفلاسفة الميتافيزيقيين عن خصومهم من الوضعيين :
إنهم الفلاسفة الذين يفاخرون بأنهم ليسوا بفلاسفة ، إن موقفهم من إنكار الفلسفة موقف فلسفى لا محالة ]
وعلى هذا القياس يكون الغزالى قد تفلسف وهو يهدم الفلسفة ، فالتهافت - إذن - إن لم يكن فلسفى الغاية فهو فلسفى الموضوع .
And if criticism enters thus into the scope of philosophy, then the book Tahafut is therefore philosophy. Yes, the goal of the book Tahafut is to show reason in the guise of incapable of seizing divine truths, and for this reason, Ghazali attempts in it to wrest people's trust from reason as a source through which divine matters are known. But Ghazali, while attempting to restrict the authority of reason, takes reason itself as a mount to reach this end. So his work is a rational attempt to prove the inadequacy of reason in the field of theology, and a rational testimony that reason has a limit at which one must stop. So whoever looks at the goal of the book Tahafut—that which Ghazali himself depicts, as the wresting of trust from philosophy—and sees it, for this reason, far from the scope of philosophy, he is forced to consider its means, and the use of reason alone to uncover its deficiency, weakness, and the feebleness of their proofs—a work internal to philosophy. It is a work that can be depicted as an investigation into the very capacity of reason. And can such a work be far from the field of philosophy? Aristotle said long ago: 'He who denies metaphysics philosophizes metaphysically.' And he said: 'Let us philosophize if the matter necessitates that we philosophize; and if the matter does not necessitate philosophizing, we must philosophize to prove that philosophizing is not necessary.' And recently some metaphysical philosophers said about their opponents from the positivists: 'They are the philosophers who boast that they are not philosophers; their stance on the denial of philosophy is inevitably a philosophical stance.' By this analogy, Ghazali philosophized while demolishing philosophy. So the Tahafut, therefore, if it is not philosophical in goal, it is philosophical in subject.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the philosophical nature of Ghazali's Tahafut. The author begins by stating a conditional: if criticism (of philosophy) falls within the scope of philosophy, then the Tahafut is philosophy. He then explains that the Tahafut's goal is to show reason's inability to grasp divine truths, but the means used is reason itself. Thus, Ghazali's work is a rational attempt to prove reason's inadequacy in theology. The author then warns that if one focuses only on the anti-philosophical goal, one may misjudge the work as non-philosophical; but the method (using reason) places it squarely within philosophy. He supports this with two quotes: Aristotle's statement that denying metaphysics is metaphysical, and a modern philosopher's remark that positivists who deny philosophy are actually philosophizing. The conclusion is that Ghazali, by using reason to dismantle reason's authority, philosophized while destroying philosophy; hence the Tahafut is philosophical in subject if not in goal.

Key Terms
التهافت
العقل
الفلسفة
الميتافيزيقا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali's Tahafut is not philosophical because it attacks philosophy.
Correction: The passage argues that despite its anti-philosophical goal, the Tahafut is philosophical in subject because it uses rational arguments and reason itself as its method.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the Tahafut considered philosophical in subject?
A) Because Ghazali praises philosophy throughout the book.
B) Because Ghazali uses rational arguments and reason itself as his method.
C) Because the book is about metaphysics only.
D) Because Ghazali agrees with Aristotle on all points.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's use of rational arguments to critique reason makes the Tahafut a philosophical work in subject, even if its goal is anti-philosophical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you used your own intellect to argue that your intellect is insufficient to understand God? Does that contradiction unsettle you? What does that reveal about your posture before revelation?
?
Ghazali used reason to prove reason's limits. Are you willing to submit your reason to divine truth, even if your reason protests? What is one area where you resist because your reason says otherwise?
?
If the act of denying philosophy is itself philosophical, then your every intellectual stance commits you to something. What does your critique of others' positions reveal about your own philosophical commitments? Are you consistent?

Ghazali's Integrity Under Question
Narrative on Ghazali's honesty and a student's doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of intellectual honesty in scholarly debate, illustrated by the challenge to Ghazali's fairness.

Core Idea
True scholarship requires fair representation of opposing views.

This lesson explores a pivotal moment in the study of Ghazali's Tahafut: a student's doubt about Ghazali's fairness. The author recounts his own reaction to this doubt, revealing the tension between idealizing scholars and facing real accusations. As you read, consider how we balance trust in a scholar with critical scrutiny.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the author's narrative and emotional tone.
2
Identify the student's accusation
Find the student's question: does Ghazali distort opponents' views to refute them easily? Understand the core doubt.
The accusation is about potential dishonesty in scholarly presentation.
3
Notice the author's emotional reaction
Observe the author's pain and sadness. Why does he feel this way? What does it reveal about his expectations of scholars?
The author's innocent nature is troubled by the idea that scholars might act like the wicked.
4
Consider the professor's reply
The professor mentions that Orientalists raised the same issue. How does this affect the author and the questioning student?
The reply confirms the doubt and makes the author question his idealism.
5
Reflect on the role of authority
The author notes the veneration of Orientalism. How does authority shape our trust in arguments?
This lesson prompts self-reflection on how we evaluate scholarly integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : احتياط الغزالى وأمانته فى تصوير أفكار خصومه التي يعرضها توطئة للرد عليها . وأقول بصدده : إنى ما زلت أذكر حتى الآن سؤالاً تقدم به زميل لى ونحن طلاب – إلى أستاذ الفلسفة الذي كان يدرس لنا في ذلكم الوقت نصوصاً من كتاب التهافت ) يقول فيه : أليس يجوز أن يدلس الغزالى على خصومه ، فيعرض أفكارهم في صورة ركيكة مشوهة ، حتى يتأتى له ردها وإبطالها والتشنيع عليها ؟ ثم كيف يسوغ أن نثق بالغزالي في تصوير آراء الفلاسفة ؟ أنه على خلاف شديد معهم ؟ لقد آلمني - آنذاك هذا السؤال ، وأدخل على نفسى شيئاً من الكآبة والحزن ، لم أتبين على وجه التحديد - يومئذ - سببهما . وأغلب الظن أن طبيعتي الشابة البريئة - وقتئذ طبعاً - لم تستسغ أن يقال عن العلماء ـ وهمهم الحق والخير والجمال - إنهم في مسالكهم العلمية ، يفعلون ما يفعل الأشرار الذين يختصمون على العرض الفاني ، ويلفقون الأقوال ويزورون الشهود ، ليكسبوا خصومهم ولو بطريق الباطل ؛ إلى غير ذلك من هذيان يترفع عنه شرف العلم ، ويأباه كمال العلماء ويتعارض مع مهمتهم التي انتديهم الحق لها . إلا أن ثورتي الداخلية هذه – التي أبقيتها سرا بيني وبين نفسي لم تمح معالم السؤال من ذاكرتى ، ولم تعف على آثاره ، خاصة وقد أجاب الأستاذ المدرس صاحب السؤال بأن بعض المستشرقين قد أثار نفس المشكلة ، ورمى الغزالى بنفس التهمة . ولقد كان في الإجابة على هذه الصورة ، ما جعل الزميل - صاحب السؤال ـ يدل بسؤاله ويكبر من وجعلني – في الوقت ذاته - أتضاءل أمام نفسى وأفهم أن ما أضفيته على الحقيقة من سمو ، وعلى العلماء من كمال ، إنما هو من صنع الخيال ، ليس له من واقع الأمر نصيب لقد كان لاسم ( الاستشراق ) ، في النفوس من الإكبار والتقديس ما يجعل من العسير على المرء أن يخالف مستشرقاً فى رأى يراه ، أو يراجعه فيه .
Secondly: Ghazali's caution and honesty in portraying his opponents' ideas, which he presents as a prelude to refuting them. And I say concerning this: I still recall until now a question posed by a colleague of mine while we were students—to the professor of philosophy who was teaching us at that time texts from the book Tahafut—in which he said: 'Is it not permissible that Ghazali deceived his opponents, presenting their ideas in a weak, distorted form, so that he could refute, invalidate, and denounce them? Then how is it justified to trust Ghazali in portraying the views of the philosophers, given his severe disagreement with them?' That question pained me at that time and brought a kind of gloom and sadness into my soul, whose cause I did not precisely understand then. Most likely my young, innocent nature—at that time, of course—could not stomach that it be said about scholars—whose concern is truth, goodness, and beauty—that in their scholarly paths they do what the wicked do, who quarrel over perishable reputation, fabricate statements, and falsify witnesses to defeat their opponents even by false means; and other such drivel from which the honor of science is exalted, and the perfection of scholars refuses, and which contradicts the mission for which truth has appointed them. However, this internal rebellion of mine—which I kept secret between myself and me—did not erase the features of the question from my memory, nor did it efface its effects, especially since the professor who taught the answerer of the question responded that some Orientalists had raised the same problem and accused Ghazali of the same charge. And this form of answer made the colleague—the questioner—swell with his question and grow big, and made me—at the same time—diminish before myself and understand that what I had attributed to truth of sublimity and to scholars of perfection was only a product of imagination, having no share in reality. The name 'Orientalism' had such veneration and sanctification in souls that it made it difficult for one to contradict an Orientalist in an opinion he held, or to argue with him about it.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a narrative reflection on Ghazali's integrity. The author begins by stating the topic: Ghazali's caution and honesty in portraying his opponents' views before refuting them. He then recalls a specific incident from his student days: a classmate asked a professor whether Ghazali might have deliberately weakened the arguments of the philosophers to make refutation easier, and then questioned how we can trust Ghazali given his hostility toward them. The author describes his own emotional reaction—pain and sadness—which he could not fully explain at the time. He attributes this to his youthful idealism, which could not accept that scholars would use dishonest tactics like fabricating evidence. However, his internal doubt remained, especially when the professor replied that some Orientalists had already raised the same accusation. This reply reinforced the questioner's confidence and made the author feel that his idealistic view of truth and scholars was unrealistic. The author concludes by noting the intimidating authority that the label 'Orientalist' carried, making it hard to challenge their opinions.

Key Terms
احتياط
أمانته
التدليس
المستشرقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that Ghazali misrepresented his opponents deliberately to win the argument.
Correction: The author shows that such an assumption is troubling and not necessarily true; the student's question is a serious challenge that demands careful consideration, not a settled fact.

Checkpoint
What was the main concern of the student's question about Ghazali?
A) Whether Ghazali was a true philosopher.
B) Whether Ghazali intentionally weakened his opponents' arguments to refute them easily.
C) Whether Ghazali's book is too difficult to understand.
D) Whether Ghazali agreed with Aristotle.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual honesty requires presenting opposing views fairly, even when one disagrees strongly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author felt pain and sadness when his idealistic view of scholars was challenged. When have you experienced a similar discomfort when a trusted figure or idea was questioned? How did you respond, and what does this passage teach you about handling such moments?
?
The professor’s answer invoking Orientalists made the author feel small and doubt his own ideals. Have you ever let an expert’s opinion silence your inner doubts? After reading this passage, what is one step you can take to ensure you critically evaluate even respected sources?
?
The student’s question implies that strong disagreement might lead to dishonesty. Is there an area in your life where you have allowed disagreement to cloud your judgment of someone's honesty? How can the author's honest reflection inspire you to seek truth over personal bias?

Ghazali's Honesty: A Defense Against the Charge of Distortion
Editor defends Ghazali against charge of distorting opponents' arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's argument defending Ghazali's honesty in portraying his opponents' arguments, and the impact of orientalist accusations on scholarly perception.

Core Idea
Ghazali's fairness in presenting opponents' ideas is defended against orientalist accusations.

This passage is a methodological reflection by the editor of Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa. He recounts a personal experience that shaped his defense of Ghazali against claims of distorting philosophers' arguments. The editor argues that accusations of dishonesty are unfounded and stem from a misunderstanding of Ghazali's scholarly methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the editor's personal narrative come through before interpreting.
Notice the emotional tone—the editor is relating a formative experience.
2
Identify the student's accusation
The student questions whether Ghazali might have distorted the philosophers' arguments. Find the exact wording and consider its implications.
The accusation is that Ghazali presented ideas in a 'weak, distorted form' to make refutation easier.
3
Understand the editor's reaction
The editor felt pained and saddened by the question. Reflect on why he reacted so strongly—his idealism about scholars and truth.
He later realizes his idealism was naive.
4
Note the role of orientalism
The professor mentions that orientalists raised the same accusation. This adds weight to the student's question and creates a crisis for the editor.
The prestige of orientalism made it hard to challenge their views.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this passage sets up the editor's investigation. It shows that Ghazali's integrity needs defending, but the editor is committed to doing so.
This is a personal testimony, not an argument from evidence.

ثانياً : احتياط الغزالي وأمانته في تصوير أفكار خصومه التي يعرضها توطئة للرد عليها . وأقول بصدده : إني ما زلت أذكر حتى الآن سؤالاً تقدم به زميل لي ونحن طلاب – إلى أستاذ الفلسفة الذي كان يدرس لنا في ذلكم الوقت نصوصاً من كتاب التهافت ) يقول فيه : أليس يجوز أن يدلس الغزالي على خصومه ، فيعرض أفكارهم في صورة ركيكة مشوهة ، حتى يتأتى له ردها وإبطالها والتشنيع عليها ؟ ثم كيف يسوغ أن نثق بالغزالي في تصوير آراء الفلاسفة ؟ أنه على خلاف شديد معهم ؟ لقد آلمني - آنذاك هذا السؤال ، وأدخل على نفسي شيئاً من الكآبة والحزن ، لم أتبين على وجه التحديد - يومئذ - سببهما . وأغلب الظن أن طبيعتي الشابة البريئة - وقتئذ طبعاً - لم تستسغ أن يقال عن العلماء ـ وهمهم الحق والخير والجمال - إنهم في مسالكهم العلمية ، يفعلون ما يفعل الأشرار الذين يختصمون على العرض الفاني ، ويلفقون الأقوال ويزورون الشهود ، ليكسبوا خصومهم ولو بطريق الباطل ؛ إلى غير ذلك من هذيان يترفع عنه شرف العلم ، ويأباه كمال العلماء ويتعارض مع مهمتهم التي انتديهم الحق لها . إلا أن ثورتي الداخلية هذه – التي أبقيتها سرا بيني وبين نفسي لم تمح معالم السؤال من ذاكرتي ، ولم تعف على آثاره ، خاصة وقد أجاب الأستاذ المدرس صاحب السؤال بأن بعض المستشرقين قد أثار نفس المشكلة ، ورمى الغزالي بنفس التهمة . ولقد كان في الإجابة على هذه الصورة ، ما جعل الزميل - صاحب السؤال ـ يدل بسؤاله ويكبر من وجعلني – في الوقت ذاته - أتضاءل أمام نفسي وأفهم أن ما أضفيته على الحقيقة من سمو ، وعلى العلماء من كمال ، إنما هو من صنع الخيال ، ليس له من واقع الأمر نصيب لقد كان لاسم ( الاستشراق ) ، في النفوس من الإكبار والتقديس ما يجعل من العسير على المرء أن يخالف مستشرقاً في رأي يراه ، أو يراجعه فيه . شأنه
Secondly: The precaution of al-Ghazali and his honesty in depicting the ideas of his opponents which he presents as a prelude to refuting them. And I say concerning this: I still remember until now a question put forward by a colleague of mine, when we were students – to the professor of philosophy who was teaching us at that time texts from the book al-Tahafut – he said: Is it not permissible that al-Ghazali might deceive his opponents, presenting their ideas in a weak, distorted form, so that he can refute them, invalidate them, and disparage them? Then how can we trust al-Ghazali in depicting the opinions of the philosophers, given that he is in severe opposition to them? This question pained me at that time, and brought upon me some sadness and grief, the cause of which I could not precisely identify at that time. And most likely my young, innocent nature – at that time, of course – could not accept that it be said of scholars – whose concern is truth, goodness, and beauty – that in their scholarly paths they do what the wicked do who dispute over ephemeral worldly goods, fabricating statements and falsifying witnesses to win over their opponents even by false means; and such nonsense which the honor of science disdains, and the perfection of scholars rejects, and which contradicts the mission for which truth has appointed them. However, this internal uprising of mine – which I kept secret between myself and myself – did not erase the features of the question from my memory, nor did it obliterate its effects, especially since the professor-teacher answered the questioner by saying that some orientalists had raised the same problem and accused al-Ghazali of the same charge. And in the answer in this form, what made the colleague – the questioner – persist in his question and magnify himself, and made me – at the same time – diminish myself and realize that what I had attributed to truth of sublimity and to scholars of perfection was nothing but a product of imagination, having no share in reality. Indeed, the name 'orientalism' had such veneration and sanctification in souls that it became difficult for one to disagree with an orientalist in an opinion he holds, or to argue with him about it. That is his affair.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a methodological reflection. It begins by stating the topic: Ghazali's precaution and honesty in portraying his opponents' ideas as a prelude to refutation. The author then recounts a personal memory: a student colleague asked their philosophy professor whether Ghazali might have distorted the philosophers' arguments to make them easier to refute. The author felt distressed by the question, as his youthful idealism could not accept that scholars would act deceitfully. He notes that the professor's answer mentioned that some orientalists had raised the same accusation against Ghazali. The author's inner turmoil persisted, and he realized that his idealization of truth and scholars was naive. He concludes that the prestige of orientalism made it difficult to challenge their views. The passage sets up the author's later investigation into Ghazali's fairness.

Key Terms
الغزالي
التهافت
المستشرقين
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali deliberately distorted his opponents' arguments to win the debate.
Correction: The editor argues that such accusations are unfounded and stem from misunderstanding Ghazali's methodology. He defends Ghazali's honesty and precaution.

Checkpoint
What was the student's implied accusation against al-Ghazali?
A) He fabricated arguments.
B) He distorted the philosophers' ideas.
C) He was ignorant of philosophy.
D) He was a follower of the philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The editor defends Ghazali's integrity by recounting his own journey from naive idealism to critical understanding, showing that the accusation of distortion is a serious one that must be addressed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that the editor felt pain when his idealistic view of scholars was challenged. Is there a scholar or teacher you have placed on a pedestal, refusing to consider their fallibility? What might you lose – or gain – by honestly examining that now?
?
The editor kept his inner turmoil secret. When you encounter a doubt that shakes your trust in an authority or a belief, do you hide it or face it? What step will you take this week to confront one such doubt instead of suppressing it?
?
The passage shows how the name 'orientalism' carried such weight that it silenced disagreement. What names or labels carry that power over you today? Will you dare to challenge one of them, even if it means feeling small at first?

Ghazali's Presentation of the Philosophers' Proof: Clarification or Distortion?
The author compares Ghazali and Ibn Sina's presentations of the world's eternity proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali's presentation of the philosophers' proof of the world's eternity was a clarification, not a distortion, according to the author's comparative analysis.

Core Idea
Ghazali sharpened the philosophers' arguments, making them clearer than the originals.

This lesson takes you inside the author's own experience teaching both Ibn Sina's and Ghazali's works. He challenges the common suspicion that Ghazali twisted the philosophers' ideas and offers a direct comparison to prove the opposite. You will see a sample of Ibn Sina's original text and learn why the author finds Ghazali's version superior.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's academic journey
The author describes how he taught both al-Isharat and al-Tahafut at different levels. Notice his intention to let students encounter the original philosophical ideas before Ghazali's critique.
This establishes the author's credibility and his method of comparison.
2
Identify the key claim about Ghazali
The author explicitly states that Ghazali did not distort the philosophers' arguments; rather, he added clarity and purity. Find that statement and understand its significance.
This is the central thesis of the passage.
3
Examine the example given
The author challenges you to compare Ibn Sina's and Ghazali's presentations of the proof of the impossibility of the world's temporal origination. Read the quoted portion from Ibn Sina's al-Isharat carefully.
The quote outlines the Necessary Existent's attributes and the conditions for a renewed will.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this example supports the author's claim. What features of Ghazali's style might make the proof clearer? The commentary will help.
Think about the pedagogical value of Ghazali's reformulation.

لقد مرت الأيام سراعاً ، ورأيتني باحثاً عن الغزالي وكاتباً عنه ، ثم ناشراً لكتاب التهافت ومعلقاً عليه ، ثم مبعوثاً بإنجلترا ومتعرفاً إلى المستشرقين عن قرب وقارئاً لهم ، ثم مدرساً ( كتاب التهافت ) لطلاب نفس المرحلة التي كنت بها يوم أن تقدم الزميل الآنف الذكر بسؤاله ، ومدرساً .. في الوقت ذاته ـ «كتاب الإشارات » لابن سينا في المرحلة التي دونها مباشرة . ولقد تبين لي أنه قد أريد بوضع كتاب الإشارات في مرحلة دون المرحلة التي يدرس فيها كتاب التهافت إتاحة الفرصة لمعرفة الفكرة الفلسفية – في مصدرها الأصيل - قبل التعرض لرأي الغزالي فيها ، يمكن التأكد من أن خصومة الغزالي للفلاسفة حملته – أو الم تحمله – على تشويه أفكارهم : ولتحقيق هذه الغاية كان لا بد لي أن أقرأ من « كتاب التهافت » مع الطلاب ، نفس المسألة أو المسائل التي سبق أن قرأتها معهم في كتاب الإشارات . وفي هذه الحدود الضيقة استطعت أن أتبين أن الغزالي – في سلوكه مع الفلاسفة - لم ينحرف بآرائهم عن وضعها الصحيح . بل إنه – على العكس هذا قد أضفى عليها إشراقاً وصفاء لا يجدهما المرء عند ابن سينا . خذ مثلاً دليل الفلاسفة على استحالة حدوث العالم . اقرأ هذا الدليل كما يعرضه ابن سينا ، ثم اقرأه كما يعرضه الغزالي ، وتبين – بعد ذلك ـ أي العرضين أوضح وأنضر ، وأقوى وأرصن . اقرأ ابن سينا في الإشارات حيث يقول : إن واجب الوجود بذاته واجب الوجود في جميع صفاته وأحواله الأولية له . وأنه لم يتميز في العدم الصريح حال الأولى به فيها أن لا يوجد شيئاً ، أو بالأشياء أن لا توجد عنه أصلا ؛ وحال بخلافها ولا يجوز أن تسنح إرادة متجددة إلا لداع ، ولا أن تسنح جزافاً . وكذلك لا يجوز أن تسنح طبيعة ، أو غير ذلك ، بلا تجدد حال وكيف تسنح إرادة : لحال تجددت ، وحال ما يتجدد كحال ما يمهد له التجدد فيتجدد
Days passed swiftly, and I found myself a researcher about al-Ghazali and a writer about him, then a publisher of the book al-Tahafut and a commentator upon it, then a delegate to England and acquainted with the orientalists up close and a reader of them, then a teacher of the book al-Tahafut to students at the same stage in which I was when the aforementioned colleague posed his question, and at the same time a teacher of the book al-Isharat of Ibn Sina at the stage immediately below it. And it became clear to me that the intention behind placing the book al-Isharat at a stage lower than that in which al-Tahafut is studied was to provide the opportunity to know the philosophical idea – in its original source – before encountering al-Ghazali's opinion on it, so that it could be ascertained whether al-Ghazali's opposition to the philosophers led him – or did not lead him – to distort their ideas. To achieve this goal, I had to read with the students from the book al-Tahafut the same issue or issues that I had previously read with them in the book al-Isharat. Within these narrow limits, I was able to ascertain that al-Ghazali – in his conduct with the philosophers – did not deviate from their opinions from their correct position. Rather, on the contrary, he added to them clarity and purity that one does not find with Ibn Sina. Take, for example, the philosophers' proof of the impossibility of the world's temporal origination. Read this proof as Ibn Sina presents it, then read it as al-Ghazali presents it, and then determine which of the two presentations is clearer, fresher, stronger, and more solid. Read Ibn Sina in al-Isharat where he says: 'Indeed, the Necessary Existence by essence is necessary existence in all His attributes and primary states. And that He did not become distinct in pure non-existence as a state preferable to Him in which He would not produce anything, or that things would not originate from Him at all; and a state contrary to it. And it is not permissible that a renewed will should occur except due to a motive, nor that it should occur aimlessly. And likewise it is not permissible that a nature or something else should occur without a renewed state. And how can a will occur due to a state that has renewed, and what is renewed is like a state that paves the way for renewal, so it becomes renewed.'
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage continues the author's methodological narrative. He recounts his own academic journey: researching and publishing about Ghazali, then teaching both al-Tahafut and Ibn Sina's al-Isharat. The author explains that by teaching al-Isharat at a lower level than al-Tahafut, students could first learn the original philosophical ideas before seeing Ghazali's critique. This allowed the author to verify if Ghazali distorted the philosophers' arguments. He concludes that Ghazali did not distort; rather, he improved the clarity of the arguments. As an example, the author presents the philosophers' proof of the impossibility of the world's temporal origination (i.e., its eternality). He challenges the reader to compare Ibn Sina's original presentation with Ghazali's. He then quotes Ibn Sina's text from al-Isharat: the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud) is necessary in all attributes; He does not have a preferred state in pure non-existence; a renewed will (irada mutajaddida) must have a motive; nature cannot change without a renewed state. The quote is incomplete but illustrates the style.

Misconception: Ghazali distorted the philosophers' arguments to make them easier to refute.
Correction: According to the author, Ghazali actually presented their arguments with greater clarity and purity, improving upon the original formulations.

Checkpoint

A) It is weaker.
B) It is clearer and stronger.
C) It is identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged or dismissed someone's argument without first hearing it from them directly, in their own words? What might you have missed? Will you commit to seeking first-hand understanding before forming critiques?
?
This lesson shows that even a fierce critic like al-Ghazali can refine an opposing argument rather than distort it. Is there an opposing view in your life that, if you engaged honestly, might actually sharpen your own beliefs rather than threaten them? What would it take to approach it with that same intellectual courage?
?
If you were to apply the author's method—studying the original source before its critique—how would that change the way you approach disagreements with friends, family, or those of other faiths? What is one step you can take this week to understand an opposing view from its source?

The Editor's Testimony: Ghazali's Fairness to the Philosophers
Editor recounts his teaching and concludes Ghazali was fair.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the editor's teaching experience demonstrates that Ghazali did not distort the philosophers' views but instead brought clarity to their ideas.

Core Idea
Ghazali clarified, not distorted, the philosophers' ideas.

The editor of this edition shares his personal journey teaching Ghazali's Tahafut alongside Ibn Sina's Isharat. He explains his method and concludes that Ghazali's critique actually enhanced the clarity of the philosophical ideas. This passage provides a unique perspective on Ghazali's fairness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the narrative flow, then read the English translation to ensure comprehension.
Notice the personal, reflective tone of the editor.
2
Understand the method
The editor taught the same philosophical topics from both Ibn Sina's Isharat (original) and Ghazali's Tahafut (critique) to compare them.
This sequential teaching allowed direct comparison.
3
Identify the conclusion
The editor states that Ghazali did not deviate from the correct positions of the philosophers; rather, he added brilliance and clarity.
Key phrase: 'أضفى عليها إشراقاً وصفاء' (bestowed brilliance and clarity).
4
Reflect on the significance
This conclusion counters the common misconception that Ghazali distorted the philosophers' views out of hostility.
The editor's firsthand experience lends credibility.

Days have passed quickly, and I found myself a researcher on al-Ghazali and a writer about him, then a publisher of the book al-Tahafut and a commentator on it, then dispatched to England and acquainted with orientalists up close and a reader of them, then a teacher of (the book al-Tahafut) to students of the same stage I was in when the aforementioned colleague presented his question, and a teacher at the same time of Ibn Sina's 'Book of al-Isharat' in the stage immediately below it. And it became clear to me that placing the Book of al-Isharat in a stage below the stage in which the Book of al-Tahafut is studied was intended to provide an opportunity to know the philosophical idea in its original source before being exposed to al-Ghazali's opinion on it. One can ascertain whether al-Ghazali's hostility to the philosophers led him — or did it lead him? — to distort their ideas. To achieve this goal, I had to read from the 'Book of al-Tahafut' with the students the same issue or issues that I had previously read with them in the Book of al-Isharat. Within these narrow limits I was able to discern that al-Ghazali — in his behavior with the philosophers — did not deviate from the correct position of their views. Rather, on the contrary, he bestowed upon them a brilliance and clarity that one does not find with Ibn Sina.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an editorial introduction. The editor recounts his personal experience teaching al-Ghazali's Tahafut alongside Ibn Sina's Isharat. He notes that the curriculum places Isharat in a lower stage before Tahafut to familiarize students with the original philosophical ideas before encountering al-Ghazali's critique. The editor then poses the question of whether al-Ghazali's opposition to the philosophers led him to distort their views. He explains that by teaching the same topic from both books, he was able to observe that al-Ghazali did not distort the philosophers' positions. On the contrary, he made them clearer and more brilliant than Ibn Sina's original presentation.

Ghazali's Discovery of Ibn Sina's Affirmation
Ghazali notes discovering Ibn Sina's explicit affirmation of bodily resurrection in two other works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ghazali discovered Ibn Sina's explicit affirmation of bodily resurrection in his other works, and how this demonstrates scholarly integrity.

Core Idea
Ghazali found Ibn Sina explicitly affirming bodily resurrection, showing his scholarly honesty.

This passage is a later editorial note by Imam al-Ghazali. He recalls that he had previously read in Ibn Sina's works al-Najat and al-Shifa' an explicit affirmation of bodily resurrection, contrary to what he had presented in the Tahafut. A colleague had warned about the risk of misrepresenting opponents, so Ghazali adds a marginal note to ensure accuracy. This reveals Ghazali's commitment to intellectual honesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the context
Read the Arabic text and translation, understanding that this is a personal note from Ghazali. Notice that he is reflecting on his own previous reading.
2
Identify the key claim: Ibn Sina's explicit affirmation
The passage states that in al-Najat and al-Shifa', Ibn Sina explicitly affirms bodily resurrection (البعث الجسماني). This is important because Ghazali's Tahafut presents the philosophers as denying it.
This shows Ghazali's scholarly integrity in correcting potential misrepresentation.
3
Consider the implications for Ghazali's methodology
Ghazali cites a colleague's warning about caution against deception (تدليس). By adding a marginal note, Ghazali demonstrates transparency and honesty in his critical engagement with the philosophers.
This does not mean Ghazali retracts his criticism; it shows he wanted to accurately represent Ibn Sina's views.

فحضرني ما كنت قرأته قبل ذلك في كتابى « النجاة » و « الشفاء » لابن سينا بخصوص هذه المسألة ، وفيه التصريح بالبعث الجسماني ، فذكرت على الفور ، موقف الزميل الذى نبه إلى وجوب الحذر من أن يدلس الغزالى على خصومه ، وهو يروي لهم وينقل عنهم ، فأثبت - بهامش المسألة العشرين من
It occurred to me that I had previously read in the books 'al-Najat' and 'al-Shifa'' by Ibn Sina concerning this issue, and in them is an explicit affirmation of bodily resurrection. I immediately recalled the stance of the colleague who warned about the necessity of caution lest al-Ghazali deceive his opponents, while he narrates and quotes from them. So I placed in the margin of the twentieth issue.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a later editorial note by Ghazali. He says he remembered that in Ibn Sina's other works, specifically al-Najat and al-Shifa', Ibn Sina explicitly affirms bodily resurrection. This is important because in the Tahafut, Ghazali has been presenting the philosophers as denying bodily resurrection. He mentions that a colleague had warned about the need to be cautious that Ghazali might be misrepresenting his opponents. So Ghazali adds a marginal note to the twentieth issue to clarify this. This shows Ghazali's scholarly integrity.

The Editor's Reconsideration and Discovery of Ibn Sina's Manuscript
Editor recounts his evolving interpretation and finding a manuscript that clarifies Ibn Sina's stance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the editor's discovery of Ibn Sina's manuscript resolved interpretive ambiguities and clarified Ibn Sina's stance on bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
Discovery of a manuscript can overturn prior interpretations of a thinker's position.

In this reflective passage, the editor of al-Ghazali's Tahafut shares his personal intellectual journey. He initially saw Ibn Sina as confused and al-Ghazali as aggressive, but a chance manuscript led him to a clearer understanding. This lesson examines that process and its implications for studying classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the editor's narrative
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to follow the editor's account of his evolving views. Notice the personal tone and the sequence of his reflections.
The Arabic ends with a colon indicating a direct quote from Ibn Sina's manuscript.
2
Identify the editor's initial interpretation
Look for how the editor first characterized both Ibn Sina and al-Ghazali. He describes Ibn Sina as 'hesitant and confused' and al-Ghazali as 'audacious and aggressive'. What caused him to feel uneasy with this depiction?
3
Understand the role of the manuscript
The discovery of the Risala Adhawiyya changed the editor's view. Focus on what the manuscript revealed about Ibn Sina's position: it explicitly denied bodily resurrection and contained the same proof that al-Ghazali had reported in his Tahafut.
This manuscript provided direct evidence from Ibn Sina himself.
4
Reflect on the methodological lesson
The editor shows that even scholars revise their views when new evidence emerges. This highlights the importance of primary sources and the dynamic nature of scholarly understanding.
Consider how this applies to your own study of Islamic theology.

هذا ما قلته - في الطبعة الأولى - تعليقاً على نص « النجاة » و « الشفاء » المتعارض ، بعضه مع بعض ، وتعليقاً على موقف الغزالي الغامض أيضاً بالنسبة لى أن هذا الذي انتهيت إليه ـ آنئذ هو في ذلكم الوقت ، و بالرغم من ظللت غير مرتاح ، لا إلى موقف ابن سينا فى تردده واضطرابه ، ولا إلى موقف الغزالى فى افتياته وتهجمه . هكذا تصورت الرجلين في ذلكم الوقت : أحدهما مضطرب متردد والآخر مفتات متهجم . ثم لبثت غير مطمئن إلى هذا التصوير ، إلى أن ساقت لى الصدفة مخطوطاً صغيراً لابن سينا ، عنوانه : رسالة أضحوية في أمر المعاد » فلما قرأته وجدته صريحاً فى إنكار البعث الجسماني ، ووجدته يشتمل على نفس الدليل الذى حكاه الغزالى فى التهافت على لسان الفلاسفة ، فيقول ابن سينا في هذا المخطوط :
This is what I said - in the first edition - as a commentary on the text of 'al-Najat' and 'al-Shifa' contradicting each other, and as a commentary on the ambiguous position of al-Ghazali also regarding me. That this which I concluded - at that time - is what I was at that time, and despite that I remained uneasy, neither with Ibn Sina's position in his hesitation and confusion, nor with al-Ghazali's position in his audacity and aggression. Thus I imagined the two men at that time: one hesitant and confused, the other audacious and aggressive. Then I remained not satisfied with this depiction, until chance brought me a small manuscript by Ibn Sina, titled: 'Risala Adhawiyya on the Matter of the Return' (al-Ma'ad). When I read it, I found it explicit in denying bodily resurrection, and I found it contained the same proof that al-Ghazali narrated in al-Tahafut on the tongue of the philosophers. So Ibn Sina says in this manuscript:
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a autobiographical reflection by the editor. He begins by referring to his earlier commentary in the first edition on the conflicting texts of Ibn Sina's 'al-Najat' and 'al-Shifa', and also on al-Ghazali's ambiguous position. At that time, he imagined Ibn Sina as hesitant and confused, and al-Ghazali as audacious and aggressive. However, he remained uneasy with this characterization. Then, by chance, he came across a small manuscript by Ibn Sina called 'Risala Adhawiyya on the Matter of the Return' (al-Ma'ad). Upon reading it, he found it explicitly denying bodily resurrection, and it contained the same proof that al-Ghazali had attributed to the philosophers in his 'Tahafut al-Falasifa'. The editor then introduces the direct quote from Ibn Sina. Note that the Arabic ends with a colon, leading into the proof.

Ghazali's Method for Certain Knowledge
Editor summarises Ghazali's method and lists five outcomes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's methodological approach to establishing certain knowledge and the five key outcomes of his inquiry.

Core Idea
True knowledge requires a sound foundation, tested by rigorous self-critique.

The editor opens by stressing the importance of a solid foundation for knowledge. Ghazali's quest for certainty led him to define certain knowledge, test the instruments of reason and senses, and ultimately trace knowledge back to divine inspiration. This passage outlines his method and its five outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the editor's emphasis on the need for a sound foundation.
Pay attention to the five numbered outcomes at the end.
2
Identify Ghazali's Process
Observe Ghazali's sequence: define certain knowledge, test reason and senses, encounter doubt, receive divine grace, then rebuild knowledge.
The footnote indicates detailed discussion elsewhere.
3
List the Five Outcomes
Extract each of the five outcomes listed by the editor. Understand what each one contributes to Ghazali's methodology.
Outcome five is crucial: knowledge traced to inspiration.
4
Reflect on the Role of Inspiration
Consider why Ghazali ultimately grounds knowledge in inspiration rather than reason alone. How does this resolve the doubt he faced?

الوقت ذاته ، معيار القبول والرفض عنده خاطئة أو قلقة مشوشة ، كان البناء عليها بناء على أساس غير سليم ، فيجيء إما فاسداً ، وإما واهياً متداعياً ، لهذا كان الاحتياط في إقامة الأسس أمراً على جانب عظيم من الأهمية ، بل على أعظم جانب من الأهمية . وقد أخذ الغزالى نفسه بالاحتياط اللازم ، فقد حاول : أولا : أن يحدد العلم اليقينى ، ليقيس فى ضوء تحديده معارفه ؛ فإن كانت . علماً بمعناه الصحيح استبقاها ، . إلا تخلى عنها و بحث عن سواها وقى أثناء التعرف على حد العلم وجد أن قيمة الفكرة مرتبطة ارتباطاً وثيقاً بالأداة التي عن طريقها تكتسب ، وأن الوثوق بها لا يتم إلا بعد الوثوق بالأداة نفسها، فتأدى به الأمر إلى إخضاع العقل والحواس للاختبار وقد أوغل الغزالى فى بحث صلاحية العقل والحواس لكسب العلوم ، إيغالا انتهى به إلى الثقة عدم فيهما ، وهنا ارتطم الغزالى بشك لم يكن إلى التخلص منه سبيل ، لولا رحمة من الله تداركته فأعادت إليه الوثوق بالعقل (۱) ، فراح يؤسس معارفه فى ضوئه وعلى هداه ، مترسماً خطى التحديد الدقيق الذي وضعه للعلم .
و بهذا يكون الغزالى قد وضع :
أولا - للمعرفة منهجاً قويماً .
وثانياً - للعلم حداً دقيقاً يخلصه من عناصر الغموض واللبس
وثالثاً ـ فقد أظهر استحالة الوثوق بالعقل عن طريق العقل نفسه
•
ورابعاً - قد ضرب أمثلة جديرة بالاعتبار لبيان إمكان خطأ العقل في أحكامه ، وأخرى لبيان إمكان خطأ الحواس
وخامساً ـ قد رد أساس المعرفة إلى الإلهام لا إلى العقل ؛ إذ لولا الثقة في أن الله لا يمنحنا طبيعة مزيفة ، لما أمكننا التعويل على العقل في اكتساب المعرفة وأدع الغزالى يشرح - بأسلو به الواضح الآخاذ – قصته بقوله :
(۱) راجع ما كتبناه - فى مقدمتنا لكتاب الإشارات المطبوع ضمن سلسلة الذخائر تعليقاً على عودة الغزالي للعقل، وعودة وثوقه به وما كتبناه في مقدمتنا لكتاب ( الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد ) الذي يعد الآن للنشر .
[At the same time,] the criterion of acceptance and rejection according to him [was] mistaken or anxious and confused, so building upon it is building upon an unsound foundation, so it comes either corrupt or weak and crumbling. For this reason, precaution in establishing foundations is a matter of great importance, indeed of the greatest importance. And al-Ghazali himself took the necessary precaution, for he tried: first: to define certain knowledge (al-ʿilm al-yaqīnī), so that he might measure his cognitions in light of its definition; for if they were knowledge in its true meaning he would retain them, otherwise he would abandon them and seek others. While getting to know the definition of knowledge, he found that the value of an idea is closely linked to the instrument by which it is acquired, and that trust in it is only completed after trust in the instrument itself. This led him to subject reason and the senses to testing. And al-Ghazali delved deeply into investigating the validity of reason and the senses for acquiring sciences, a delving that ended with him [having] no confidence in them. Here al-Ghazali encountered a doubt from which there was no way to escape, had it not been for a mercy from God that overtook him and restored to him trust in reason (1). So he began to establish his cognitions in its light and guidance, tracing the precise definition he had set for knowledge. And by this, al-Ghazali had established: First - a sound methodology for knowledge. Second - a precise definition for knowledge that purifies it from elements of ambiguity and confusion. Third - he showed the impossibility of trusting reason by means of reason itself. Fourth - he gave examples worthy of consideration to show the possibility of error of reason in its judgments, and others to show the possibility of error of the senses. Fifth - he traced the basis of knowledge back to inspiration (al-ilhām), not to reason; for were it not for the trust that God does not grant us a deceptive nature, we could not rely on reason in acquiring knowledge. And let al-Ghazali explain - in his clear, captivating style - his story by saying: (1) Refer to what we wrote - in our introduction to the book al-Ishārāt, printed within the Dhakhā'ir series, commenting on al-Ghazali's return to reason and his trust in it, and what we wrote in our introduction to the book al-Iqtiṣād fī al-Iʻtiqād, which is now being prepared for publication.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is an editorial summary explaining Ghazali's preliminary method in his quest for certain knowledge. The editor first states that if the foundation of knowledge is flawed, any building upon it will be corrupt or weak. Hence, Ghazali took great precaution. He began by defining certain knowledge (al-ʿilm al-yaqīnī). While defining it, he realized that the value of an idea depends on the reliability of the instrument (the intellect and senses) by which it is acquired. So he subjected both reason and the senses to rigorous testing, and this led him to doubt them until divine grace restored his trust in reason. Then the editor lists five key outcomes of Ghazali's methodology: (1) a sound methodology for knowledge; (2) a precise definition of knowledge free from ambiguity; (3) the impossibility of proving the reliability of reason by reason itself; (4) examples illustrating the possibility of error in both reason and the senses; and (5) the grounding of knowledge in divine inspiration rather than reason alone, since without trust that God does not deceive, reason cannot be relied upon. Finally, the editor introduces Ghazali's own narrative quotation. The footnote directs readers to the editor's other works for further discussion.

Key Terms
العلم اليقيني
العقل
الحواس
الإلهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali completely rejected reason and accepted only inspiration.
Correction: Ghazali subjected reason to rigorous testing and eventually restored trust in it through divine grace, but he did not reject it outright; rather, he grounded it in divine inspiration, making reason reliable only because God guarantees its trustworthiness.

Checkpoint
According to the editor, what is the fifth outcome of Ghazali's method?
A) He defined certain knowledge precisely.
B) He showed the impossibility of trusting reason by reason itself.
C) He traced the basis of knowledge to inspiration, not reason.
D) He gave examples of errors in the senses.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's method shows that certain knowledge requires a secure foundation, achieved by critically examining reason and senses, and ultimately relying on divine inspiration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek certainty in your beliefs, have you ever subjected your own reasoning to the kind of rigorous test that Ghazali performed on his? What would it mean for you to truly examine the instruments by which you claim to know something?
?
Ghazali only escaped doubt through a mercy from God. Are you willing to admit that without divine intervention, your own intellect is insufficient to reach certainty? How does this humility change the way you approach your faith?
?
The editor says Ghazali traced the basis of knowledge back to inspiration, not reason. Does your daily reliance on your own understanding leave any room for that kind of dependence on God? What would it look like to make inspiration your ultimate foundation?

Ghazali on Certain Knowledge and Sensory Doubt
Ghazali defines certain knowledge and then questions even sensory truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's definition of certain knowledge and his method of systematic doubt regarding the senses.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge cannot be shaken even by miracles, but the senses can deceive.

In this autobiographical passage from al-Munqidh min al-Ḍalāl, Ghazali recounts his philosophical quest for certainty. He first defines what true certainty means, then turns to examine the reliability of sense perception. This sets the stage for his eventual resolution through divine illumination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the personal narrative style as Ghazali describes his inner reflection.
2
Identify the definition of certain knowledge
Find the sentence where Ghazali defines certain knowledge (al-ʿilm al-yaqīnī). Note the key characteristics: disclosure without doubt, no possibility of error, and security from error that would withstand even a miracle.
The example of ten and three illustrates this indubitability.
3
Understand the test of sensory knowledge
After defining certainty, Ghazali examines his own beliefs and finds only sensory and necessary truths seem certain. He then subjects these to doubt to see if they meet his definition.
Pay attention to the shadow example that shows sensory deception.
4
Reflect on the purpose of doubt
Ghazali is not a skeptic; he is methodologically testing the foundations of knowledge. His doubt is a tool to find indubitable truths.
This is a common misunderstanding to avoid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقلت في نفسي إنما مطلوبي العلم بحقائق الأمور ، فلابد من طلب حقيقة العلم ما هي ؟
فظهر لى أن العلم اليقيني هو الذي تنكشف فيه العلوم انكشافاً لا ريب ، ولا يتمارنه إمكان الغلط والوهم ، ولا يتسع القلب لتقدير ذلك ، بل الأمان من الخطأ ينبغى أن يكون مقارناً لليقين مقارنة لو تحدى بإظهار بطلانه مثلا من يقلب الحجر ذهباً والعصا ثعباناً لم يورث ذلك شكئًا وإنكاراً ، فإنى إذا علمت أن العشرة أكثر من الثلاثة ، فلو قال لى قائل : لا ، بل الثلاثة أكثر بدليل أنى أقلب هذه العصا ثعباناً ، وقلبها ، وشاهدت ذلك منه ، لم أشك بسببه فى معرفتى ، ولم يحصل منه إلا التعجب من كيفية قدرته عليه ! فأما الشك فيما علمته ، فلا
ثم علمت أن كل ما لا أعلمه على هذا الوجه ، ولا أتيقنه هذا النوع من اليقين، فهو علم لاثقة به ، ولا أمان معه ، وكل علم لا أمان معه ، فليس بعلم يقيني . ثم فتشت عن علومى ، فوجدت نفسي عاطلا من علم موصوف بهذه الصفة، إلا فى الحسيات والضروريات فقلت ، الآن بعد حصول اليأس ، لا مطمع في اكتساب المشكلات إلا من الجليات ، وهى الحسيات والضروريات ، فلابد من إحكامها أولا لأتيقن أن ثقتى بالمحسوسات ، وأماني من الغلط في الضروريات ، أهو من جنس أمانى الذى كان من قبل فى التقليديات ، ومن جنس أمان أكثر الخلق في النظريات ، أم هو أمان محقق لا غدر فيه ولا غائلة له ؟ فأقبلت بجد بليغ ، أتأمل فى المحسوسات والضروريات وأنظر هل يمكننى أن أشكك نفسى فيها ، فانتهى بى طول التشكيك إلى أن لم تسمح نفسى بتسليم الأمان في المحسوسات أيضاً، وأخذ يتسع هذا الشك فيها ويقول : من أين الثقة بالمحسوسات ، وأقواها حاسة البصر ، وهي تنظر إلى الظل فتراه واقفاً غير متحرك ، وتحكم بنفى الحركة ؟ ثم بالتجربة والمشاهدة بعد ساعة تعرف أنه متحرك ، وأنه لم يتحرك دفعة بغتة ، بل على التدريج ذرة ذرة ، حتى لم تكن له حالة وقوف
I said to myself: my aim is knowledge of the realities of things, so I must seek what the reality of knowledge is. It became clear to me that certain knowledge (al-ʿilm al-yaqīnī) is that in which what is known is disclosed with a disclosure that leaves no doubt, and the possibility of error and illusion does not accompany it, nor does the heart allow room for that estimation; rather, security from error should be associated with certainty in such a way that if someone were to challenge [it] by demonstrating its falsity – for example, by turning a stone into gold and a stick into a serpent – that would not engender any doubt or denial. For if I know that ten is more than three, and someone says to me: 'No, three is more, and my proof is that I turn this stick into a serpent,' and he actually turns it and I see it, I would not doubt my knowledge because of that; it would only cause me to marvel at how he is able to do it! As for doubting what I know, no. Then I knew that everything I do not know in this way, and do not have of this kind of certainty, is knowledge that is not reliable, nor is there security with it; and every knowledge that has no security with it is not certain knowledge. Then I examined my knowledge and found myself empty of knowledge described by this attribute, except in sensory (ḥissiyāt) and necessary (ḍarūriyyāt) [truths]. So I said: now, having despaired, there is no hope of acquiring problematic matters except from self-evident ones, namely the sensory and necessary. So I must master them first to ascertain whether my trust in sensory things and my security from error in necessary things is of the same kind as my previous trust in traditional matters and of the kind of trust of most people in theoretical matters, or is it a verified security that has no treachery and no hidden flaw? So I set about with great earnestness, contemplating sensory and necessary things and considering whether I could cast doubt on myself regarding them. And prolonged doubting led me to the point where my soul would not concede security in sensory things either. And this doubt began to expand concerning them, saying: 'Whence the trust in sensory things, the strongest of which is the sense of sight, which sees the shadow and finds it stationary, not moving, and judges that there is no motion? Then, after an hour, through experience and observation, you know that it is moving, and that it did not move all at once, but gradually, bit by bit, so that it never had a state of rest.'
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's own narrative of his quest for certain knowledge, taken from his autobiographical work al-Munqidh min al-Ḍalāl. He begins by stating his aim: to know the reality of knowledge itself. He then defines certain knowledge (al-ʿilm al-yaqīnī) as a disclosure so complete that no possibility of error or illusion remains, and the heart has no room to even consider the chance of mistake. The security from error must be so absolute that even if someone performed a miracle like turning a stone into gold or a stick into a serpent to disprove it, it would not cause him to doubt the original knowledge; he would simply marvel at the miracle. His example is the knowledge that ten is more than three — no miracle could shake that. He then concludes that any knowledge that does not meet this standard is not reliable, hence not certain. Upon examining all his beliefs, he found that only sensory and necessary truths (like the ten/three example) seemed to possess this certainty. So he decided to test even these, to see if his trust in them was solid or could be undermined. He then began a process of systematic doubt, and found that even the senses can be deceived: the sense of sight sees a shadow as stationary, but later observation shows it moves. Thus, the sensory truths also become doubtful. The passage ends as he is deepening his doubt about sensory knowledge, setting the stage for his eventual resolution through divine illumination.

Key Terms
العلم اليقيني
المحسوسات
الضروريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is a complete skeptic who denies all knowledge.
Correction: Ghazali's doubt is a methodological tool to test the foundations of certainty, not an end in itself. He later affirms certain knowledge through divine illumination.

Checkpoint
What example does Ghazali use to illustrate the indubitability of certain knowledge?
A) A shadow that appears to move but is still.
B) Knowing that ten is more than three, even if someone turns a stick into a serpent.
C) The sense of sight perceiving a stationary shadow.
D) The possibility of turning stone into gold.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defines certain knowledge as that which cannot be shaken even by miracles, yet he argues that sensory knowledge fails this test due to illusions like the moving shadow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You likely claim to 'know' many things about your faith, your life, and yourself. But what have you never actually tested? If a doubt were to enter your heart, would it crumble? Ghazali sought a certainty that could withstand anything. What is one belief you hold that you have not examined with such rigor? Will you examine it this week?
?
Imagine someone performed a clear miracle — the kind you'd have to see to believe — to prove something that contradicts a core belief of yours. Would you doubt your belief, or would you, like Ghazali, remain certain and simply marvel? Why? What does your answer reveal about the depth of your conviction?
?
Your senses have deceived you before — perhaps you thought you heard something or saw movement that wasn't there. If such small deceits happen daily, how can you be sure that your perception of Allah's signs, or your own spiritual state, is accurate? What step can you take today to ground your trust in something beyond your senses?

The Star Example: Sense Corrected by Reason
Ghazali gives an example of a star that appears small but is large, showing reason overruling sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reason can correct sense perception, using the example of the star.

Core Idea
The senses can deceive, but reason reveals the truth.

In this passage, Ghazali uses a simple observation—a star that looks tiny to the eye—to illustrate a profound epistemological point: our senses are not always reliable. He shows that rational demonstration can overturn sensory evidence, a claim that becomes a crucial step in his skeptical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's concise style come through.
Notice the contrast between the sensory judgment (small) and the rational judgment (large).
2
Identify the two judgments
Identify the judgment of sense (حاكم الحس) and the judgment of reason (حاكم العقل). See how reason belies the senses.
The word 'يكذبه' (belies) is strong—it means reason declares the senses false.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: if reason can correct the senses here, can reason itself be doubted? This sets the stage for Ghazali's later skepticism.
This example is a building block, not the final conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتنظر إلى الكوكب فتراه صغيراً فى مقدار دينار ، ثم الأدلة الهندسية تدل على أنه أكبر من الأرض في المقدار. هذا وأمثاله من المحسوسات يحكم فيه حاكم الحس بأحكامه ، ويكذبه حاكم العقل ويخونه ، تكذيباً لا سبيل إلى مدافعته.
You look at the star and see it as small, about the size of a dinar. Then geometrical proofs indicate that it is larger than the earth in size. This and similar sense-perceptions (al-mahsūsāt): the judge of sense (ḥākim al-ḥiss) passes its judgments on them, but the judge of reason (ḥākim al-ʿaql) belies it and betrays it, a refutation that cannot be repelled.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a concrete example used to demonstrate the fallibility of sense perception. Ghazali observes that the star appears tiny to the eye, yet rational geometrical proof shows it is actually vast. He generalizes this to all sense-perceptions: the sensory judge issues its verdict, but the rational judge contradicts it decisively. The point is that reason can correct the senses, but this very fact will later be turned into a skeptical argument against reason itself.

Key Terms
المحسوسات
حاكم الحس
حاكم العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: What we see with our eyes is always accurate.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the eye perceives the star as small, but reason proves it is larger than the earth, so senses can be mistaken.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's example, what does the sense of sight perceive about the star?
A) It is larger than the earth.
B) It is small, about the size of a dinar.
C) It is round.
D) It is moving rapidly.

Key Takeaway
Reason can correct the senses, as demonstrated by the star that appears small but is actually large.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The star appears small, but reason shows it's enormous. What 'small' truths in your life might actually be much larger and more significant than you perceive? Will you seek deeper understanding through reason and revelation?
?
If reason can correct your eyes, can it also correct your heart's assumptions? What is one belief you hold about yourself or God that you have never examined rationally, and what step will you take today to test it?

The Skeptical Challenge to Rational Truths
Ghazali introduces the possibility that rational truths may be as unreliable as sense if a higher judge exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali presents a skeptical argument that rational a priori truths may be unreliable if a higher epistemic authority exists.

Core Idea
Rational truths are not immune to doubt because a higher judge could overturn them.

In this passage, Ghazali takes the skeptical argument further. Having cast doubt on sense perception, he now questions the certainty of rational truths. The sense-perceptions are personified to argue that reason itself was the judge that disproved them, so why should reason be trusted? Perhaps a higher judge—such as mystical insight—could similarly disprove reason. This introduces a radical epistemological skepticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the personification of sense-perceptions.
2
Identify the key argument
Identify the argument: reason overthrew sense, so sense questions reason's authority. Look for the phrase 'fla'alla warā'a idrāk al-ʿaql ḥākiman ākhara'.
3
Reflect on the possibility of a higher judge
Consider the implications: if a higher judge could overturn reason, then rational truths are not absolutely certain. Reflect on why the non-manifestation of that judge does not prove impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقلت : قد بطلت الثقة بالمحسوسات أيضاً ، فلعله لا ثقة إلا بالعقليات التي هي من الأوليات ، كقولنا : العشرة أكثر من الثلاثة ، والنفى والإثبات لا يجتمعان فى الشيء الواحد ، والشيء الواحد لا يكون حادثاً قديماً ، موجوداً معدوماً ، واجباً محالاً فقالت المحسوسات : بم تأمن (١) أن تكون ثقتك بالعقليات كثقتك بالمحسوسات وقد كنت واثقاً بى ، فجاء حاكم العقل فكذبني ، ولولا حاكم العقل لكنت تستمر على تصديقى ؟ فلعل وراء إدراك العقل حاكماً آخر ، إذا تجلى كذب العقل في حكمه ، كما تجلى حاكم العقل فكذب الحس في حكمه، وعدم تجلى ذلك الإدراك لا يدل على استحالته . فتوقفت النفس في جواب ذلك قليلا وأيدت أشكالها بالمنام وقالت : أما تراك تعتقد في النوم أموراً وتتخيل أحوالاً وتعتقد لها ثباتاً واستقراراً ، ولا تشك في تلك الحالة فيها ، ثم تستيقظ فتعلم أنه لم يكن الجميع متخيلاتك ومعتقداتك أصل وطائل ؟ فيم تأمن أن يكون جميع ما تعتقده في يقظتك بحس أو عقل ، هو حق بالإضافة إلى حالتك التي أنت فيها ، لكن يمكن أن تطرأ عليك حالة تكون نسبتها إلى يقظتك كنسبة يقظتك إلى منامك ، وتكون يقظتك نوماً بالإضافة إليها ، فإذا وردت تلك الحالة تيقنت جميع ما توهمت بعقلك خيالات لا حاصل لها . ولعل تلك الحالة ما يدعيه الصوفية أنها حالتهم. إذ يزعمون أنهم يشاهدون في أحوالهم التي لهم – إذا غاصوا في أنفسهم ، وغابوا عن حواسهم - أحوالاً لا توافق هذه المعقولات.
I said: 'Trust in sense-perceptions has also been invalidated. Perhaps there is no trust except in rational truths (al-ʿaqliyyāt) that are among the primary truths (al-awwaliyyāt), such as our saying: ten is more than three, negation and affirmation cannot coexist in one thing, one thing cannot be both originated and eternal, both existent and non-existent, both necessary and impossible.' Then sense-perceptions said: 'What assurance do you have that your trust in rational truths is like your trust in sense-perceptions? You used to trust me, then the judge of reason came and belied me. Were it not for the judge of reason, you would have continued to believe me. Perhaps behind the perception of reason there is another judge who, when he manifests, will belie reason in its judgment, just as the judge of reason manifested and belied sense in its judgment. The non-manifestation of that perception does not prove its impossibility.' Then the soul paused in response to that for a while, and it reinforced its doubts by [the example of] sleep, saying: 'Do you not see that in sleep you believe in matters and imagine states, and you consider them stable and settled, and you do not doubt them in that state? Then you wake up and know that all your imaginings and beliefs had no substance or reality. So what assurance do you have that everything you believe in your waking state, whether by sense or reason, is true relative to your current state? But it is possible that a state may come upon you whose relation to your waking state is like the relation of your waking state to your sleep, and your waking state would be sleep compared to it. When that state comes, you will be certain that all that you imagined by your reason are baseless fantasies. Perhaps that state is what the Sufis claim as their state, for they claim that in their states – when they dive into themselves and become absent from their senses – they witness states that do not conform to these rational truths.'
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a skeptical argument against the certainty of rational a priori truths. Ghazali first states that after losing trust in the senses, he considers rational truths as secure. But the sense-perceptions are personified and reply: you trusted me, but reason overruled me; so why should you trust reason when perhaps a higher judge (such as mystical insight or the afterlife) might overrule reason? The non-appearance of that judge does not prove its impossibility. The soul then reinforces this doubt by the analogy of dreaming: in a dream, you are fully convinced of falsehoods, only to realize upon waking that they were unreal. Similarly, your waking state might be a dream relative to a higher state of consciousness, such as that claimed by Sufis who experience truths beyond reason. Ghazali presents this as a serious epistemological challenge.

Key Terms
العقليات
الأوليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali is arguing that rational truths are false or that he endorses skepticism.
Correction: He is presenting a skeptical challenge to be resolved, not advocating for skepticism. The passage is part of a larger argument leading to a defense of certain knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the personified sense-perception say about rational truths in this passage?
A) They are absolutely certain because they are self-evident.
B) They may be overturned by a higher judge, just as sense was overturned by reason.
C) They are less reliable than sense-perception.
D) They are impossible to know.

Key Takeaway
Rational a priori truths are not beyond doubt because a higher epistemic authority could theoretically disprove them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of the single most certain belief you hold about Allah or your faith. What would it mean for you if a higher authority—like a deeper unveiling of divine truth—were to show that belief to be incomplete or even wrong? Would you submit to that higher judgment, or would you cling to your current understanding out of pride? Where is your ultimate trust placed: in your intellect or in the truth that God may reveal beyond it?
?
The passage suggests that your trust in reason is no different from your earlier trust in your senses. In what areas of your life—especially in matters of religion—are you relying on your own reasoning more than on the revealed text or the guidance of the scholars? Can you identify a specific issue where you have resisted a teaching because it didn't 'make sense' to you? What would it take for you to genuinely open yourself to the possibility that your reasoning might be overturned by a higher judgment?
?
Non-manifestation of a higher judge does not prove its impossibility. Are there truths in your spiritual life that you have assumed are settled simply because you have not been challenged by a higher perspective? Could it be that your current level of certainty is actually a form of complacency? What would it cost you to actively seek that higher judge—through deep study of revelation, sincere supplication, or the company of the righteous—even if it risks unsettling your current convictions?

The Dream Analogy and Sufi Higher State
Ghazali uses the dream analogy to doubt rational truths and mentions Sufi states as a possible higher reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of dreaming and waking calls into question the certainty of rational beliefs.

Core Idea
Our waking state might be a dream relative to a higher state of consciousness.

Ghazali deepens the skeptical challenge by using the example of dreaming. In a dream, we are fully convinced of falsehoods, only to realize upon waking they were illusions. Similarly, our waking convictions—even those based on reason—might be illusions relative to a higher state, such as that experienced by Sufis. This analogy is powerful in undermining epistemic certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the dream analogy
Read the passage starting from 'فتوقفت النفس' to the end. Notice how the analogy is presented.
2
Understand the comparison
Identify the key comparison: waking state is to higher state as sleep is to waking. This implies that our current certainty could be delusive.
3
Consider the Sufi reference
Reflect on the mention of Sufis who claim to experience states that contradict rational truths. What does this suggest about the limits of reason?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقلت : قد بطلت الثقة بالمحسوسات أيضاً ، فلعله لا ثقة إلا بالعقليات التي هي من الأوليات ، كقولنا : العشرة أكثر من الثلاثة ، والنفى والإثبات لا يجتمعان فى الشيء الواحد ، والشيء الواحد لا يكون حادثاً قديماً ، موجوداً معدوماً ، واجباً محالاً فقالت المحسوسات : بم تأمن (١) أن تكون ثقتك بالعقليات كثقتك بالمحسوسات وقد كنت واثقاً بى ، فجاء حاكم العقل فكذبني ، ولولا حاكم العقل لكنت تستمر على تصديقى ؟ فلعل وراء إدراك العقل حاكماً آخر ، إذا تجلى كذب العقل في حكمه ، كما تجلى حاكم العقل فكذب الحس في حكمه، وعدم تجلى ذلك الإدراك لا يدل على استحالته . فتوقفت النفس في جواب ذلك قليلا وأيدت أشكالها بالمنام وقالت : أما تراك تعتقد في النوم أموراً وتتخيل أحوالاً وتعتقد لها ثباتاً واستقراراً ، ولا تشك في تلك الحالة فيها ، ثم تستيقظ فتعلم أنه لم يكن الجميع متخيلاتك ومعتقداتك أصل وطائل ؟ فيم تأمن أن يكون جميع ما تعتقده في يقظتك بحس أو عقل ، هو حق بالإضافة إلى حالتك التي أنت فيها ، لكن يمكن أن تطرأ عليك حالة تكون نسبتها إلى يقظتك كنسبة يقظتك إلى منامك ، وتكون يقظتك نوماً بالإضافة إليها ، فإذا وردت تلك الحالة تيقنت جميع ما توهمت بعقلك خيالات لا حاصل لها . ولعل تلك الحالة ما يدعيه الصوفية أنها حالتهم. إذ يزعمون أنهم يشاهدون في أحوالهم التي لهم – إذا غاصوا في أنفسهم ، وغابوا عن حواسهم - أحوالاً لا توافق هذه المعقولات.
I said: 'Trust in sense-perceptions has also been invalidated. Perhaps there is no trust except in rational truths (al-ʿaqliyyāt) that are among the primary truths (al-awwaliyyāt), such as our saying: ten is more than three, negation and affirmation cannot coexist in one thing, one thing cannot be both originated and eternal, both existent and non-existent, both necessary and impossible.' Then sense-perceptions said: 'What assurance do you have that your trust in rational truths is like your trust in sense-perceptions? You used to trust me, then the judge of reason came and belied me. Were it not for the judge of reason, you would have continued to believe me. Perhaps behind the perception of reason there is another judge who, when he manifests, will belie reason in its judgment, just as the judge of reason manifested and belied sense in its judgment. The non-manifestation of that perception does not prove its impossibility.' Then the soul paused in response to that for a while, and it reinforced its doubts by [the example of] sleep, saying: 'Do you not see that in sleep you believe in matters and imagine states, and you consider them stable and settled, and you do not doubt them in that state? Then you wake up and know that all your imaginings and beliefs had no substance or reality. Death as the Ultimate Awakening
Ghazali equates death with awakening from worldly sleep.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the analogy of sleep and death to question epistemic certainty and prepare for the afterlife.

Core Idea
Death is the ultimate awakening from the dream of this world.

Ghazali deepens his skeptical analogy by suggesting that death is the state where true reality is revealed. Citing a famous hadith and a Quranic verse, he argues that our worldly perceptions may be like a dream compared to the afterlife. This lesson explores how this analogy challenges our confidence in empirical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the hadith and Quranic reference. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The hadith is central to the analogy.
2
Understand the analogy
How does Ghazali compare worldly life to sleep and death to awakening? Identify the key elements: life as sleep, death as waking up.
3
Consider the implications
If death reveals true reality, what does that say about knowledge in this life? Think about how this challenges certainty.
This connects to Ghazali's broader skeptical argument.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verse
How does Qaf 50:22 ('We have removed your covering...') support the idea of covering being removed at death?
The verse is used as confirmation from revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولعل تلك الحالة هي الموت ، إذ قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم « الناس نيام فإذا ماتوا انتبهوا » . فلعل الحياة الدنيا نوم بالإضافة إلى الآخرة فإذا مات ظهرت له الأشياء على خلاف ما يشاهده الآن ، ويقال له عند ذلك : فكشفنا عنك غطاءك فبصرك اليوم حديد.
Perhaps that state is death, for the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: 'People are asleep, and when they die, they wake up.' So perhaps worldly life is sleep compared to the afterlife; when a person dies, things appear to him contrary to what he now sees, and it is said to him at that time: 'We have removed your covering from you, and your sight today is sharp.'
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous dream analogy by identifying the higher state with death. Ghazali cites the prophetic hadith: 'People are asleep; when they die, they wake up.' Thus, worldly life is sleep relative to the afterlife. At death, the true nature of things is revealed, opposite to what was perceived in this life. The Quranic verse (Qaf 50:22) 'We have removed your covering...' is invoked to support this. This reinforces the skeptical argument: our current certainties may be illusions compared to the reality of the afterlife.
