The Sufi Path: Unveiling and Cutting Attachments
Ghazali introduces the Sufi path and its foundational principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational principle of the Sufi path as presented by Ghazali—cutting the heart's attachments to the world.

Core Idea
Sufism's foundation is cutting worldly attachments to attain unveiling.

In this passage, Ghazali introduces the fourth group, the Sufis, and explains their claim to direct knowledge of divine secrets through unveiling and vision. He then describes his personal adoption of their path, which led him to abandon his status and wander in seclusion. The core teaching is that the Sufi method rests on cutting the heart's attachments from this world, turning entirely to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice how Ghazali moves from describing the Sufi claim to his personal experience.
2
Identify the Sufi claim and method
Identify what the Sufis claim (unveiling, direct vision, access to the Preserved Tablet) and their stated method (knowledge and action).
The question 'What is the path?' and answer 'knowledge and action' is key.
3
Understand the foundation of cutting attachments
Focus on the paragraph beginning with 'the foundation of their way is: 1. Cutting the heart's attachments...' Break down each element mentioned.
This is the core lesson: the heart must be detached from worldly concerns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقيت رابعة الفرق، بقى المتصوفة الذين يقولون بالكشف والمعاينة، والاتصال بعالم الملكوت، والأخذ عنه مباشرة، والاطلاع على اللوح المحفوظ وما يحتويه من أسرار، ولكن ما الطريق إلى الكشف والمعاينة؟، أجابوه بأنها علم وعمل. مضى الغزالى يستوضحهم ويطبق على نفسه حتى أدت الحال إلى أن به ترك هذا الجاه العريض، والشأن المنظوم الخالي عن التكدير والتنغيص، والأمن المسلّم الصافي عن منازعة الخصوم. وخرج هائماً على وجهه إلى الصحارى والقفار، ذاهباً مرة إلى الشام، وأخرى إلى الحجاز، وثالثة إلى مصر. كل ذلك فراراً بنفسه من الناس، وجرياً وراء الخلوة، تطبيقاً لما أشار به عليه الصوفية، الذين يرون أن أساس طريقتهم. 1 قطع علائق القلب عن الدنيا؛ بالتجافى عن دار الغرور، والإنابة إلى دار الخلود، والإقبال بكنه الهمة على الله تعالى، وذلك لا يتم إلا بالإعراض عن الجاه والمال، والهرب من الشواغل والعلائق، بل يصير قلبه إلى حالة يستوى فيها وجود كل شيء وعدمه.
The fourth group remains, the Sufis who claim unveiling and direct vision, connection with the world of the Kingdom, taking directly from it, and access to the Preserved Tablet and what it contains of secrets. But what is the path to unveiling and vision? They answered that it is knowledge and action. al-Ghazali proceeded to question them and apply it to himself until the situation led him to abandon this great status, and the organized affair free from trouble and vexation, and the assured safety free from the contention of opponents. And he went out wandering to deserts and wastelands, going once to Syria, another time to the Hijaz, and a third to Egypt. All that was to flee from people, and to pursue seclusion, applying what the Sufis had indicated to him, who consider that the foundation of their way is: 1. Cutting the heart's attachments from this world; by turning away from the abode of delusion, and turning to the abode of eternity, and directing the whole intention towards God Almighty; and that is only achieved by turning away from status and wealth, and fleeing from distractions and attachments; rather, his heart reaches a state where the existence of everything and its non-existence are equal.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth group, the Sufis, and their claim of direct knowledge (kashf) and vision (muʻayana) through connection with the angelic realm and the Preserved Tablet. When asked the method, they replied 'knowledge and action.' Ghazali then describes his own adoption of this path: he abandoned his prestigious position and security, and wandered in seclusion through Syria, Hijaz, and Egypt. The foundation of the Sufi method, as Ghazali explains, is cutting the heart's attachments from the world by turning away from worldly delusion and turning entirely to God. This is achieved by renouncing status and wealth, escaping distractions, until the heart reaches a state of indifference to the existence or non-existence of any worldly thing.

Key Terms
كشف
معاينة
لوح محفوظ
علم وعمل
زهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sufism is merely passive withdrawal from the world without any structured practice.
Correction: The passage shows that Sufism requires active renunciation, turning of the heart, and a state of indifference through specific practices.

Checkpoint
What is the foundation of the Sufi path according to this passage?
A) Knowledge and practice
B) Asceticism and worship
C) Cutting the heart's attachments from the world
D) Traveling to Syria

Key Takeaway
The Sufi path's foundation is severing all worldly attachments so that the heart becomes solely focused on God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What status or wealth are you holding onto that, if you were honest with yourself, you know is an obstacle between you and Allah? Are you willing to actually flee from it, not just in theory but in practice, as al-Ghazali did?
?
Imagine your heart reaching a state where the existence or non-existence of everything you love is equal to you. What would that feel like? What attachment do you secretly fear losing that reveals you are not there yet?
?
Al-Ghazali abandoned a life of security, prestige, and ease to wander in deserts. What comfort or approval from people are you unwilling to sacrifice? When will you stop justifying your attachment and truly begin to cut it?

Stages of Dhikr According to Ghazali
Ghazali details the progressive stages of remembrance leading to divine openings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the progressive stages of dhikr as described by Imam al-Ghazali, from verbal repetition to the loss of choice and the awaiting of divine openings.

Core Idea
Dhikr moves from tongue to heart to divine openings.

Imam al-Ghazali provides a detailed description of the Sufi practice of dhikr (remembrance). He outlines a progression that begins with verbal repetition and culminates in a state where the practitioner awaits divine openings. This passage is central to understanding the Sufi method of spiritual purification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow. Notice the stages from verbal to silent.
2
Identify the stages
List the stages: seclusion, verbal dhikr, habitual flow, meaning without words, loss of choice, waiting.
3
Understand the loss of choice
Note that choice ends after the stage of pure meaning; only continuance and waiting remain.
4
Reflect on the goal
Ask yourself: what is the purpose of this practice? To reach divine openings (futuhat). This is the ultimate aim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن تمام طريقتهم أيضاً، كما يقول الغزالي: أن تخلو بنفسك فى زاوية، تقتصر، من العبادة على الفرائض والرواتب وتجلس فارغ القلب، مجموع الهم، مقبلا بذكرك على الله، وذلك في أول الأمر بأن تواظب باللسان على ذكر الله تعالى فلا تزال تقول: الله الله مع حضور القلب وإدراكه، إلى أن تنتهى إلى حالة لو تركت تحريك اللسان لرأيت كأن الكلمة جارية على لسانك لكثرة اعتياده، ثم تصير مواظباً عليه، إلى أن لا يبقى في قلبك إلا معنى اللفظ، ولا يخطر ببالك حروف اللفظ وهيئات الكلمة، بل يبقى المعنى المجرد حاضراً فى قلبك على اللزوم والدوام، ولك اختيار إلى هذا الحد فقط، ولا اختيار بعده لك، إلا في الاستدامة لدفع الوساوس الصارفة، ثم ينقطع اختيارك فلا يبقى لك إلا الانتظار لما يظهر من فتوح ظهر مثله للأولياء، وهو بعض ما يظهر الأنباء . . . ومنازل أولياء الله فيه لا تحصى.
And part of their complete way also, as al-Ghazali says: that you seclude yourself in a corner, limiting worship to the obligatory and supererogatory prayers, and you sit empty-hearted, with gathered concern, turning your dhikr towards God. At the beginning, you persist with the tongue in the remembrance of God Almighty, so you keep saying: 'Allah, Allah' with presence of heart and awareness, until you reach a state where if you left off moving your tongue, you would see that the word flows on your tongue due to much habit. Then you become persistent upon it until there remains in your heart only the meaning of the word, and the letters of the word and the forms of the word do not occur to your mind, rather only the abstract meaning remains present in your heart continuously and perpetually. You have choice only up to this limit, and after that you have no choice except in continuance to repel distracting thoughts. Then your choice ends, and there remains for you only waiting for what appears of openings [futuhat] of the like that appeared to the saints, and it is some of what appears from the Prophet [؟]. And the stations of the saints of God therein are countless.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's detailed description of the Sufi practice of dhikr (remembrance). It begins with seclusion and limiting worship to obligatory and sunna prayers. The practitioner sits with an empty heart, focusing entirely on God. The first stage is verbal repetition of 'Allah, Allah' with presence of heart. Eventually, repetition becomes habitual such that even without moving the tongue, the word seems to flow. Then comes a stage where only the meaning of the word remains in the heart, detached from linguistic form. The practitioner has free choice only up to this point; after that, choice ends and the practitioner can only strive to maintain the state and repel distractions. Then even that choice ceases, and one simply waits for divine openings (futuhat) similar to those experienced by saints (awliya). The number of stations for saints is uncountable.

Key Terms
ذكر
خلوة
فتح
ولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dhikr is merely mechanical repetition of words.
Correction: Ghazali explains that dhikr progresses from verbal repetition to interiorization of meaning, culminating in a state where choice ends and one awaits divine openings.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, at what stage does the practitioner no longer have choice?
A) When the word flows automatically
B) When only the meaning remains
C) When choice ends and only waiting remains
D) At the beginning

Key Takeaway
Dhikr is a progressive practice that moves from verbal repetition to the heart, and finally to the awaiting of divine openings beyond personal choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to sit alone in a corner, limiting yourself to obligatory worship, what would your heart be full of? Are you willing to empty your heart of everything but God, even the need for control?
?
You have choice up to a point in dhikr—but then your choice ends. Are you ready to let go of your own effort and simply wait for God to open your heart, trusting that He will act when He wills?
?
The 'word flows on your tongue' through habit—but has your dhikr ever moved beyond your tongue into the silent presence of meaning? What is holding you back from that interior transformation?

The Sufi Method of Purification and Laduni Knowledge
Ghazali describes the Sufi method and introduces laduni knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sufi method of achieving knowledge through inner purification and the concept of laduni knowledge as a divine gift.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God comes through purification, not study.

In this passage, Ghazali outlines the distinctive method of the Sufis: it is not about intellectual reasoning but about inner purification. He explains how the heart, once cleansed, becomes a mirror that reflects knowledge from the Preserved Tablet—a knowledge called 'laduni' (divine gift). This lesson unpacks that process.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the sequence: purification from sins, polishing by obedience, then brightness of the heart, and finally reflection from the Preserved Tablet.
The key term 'علم لدني' (laduni knowledge) is derived from the Qur'anic phrase 'من لدنا' (from Us).
2
Identify the key elements of the method
Look for the steps Ghazali mentions: (1) purification from sins, (2) polishing by acts of obedience, (3) readiness and waiting. Understand how these lead to knowledge.
3
Connect to the Qur'anic proof
Ghazali quotes two Qur'anic verses to support laduni knowledge and the promise of provision (understood as knowledge) for the God-fearing. Reflect on how these verses are used.
Verse 1: 'And We taught him from Our Own knowledge' (18:65). Verse 2: 'And whoever fears Allah, He will make for him a way out and provide for him from where he does not expect' (65:2-3).
4
Reflect on the role of human effort
Note that the method requires effort (purification, polishing) but the knowledge itself is a divine reflection – a gift. This balances action and grace.
This guards against the misconception that laduni knowledge is earned solely by effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا منهج الصوفية؛ وقد رد الأمر فيه إلى تطهير محض من جانبك وتصفية وجلاء، ثم استعداد وانتظار فقط. وإيضاح ذلك أن القلب إذا طهر من أدران المعاصي، وصقل بالطاعات، أشرقت صفحته، فانعكس عليها من اللوح المحفوظ ما شاء الله أن يكون وهذا هو العلم المعروف بالعلم اللدنى أخذاً من قوله تعالى: وآتيناه من لدنا علما. وفسروا الرزق فى قوله تعالى: ومن يتق الله يحعل له مخرجا ويرزقه من حيث لا يحتسب بالعلم من غير تعلم. طبق الغزالي هذا المنهج على نفسه حتى طهرت وصقل قلبه، كما يحدثنا هو: وانكشف لى أثناء هذه الخلوات أمور لا يمكن إحصاؤها واستقصاؤها والقدر الذي أذكره لينتفع به، أنى علمت يقيناً أن الصوفية هم السالكون لطريق الله تعالى خاصة، وأن سيرتهم أحسن السير، وطريقهم أصوب الطرق، وأخلاقهم أزكى الأخلاق، بل لو جمع عقل العقلاء، وحكمة الحكماء وعلم الواقفين على أسرار الشرع من العلماء، ليغيروا شيئاً من سيرهم وأخلاقهم ويبدلوه بما هو خير منه، لم يجدوا إليه سبيلا، فإن جميع حركاتهم وسكناتهم، في ظاهرهم و باطنهم، مقتبسة من نور مشكاة النبوة، وليس وراء نور النبوة على وجه الأرض نور يستضاء به . . وأنهم في يقظتهم يشاهدون الملائكة وأرواح الأنبياء، و يسمعون منهم أصواتاً، و يقتبسون منهم فوائد. إلى أن يقول: وكان ما كان مما لست أذكره فظن خيراً ولا تسأل عن الخبر. إذن عرف الغزالى ما كانت تتوق إليه نفسه من المعارف، وأدركها إدراكاً معه الخطأ و بذلك يكون الغزالى قد تخلص من الشك الذى يدور حول معرفة الفرقة.
This is the method of the Sufis; and the matter is reduced to pure purification from your side and cleansing and polish, then readiness and waiting only. And the explanation of that is that the heart, when purified from the filth of sins, and polished by acts of obedience, its surface becomes bright, and there is reflected upon it from the Preserved Tablet what God wills to be, and this is the knowledge known as laduni knowledge, taken from His saying: 'And We taught him from Our Own knowledge.' And they interpreted the provision in His saying: 'And whoever fears God, He will make for him a way out and provide for him from where he does not expect' as knowledge without learning. al-Ghazali applied this method to himself until his heart was purified and polished, as he tells us: 'And there were revealed to me during these seclusions things that cannot be enumerated or fully described, and the extent that I mention for benefit: that I knew with certainty that the Sufis are especially the followers of the path of God Almighty, and that their conduct is the best conduct, their path the most correct path, their character the purest character; indeed, if the intellects of the intelligent, the wisdom of the wise, and the knowledge of those who are aware of the secrets of the Law from the scholars were gathered to change anything of their conduct and character and replace it with something better, they would not find a way to do so. For all their movements and stillnesses, in their outward and inward, are borrowed from the light of the niche of prophecy, and there is no light on the face of the earth beyond the light of prophecy to be illumined by. And that in their waking state they see the angels and the spirits of the prophets, and hear voices from them, and borrow benefits from them.' Until he says: 'And what happened happened that I do not mention, so think well and do not ask about the news.' So al-Ghazali came to know what his soul had been longing for from knowledges, and he attained them with an attainment free from error [?]. And by that al-Ghazali got rid of the doubt concerning knowing the sect.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage summarizes the Sufi method as pure purification, cleansing, and then readiness and waiting. The heart, once cleansed, reflects knowledge from the Preserved Tablet—this is 'laduni knowledge' (direct divine knowledge), supported by Quranic verses. Ghazali then recounts his personal application: through seclusion, his heart was purified, and he experienced unveilings. He testifies that he knows with certainty that the Sufis are the true followers of God's path, their conduct, path, and character being the best of all. Even if all human intellect and wisdom were combined, they could not improve upon the Sufi way, because all their actions are borrowed from the light of the niche of prophethood. In their wakeful state, they see angels and prophets, hear them, and benefit from them. Ghazali refrains from detailing further but states that he attained the knowledge he sought and thus resolved his doubt about which group follows the truth.

Key Terms
تطهير
تصفية
جلاء
علم لدني
تقوى
اللوح المحفوظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Laduni knowledge can be acquired solely by human effort and study.
Correction: Ghazali emphasizes that while purification and obedience are necessary, the knowledge itself is a divine reflection from the Preserved Tablet, not a direct result of effort.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the source of laduni knowledge?
A) Intensive study of books
B) Purification of the heart and reflection from the Preserved Tablet
C) Arguments with philosophers
D) Following the consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Laduni knowledge is a divine gift that comes after inner purification, not through intellectual effort alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What specific sin are you still making excuses for that is dulling the mirror of your heart, and when will you truly purify yourself from it?
?
Are you relying more on intellectual study or on sincere obedience and patience to know God—and does your heart feel distant as a result?
?
If you truly believed that waiting in readiness after purification would bring knowledge from the Preserved Tablet, how would you change your daily spiritual routine starting today?

Ghazali's Personal Witness of the Sufi Way
Ghazali gives his personal testimony of the Sufi path's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's personal conviction that the Sufi path is superior to all others, based on his experiential knowledge during seclusion.

Core Idea
Ghazali testifies that the Sufi way is the only true path to God.

After explaining the Sufi method, Ghazali shares his own experience. He applied the method during seclusion and attained certain knowledge that the Sufis are the true followers of the prophetic path. This lesson presents his testimony, including his claims about the uniqueness of their conduct, path, and character, all derived from the light of prophecy.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's testimony
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that Ghazali speaks from personal experience ('I knew with certainty').
The passage begins with 'وانكشف لى' (and there were revealed to me).
2
Identify the claims Ghazali makes
List the specific claims: (1) Sufis are the followers of God's path, (2) their conduct is best, (3) their path is most correct, (4) their character is purest, (5) no one can improve them, (6) all their actions are from the light of prophecy.
3
Understand the evidence he offers
Ghazali supports his claims by stating that the Sufis' movements and stillness are borrowed from the niche of prophecy, and that in wakefulness they see angels and prophets.
This is an experiential claim, not a logical argument.
4
Reflect on his final statement
Ghazali intentionally withholds some details ('what happened happened') and advises the reader to think well. Consider why he does this.
This shows that not all spiritual experiences can be communicated.
5
Connect to the theme of doubt
The passage concludes that Ghazali thereby resolved his doubt about which group follows the truth. Reflect on how this testimony functions as a resolution.
This connects to the larger context of Ghazali's crisis of skepticism.

The Sufi Path to Divine Knowledge
Ghazali describes the Sufi path of purification leading to divinely-bestowed knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sufi methodology of purification and how it leads to divinely-bestowed knowledge (ilm ladunī) through the reflection of the Preserved Tablet on a purified heart.

Core Idea
Purification of the heart is the key to receiving divine knowledge directly.

Ghazali presents the Sufi methodology as a path of inner purification that culminates in knowledge from the Preserved Tablet. This passage explains how the heart, once cleansed from sins and polished with obedience, becomes a mirror reflecting divine truths, a knowledge known as 'ilm ladunī. The Quranic verses confirm that this provision comes from divine fear and trust.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Ghazali's concise style and use of Quranic references.
2
Identify the two aspects of the Sufi path
Look for the two elements Ghazali mentions: purification (tathīr) and readiness (isti'dād). Understand how they relate to each other.
Purification is active on your part; readiness is passive.
3
Understand the metaphor of the heart as a mirror
Ghazali compares the purified heart to a polished surface that reflects knowledge from the Preserved Tablet. Grasp this metaphor deeply.
The heart does not create knowledge but receives it.
4
Connect to the Quranic evidence
Examine the two Quranic verses Ghazali cites: 18:65 about 'ilm ladunī and 65:2-3 about provision. Understand how they support his argument.
The Sufis interpret 'provision' as knowledge without learning.
5
Reflect on the concept of ilm ladunī
Ask yourself: What does this mean for the seeker? Is this knowledge available to anyone? How does it differ from acquired knowledge?
This is a key concept in Sufi epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا منهج الصوفية ؛ وقد رد الأمر فيه إلى تطهير محض من جانبك وتصفية وجلاء ، ثم استعداد وانتظار فقط ] وإيضاح ذلك أن القلب إذا طهر من أدران المعاصي ، وصقل بالطاعات ، أشرقت صفحته ، فانعكس عليها من اللوح المحفوظ ما شاء الله أن يكون وهذا هو العلم المعروف بالعلم اللدنى أخذاً من قوله تعالى : وآتيناه من لدنا علما » وفسروا الرزق فى قوله تعالى : ومن يتق الله يحعل له مخرجا ويرزقه من حيث لا يحتسب » بالعلم من غير تعلم .
This is the methodology of the Sufis; the matter is reduced to pure purification on your part, and refinement and polishing, then readiness and waiting only. The explanation of that is that when the heart is purified from the filth of sins and polished with acts of obedience, its surface shines, and there is reflected upon it from the Preserved Tablet whatever God wills to be. And this is the knowledge known as 'ilm ladunī (divinely-bestowed knowledge), taken from His statement: 'And We gave him from Us knowledge.' And they interpreted the provision in His statement: 'And whoever fears God, He will make for him a way out and provide for him from where he does not expect' as knowledge without learning.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage explains the Sufi method of acquiring knowledge. Ghazali states that the Sufi path reduces to two aspects: purification (tathīr) and refinement (tasfiya) on the part of the seeker, followed by readiness and waiting. He then elaborates: when the heart is purified from sins and polished with acts of obedience, its surface shines, and knowledge from the Preserved Tablet (al-lawḥ al-mahfūẓ) is reflected onto it. This is the knowledge called 'ilm ladunī, referencing the Qur'anic verse about Khidr: 'And We gave him from Us knowledge' (Q. 18:65). He also notes that the scholars have interpreted the 'provision' in the verse 'And whoever fears God... He will provide for him from where he does not expect' (Q. 65:2-3) as this kind of knowledge acquired without learning.

Key Terms
منهج الصوفية
تطهير
تصفية
اللوح المحفوظ
العلم اللدني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Sufi path requires no effort, only waiting for divine knowledge to come.
Correction: Ghazali emphasizes active purification and refinement (tathīr and tasfiya) on the part of the seeker, and only then readiness and waiting. Effort is essential before reception.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the source of 'ilm ladunī (divinely-bestowed knowledge)?
A) Intensive study of books
B) Purification of the heart leading to reflection from the Preserved Tablet
C) Direct teaching by a human teacher
D) Logical deduction

Key Takeaway
The Sufi path to divine knowledge is through inner purification and polishing, which allows the heart to reflect knowledge from the Preserved Tablet, a gift from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you actively purifying your heart from sins and polishing it with obedience, or are you relying solely on your intellect to acquire knowledge? What specific sin or habit are you holding onto that blocks the reflection of divine knowledge?
?
The passage says the heart shines like a mirror to receive knowledge from the Preserved Tablet. Is your heart clouded by negligence or worldly attachments? What will you do today to polish it?
?
You claim to seek truth, but are you willing to wait upon Allah's gift, or do you insist on acquiring knowledge only through your own effort? Which sin has dimmed your heart's receptivity, and when will you repent from it?

Ghazali's Testimony: The Superiority of the Sufi Path
Ghazali states his certainty that the Sufis are the true path, with conduct derived from prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's affirmation of the Sufi path as the most excellent and why he considers it derived from the light of prophethood.

Core Idea
The Sufi path is the best because it is drawn directly from the light of prophethood.

In this passage from his spiritual autobiography, Ghazali shares the fruits of his retreats and his certainty about the Sufi way. He claims that all aspects of their conduct are superior to any other path, deriving from the very source of divine guidance. This lesson examines his reasoning and the basis for his unqualified endorsement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow of Ghazali's testimony.
2
Identify the superlative claims
Ghazali uses strong language: 'best conduct', 'most correct path', 'purest manners'. Find these and understand the hypothetical challenge he presents.
3
Understand the source of superiority
The key is the phrase 'light of the niche of prophethood'. Reflect on why this makes the Sufi path unchangeable.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Consider that Ghazali is not just praising but giving a rationale based on divine light. This is a foundational claim for his Sufi epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طبق الغزالي هذا المنهج على نفسه حتى طهرت وصقل قلبه ، كما يحدثنا هو 1 وانكشف لى أثناء هذه الخلوات أمور لا يمكن إحصاؤها واستقصاؤها والقدر الذي أذكره لينتفع به ، أنى علمت يقيناً أن الصوفية هم السالكون لطريق الله تعالى خاصة ، وأن سيرتهم أحسن السير ، وطريقهم أصوب الطرق ، وأخلاقهم أزكى الأخلاق ، بل لو جمع عقل العقلاء ، وحكمة الحكماء وعلم الواقفين على أسرار الشرع من العلماء ، ليغيروا شيئاً من سيرهم وأخلاقهم ويبدلوه بما هو خير منه ، لم يجدوا إليه سبيلا ، فإن جميع حركاتهم وسكناتهم، في ظاهرهم و باطنهم ، مقتبسة من نور مشكاة النبوة، وليس وراء نور النبوة على وجه الأرض نور يستضاء به . . وأنهم في يقظتهم يشاهدون الملائكة وأرواح الأنبياء ، و يسمعون منهم أصواتاً ، و يقتبسون منهم فوائد ] يأمن إلى أن يقول : وكان ما كان مما لست أذكره فظن خيراً ولا تسأل عن الخبر ]
Al-Ghazali applied this methodology to himself until his heart was purified and polished, as he himself tells us. And there was disclosed to me during these retreats matters that cannot be enumerated or fully surveyed. But the amount that I will mention so that benefit may be derived from it is that I knew with certainty that the Sufis are the ones who tread the path of God Most High specifically, and that their conduct is the best conduct, their path the most correct path, and their manners the purest manners. Indeed, if the intellect of the intelligent, the wisdom of the wise, and the knowledge of those who know the secrets of the Law from among the scholars were gathered together to change something of their conduct and manners and replace it with something better, they would find no way to do so. For all their movements and stillnesses, in their outward and inward, are borrowed from the light of the niche of prophethood, and there is no light on the face of the earth beyond the light of prophethood by which one may be illuminated... And that in their wakefulness they see the angels and the souls of the prophets, and they hear sounds from them, and they derive benefits from them. [؟] until he said: 'And what happened was what I do not mention, so think well and do not ask about the news.'
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's personal testimony about his experience after applying the Sufi method. He states that during retreats (khalawāt) matters were disclosed to him beyond enumeration. He then recounts his certainty that the Sufis are the true followers of God's path. Their conduct, path, and manners are superior to all others; no amount of combined intelligence and wisdom could improve them. The reason is that all their actions, both outward and inward, are derived from the light of the niche (mishkāt) of prophethood, which is the ultimate source of illumination. He further claims that in their wakefulness they witness angels and the souls of prophets, hear sounds, and derive benefits. He concludes with the enigmatic statement: 'What happened was what I do not mention, so think well and do not ask about the news.' This indicates an ineffable mystical experience.

مشكاة النبوة
خلوات

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If the combined wisdom of all scholars cannot change the conduct of the Sufis, why do you still feel comfortable picking and choosing which aspects of the prophetic way to follow? Which habit or belief do you hold that would need to be corrected if you truly believed this passage?

Mystical Experiences: Witnessing Angels and Prophets
Ghazali describes the Sufis' waking visions of angels and prophets and their benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's description of the Sufis' mystical experiences, including witnessing angels and prophets while awake.

Core Idea
Sufis in their wakefulness directly witness angels and the souls of prophets.

Ghazali continues his testimony by revealing the extraordinary experiences of the Sufis during their spiritual journeys. He claims that they see and hear angels and prophets, deriving knowledge from them. His enigmatic concluding remark hints at an even greater mystery beyond description.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify what the Sufis witness
Look at the Arabic and translation: the Sufis see angels and the souls of prophets while awake.
2
Note the sensory modalities
They see, hear sounds, and derive benefits. This is a multi-sensory experience, not just intellectual.
3
Consider the wakefulness aspect
Ghazali emphasizes 'in their wakefulness' (fi yaqazatihim), distinguishing it from dreams or meditative states.
4
Reflect on the enigmatic conclusion
The final sentence suggests an ineffable experience that Ghazali chooses not to disclose. Think about why he might hold back.

The Reliability of Ghazali's Post-Enlightenment Writings
Ghazali asserts that only his post-transformation writings are reliable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considered only his writings after spiritual transformation to be reliable sources for his final views.

Core Idea
Ghazali's later works alone represent his final, error-free views.

Imam al-Ghazali underwent a profound spiritual crisis and transformation, after which he claimed to have attained certain knowledge free from error. In this passage, he sets a critical methodological rule: only his post-enlightenment writings should be taken as authoritative. This lesson examines that claim and its implications for studying Ghazali's thought.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's own statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the emphasis on 'from this time only'.
2
Understand the two doubts he overcame
Ghazali mentions overcoming doubt about the saved sect and the scales of truth. Understand what these refer to.
Scholars interpret 'scales of truth' as his epistemological methodology.
3
Apply the rule to Ghazali's corpus
Consider how this changes your approach to Ghazali's earlier works like the Tahafut. Should they be read differently?
This is a key point for Islamic philosophy research.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذن عرف الغزالى ما كانت تتوق إليه نفسه من المعارف ، وأدركها إدراكاً معه الخطأ . و بذلك يكون الغزالى قد تخلص من الشك الذى يدور حول معرفة الفرقة الناجية بعد أن تخلص من الشك الذى يدور حول موازين الحقيقة . ومنذ هذا الوقت فقط يمكن استمداد مؤلفاته لتكون مصدراً يصور لنا آراءه وأفكاره ونظر ياته . أما ما قبل ذلك التاريخ فلا على أنه لا ينبغى الوثوق بكل مؤلفاته فى هذه الفترة لتكون مستمداً لأفكاره؛
Thus Ghazali came to know what his soul yearned for of knowledge, and he grasped it with an understanding free from error. And thereby Ghazali had overcome the doubt concerning knowledge of the saved sect after having overcome the doubt concerning the scales of truth. And from this time only can his works be relied upon as a source depicting his views, ideas, and theories. As for before that date, one should not trust all his works from that period to be a source for his ideas.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is an important methodological note. Ghazali states that after his spiritual transformation, he attained knowledge free from error. He overcame doubt regarding the saved sect (al-firqa al-nājiya) as well as doubt regarding the scales of truth (mawāzīn al-ḥaqīqa). Consequently, only from this point onward can his writings be considered reliable sources for his final views and ideas. Before this date, his works from that period should not be fully trusted. This serves as a crucial guideline for interpreting Ghazali's corpus.

Key Terms
الفرقة الناجية
موازين الحقيقة
الشك
المعارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All of Ghazali's works are equally authoritative and represent his final views.
Correction: Ghazali himself states that only his post-enlightenment works should be trusted, and earlier writings should be used with caution.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, when did his works become reliable sources for his ideas?
A) From the beginning of his writing career
B) Only from the time he wrote the Tahafut
C) Only after he overcame doubt and attained certain knowledge
D) Always, as he was a great scholar

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's post-enlightenment writings alone are authoritative; his earlier works do not necessarily reflect his final position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'scales of truth' (موازين الحقيقة) are you using to judge what is true? Have you ever honestly questioned whether your current criteria for knowledge are reliable, or are you content with inherited assumptions?
?
Ghazali had to overcome two kinds of doubt: one about method and one about ultimate salvation. Are there areas in your faith where you are still living with unresolved doubt? What would it take for you to seek the same kind of certainty—not comfortable ignorance, but hard-won conviction?
?
If your own earlier writings or opinions were examined, would you say they reflect your final, considered beliefs? Where in your life do you need to admit you were wrong and start anew, like Ghazali did after his transformation?

The Three Types of People and Their Teaching Methods
Ghazali classifies people into three types and assigns a distinct method of address to each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of people into three types and the corresponding methods he prescribes for teaching them.

Core Idea
One method does not fit all: Ghazali tailors teaching to the learner's type.

In this passage, Ghazali presents his pedagogical framework based on the diversity of human intellects. He argues that religion is easy and cannot impose a single approach on all people. Consequently, he divides people into three categories—commoners, elite, and dialecticians—and assigns to each a distinct method of address: exhortation, wisdom, and debate. This classification reflects his deep insight into human psychology and is rooted in Qur'anic guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then follow the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the context.
The passage is a continuous argument; pay attention to how it builds from premise to classification to methods.
2
Identify the three types of people
Find the three categories Ghazali lists: commoners (al-ʻawāmm), elite (al-khawāṣṣ), and the intermediate group of dialecticians (ahl al-jadal). Note what characterizes each.
The elite have three specific qualities listed later.
3
Examine the three qualities of the elite
Ghazali details three prerequisites for the elite to benefit from instruction: innate intelligence, freedom from blind following, and trust in the teacher. Understand why each matters.
These qualities are not acquired but are gifts or personal dispositions.
4
Match the methods to the types
Identify the method assigned to each group: exhortation (mawʻiẓa) for commoners, wisdom (ḥikma) for the elite, and debate (mujādala) for the dialecticians. Notice the Qur'anic basis.
The verse reference (16:125) combines these three methods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن للغزالى بصدد تصوير الحقيقة وإعلانها رأياً جديراً بالاعتبار ، ذلك أنه أن الناس متفاوتون فى الاستعدادات والمدارك . والدين فى نظر الغزالى سمح سهل : لا يمكن أن ينظر إلى الناس جميعاً ، مع اختلاف مداركهم واستعداداتهم نظرة واحدة ، فيكلف كليل الذهن فوق طاقته من المباحث النظرية ، أو يحظر على الطلعة المتوثب ، إشباع رغباته ، بالبحث والنظر ولذلك يقول (۱) : الناس ثلاثة أصناف : ۱ - عوام ، وهم أهل السلامة البله ۲ - خواص ، وهم أهل الذكاء والبصيرة - و يتولد بينهم طائفة هم أهل الجدل . . أما الخواص فإنى أعالجهم بأن أعلمهم الموازين القسط ، وكيفية الوزن بها ، فيرتفع الخلاف بينهم على قرب ، وهؤلاء قوم اجتمع فيهم ثلاث خصال : إحداها - القريحة النافذة ، والفطنة القوية ، وهذه عطية فطرية ، وغريزة جبلية ، لا يمكن كسبها الثانية - خلو باطنهم من تقليد وتعصب لمذهب موروث مسموع ، فإن المقلد لا يصغى ، والبليد و إن أصغى فلا يفهم . الثالثة – أن يعتقد في أنى من أهل البصيرة بالميزان، ومن لم يؤمن بأنك من أهل الحساب ، لا يمكنه أن يتعلم منك . وأما البله وهم جميع العوام ، وهؤلاء هم الذين ليس لهم فطنة لفهم الحقائق ، فأدعو هؤلاء إلى الله بالموعظة ، كما أدعو أهل البصيرة بالحكمة ، وأدعو أهل الشغب بالمجادلة . وقد جمع الله سبحانه وتعالى هذه الثلاثة في آية واحدة حيث قال :
Because al-Ghazali, with regard to depicting and declaring the truth, holds an opinion worthy of consideration, namely that people differ in their preparedness and perceptions. Religion in Ghazali's view is tolerant and easy: it is not possible to look at all people, despite the difference in their perceptions and preparedness, with a single view, such that one burdens the dull-witted beyond his capacity with theoretical investigations, or prohibits the eager aspirant from satisfying his desires through research and reflection. Therefore he says: People are three types: 1) the commoners, who are people of simple-minded safety; 2) the elite, who are people of intelligence and insight; and there emerges among them a group who are people of dialectic. As for the elite, I treat them by teaching them the just scales and the method of weighing with them, so that disagreement among them quickly resolves. These are a people who possess three qualities: first, penetrating acumen and strong intelligence—this is a natural gift and innate disposition that cannot be acquired; second, their inner being is free from blind adherence and fanaticism for an inherited doctrine heard [from others], for the blind follower does not listen, and the dull-witted, even if he listens, does not understand; third, that he believes that I am one of those who possess insight into the balance, and whoever does not believe that you are among the people of calculation cannot learn from you. As for the simpletons—and they are all the commoners—they are those who have no acumen for understanding realities, so I call these to God through exhortation (al-mawʻiẓa), just as I call the people of insight through wisdom (al-ḥikma), and I call the people of contentiousness through debate (al-mujādala). And God the Exalted and Glorious has combined these three in one verse where He said: [citation follows].
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's pedagogical classification. He states that people differ in preparedness (istiʻdādāt) and perceptions (madārik). Religion is easy, so one should not impose the same method on all. He then divides people into three types: (1) the commoners (al-ʻawāmm) – simple-minded; (2) the elite (al-khawāṣṣ) – intelligent and insightful, and a subgroup that emerges among them, the people of dialectic (ahl al-jadal). For the elite, he teaches them the 'just scales' (al-mawāzīn al-qisṭ) and how to weigh, leading to quick resolution of disagreements. They possess three qualities: innate intelligence, freedom from blind following, and trust in the teacher's expertise. For the simpletons, he uses exhortation (al-mawʻiẓa); for the people of insight (ahl al-baṣīra), he uses wisdom (al-ḥikma); and for the quarrelsome (ahl al-shaghab), he uses debate (al-mujādala). He cites that these three methods are combined in one Qur'anic verse (likely 16:125).

Key Terms
العوام
الخواص
أهل الجدل
الموازين القسط
الموعظة
الحكمة
المجادلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what method is appropriate for the commoners (al-ʻawāmm)?
A) Teaching the just scales
B) Exhortation (al-mawʻiẓa)
C) Debate (al-mujādala)
D) Philosophical arguments

Key Takeaway
Ghazali teaches that effective religious instruction must match the learner's intellectual capacity, using exhortation for the simple, wisdom for the insightful, and debate for the contentious.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you teach or advise someone, do you first consider their intellectual capacity and background? Or do you use a one-size-fits-all approach? Think of a specific person you recently tried to guide—did you use exhortation, wisdom, or debate, and was it effective? Commit to adjusting your method next time based on what this lesson teaches.
?
Examine yourself: Do you possess the three qualities of an elite learner, especially freedom from blind imitation of your inherited madhhab or popular opinions? If not, are you willing to honestly seek the truth even if it means abandoning comfortable positions? What step will you take this week to cultivate that freedom?
?
When you find yourself in a religious debate, do you aim to win or to weigh arguments with the just scales? Recall a recent argument—did you listen and seek truth, or did you defend your position at all costs? Repent if necessary, and resolve to debate with the etiquette Ghazali implies: wisdom, exhortation, and argument in the best way.

The Three Classes of People
Ghazali classifies people into three types based on intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's classification of people into three types based on their intellectual capacities and why a single approach to religious instruction is ineffective.

Core Idea
People vary in intellect; religion is flexible and requires tailored approaches.

Al-Ghazali begins by asserting a fundamental principle: people are not equal in their mental abilities. Religion, being tolerant and easy, cannot impose the same demands on everyone. In this passage, he introduces his famous threefold classification of people, which forms the basis for his entire method of inviting to the truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise
Read the opening lines where al-Ghazali states that people differ in aptitudes and that religion is easy. Notice that he rejects a uniform approach.
2
Identify the three classes
Find the three categories: common people (awamm), elite (khawass), and the disputatious (ahl al-jadal). Understand the brief characterization of each.
The disputatious are described as emerging between the other two.
3
Reflect on the rationale
Ask yourself why al-Ghazali starts with this classification. How does it relate to the idea that religion is 'tolerant and easy'?
This sets the stage for the specific methods that follow.

لأن للغزالى بصدد تصوير الحقيقة وإعلانها رأياً جديراً بالاعتبار ، ذلك أنه أن الناس متفاوتون في الاستعدادات والمدارك . والدين في نظر الغزالى سمح سهل : لا يمكن أن ينظر إلى الناس جميعاً ، مع اختلاف مداركهم واستعداداتهم نظرة واحدة ، فيكلف كليل الذهن فوق طاقته من المباحث النظرية ، أو يحظر على الطلعة المتوثب ، إشباع رغباته ، بالبحث والنظر ولذلك يقول (۱) : الناس ثلاثة أصناف : ۱ - عوام ، وهم أهل السلامة البله ۲ - خواص ، وهم أهل الذكاء والبصيرة - و يتولد بينهم طائفة هم أهل الجدل . أما الخواص فإنى أعالجهم بأن أعلمهم الموازين القسط ، وكيفية الوزن بها ، فيرتفع الخلاف بينهم على قرب ، وهؤلاء قوم اجتمع فيهم ثلاث خصال : إحداها - القريحة النافذة ، والفطنة القوية ، وهذه عطية فطرية ، وغريزة جبلية ، لا يمكن كسبها الثانية - خلو باطنهم من تقليد وتعصب لمذهب موروث مسموع ، فإن المقلد لا يصغى ، والبليد و إن أصغى فلا يفهم . الثالثة – أن يعتقد في أنى من أهل البصيرة بالميزان، ومن لم يؤمن بأنك من أهل الحساب ، لا يمكنه أن يتعلم منك . وأما البله وهم جميع العوام ، وهؤلاء هم الذين ليس لهم فطنة لفهم الحقائق ، فأدعو هؤلاء إلى الله بالموعظة ، كما أدعو أهل البصيرة بالحكمة ، وأدعو أهل الشغب بالمجادلة . وقد جمع الله سبحانه وتعالى هذه الثلاثة في آية واحدة حيث قال : (۱) القسطاس المستقيم ص ٨٦ مطبعة التقدم أدع إلى سبيل ربك بالحكمة والموعظة الحسنة وجادلهم بالتي هي أحسن] . فعلم أن المدعو إلى الله تعالى بالحكمة قوم ، و بالموعظة قوم ، وبالمجادلة قوم ؛ فإن الحكمة إن غذى بها أهل الموعظة أضرت بهم كما تضر بالطفل الرضيع التغذية بلحم الطير ، وأن المجادلة إن استعملت مع أهل الحكمة اشمأزوا منها ، كما يشمئز طبع الرجل القوى من الارتضاع بلبن الآدمى ، وأن من استعمل الجدال مع أهل الجدال لا بالطريق الأحسن ، كما تعلم من القرآن ، كان كمن غذى البدوى بخبز البر ، وهو لم يألف إلا التمر ، أو البلدى بالتمر ، وهو لم يألف إلا البر وأما أهل الجدل فهم طائفة فيهم كياسة ، ترقوا بها عن العوام ، ولكن كياستهم ناقصة ؛ إذ كانت الفطرة كاملة ، ولكن في باطنهم خبث وعناد ، وتعصب وتقليد ؛ فذلك يمنعهم عن إدراك الحق ، وتكون هذه الصفات أكنة على قلو بهم أن يفقهوه وفي آذانهم وقراً . و إنى أدعوهم بالتلطف إلى الحق ، وأعنى بالتلطف ألا أتعصب عليهم ولا أعنفهم ، ولكن أرفق وأجادل بالتي هي أحسن ] . وفى هذا ما يشير إلى أن الغزالى يقدم للناس ألواناً مختلفة من المعرفة ، وإلى أن من الناس طائفة تخفى عليهم الحقيقة لعدم طاقتهم إياها ، لهذا فهو يقف منهم الموقف الذي يرى أن الشرع أمر به ، وهو مخاطبة الناس على قدر عقولهم وكثيراً ما ردد في كتبه ذلك الأثر خاطبوا الناس على قدر عقولهم أتريدون أن يكذب الله ورسوله ؟ ]
Because al-Ghazali, in the context of portraying and declaring the truth, holds an opinion worthy of consideration: that people vary in aptitudes and perceptions. Religion in al-Ghazali's view is tolerant and easy: it is not possible to look at all people, with the difference in their perceptions and aptitudes, with one view, so as to burden the dull-minded beyond his capacity with theoretical investigations, or to prohibit the eager aspirant from satisfying his desires through research and reflection. Hence he says: People are of three classes: 1 - the common people, who are the people of safety and simplicity; 2 - the elite, who are the people of intelligence and insight; and between them arises a group who are the people of disputation. As for the elite, I treat them by teaching them the just balances and the manner of weighing with them, so that disagreement among them will soon cease. These are people in whom three characteristics are combined: first, penetrating intellect and strong acumen, and this is a gifted faculty and innate disposition that cannot be acquired; second, their inner being is free from imitation and fanaticism for a heard inherited madhhab (school), for the imitator does not listen, and the stupid even if he listens does not understand; third, that he believes that I am among the people of insight concerning the balance, and whoever does not have faith that you are among the people of calculation cannot learn from you. As for the simpletons, who are all the common people, these are those who have no acumen to understand the truths, so I call these to God through admonition, just as I call the people of insight through wisdom, and I call the people of contentiousness through disputation. And Allah, exalted and high, has gathered these three in one verse where He said: (1) Al-Qistas al-Mustaqim, p.86, Matba'at al-Taqaddum; 'Call to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good admonition and dispute with them in the way that is best.' So it is known that those called to Allah Most High through wisdom are a people, through admonition are a people, and through disputation are a people. For if wisdom is fed to the people of admonition, it harms them just as feeding a suckling child with bird meat harms him; and if disputation is used with the people of wisdom, they feel disgusted from it, just as the nature of a strong man feels disgust at suckling from human milk; and whoever uses disputation with the people of disputation not in the best way, as you learn from the Quran, is like one who feeds a Bedouin with wheat bread when he is used only to dates, or a townsman with dates when he is used only to wheat. As for the people of disputation, they are a group possessing cleverness by which they have risen above the common people, but their cleverness is deficient; for their innate disposition is complete, but within them is malice, stubbornness, fanaticism, and imitation; that prevents them from perceiving the truth, and these attributes are covers upon their hearts that they should understand it, and in their ears is deafness. And I call them with gentleness to the truth, and I mean by gentleness that I do not act fanatically against them nor treat them harshly, but I am gentle and dispute in the way that is best. And in this is an indication that al-Ghazali presents different colors of knowledge to people, and that among people is a group for whom the truth is hidden due to their lack of ability for it; hence he takes with them the stance that he sees the Law commanded, which is addressing people according to their intellects; and he frequently repeated in his books that effect: 'Address people according to their intellects; do you want that Allah and His messenger should be belied?'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a classificatory and pedagogical exposition by al-Ghazali on how to invite people to the truth. It begins with the premise that people vary in their intellectual capacities and that religion is flexible, so no single approach suits all. Al-Ghazali then divides people into three categories: (1) the common people (al-'awamm), who are simple and safe; (2) the elite (al-khawass), who are intelligent and insightful; and (3) the disputatious (ahl al-jadal), who are clever but flawed by obstinacy. For the elite, he advocates teaching the 'just balances' (al-mawazin al-qist) – a reference to his logical method in Al-Qistas al-Mustaqim – and lists three qualities necessary for them: innate sharpness, freedom from blind imitation, and trust in the teacher's expertise. For the common people, he prescribes gentle admonition (al-maw'izah). For the disputatious, he uses disputation (al-mujadalah) but in the best manner, avoiding harshness. He cites Quran 16:125 as scriptural support for these three methods. Al-Ghazali then employs vivid metaphors: feeding wisdom to those suited only for admonition harms like feeding a baby bird meat; disputation disgusts the wise like an adult disgusted by human milk; and improper disputation with the disputatious is like feeding a Bedouin wheat bread when he is used to dates. He expands on the characteristics of the disputatious: they have some cleverness but are impeded by malice, stubbornness, fanaticism, and imitation, which act as veils on their hearts. He concludes by affirming that this graded approach is enjoined by the Law, citing the principle of addressing people according to their intellects.

Key Terms
عوام
خواص
أهل الجدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ghazali believes everyone should receive the same level of religious instruction.
Correction: Al-Ghazali explicitly states that people vary and that religion is easy, so one cannot treat all alike.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, how many classes of people does he identify?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali classifies people into three intellectual types, setting the stage for tailored methods of invitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of al-Ghazali's three categories do you honestly see yourself in today? Does that alignment bring you comfort or stir a desire to move towards the elite? What specific knowledge or habit will you pursue this week to deepen your understanding?
?
Have you ever looked down on someone who is content with simple faith, dismissing them as 'just common'? How does al-Ghazali's positive description of the common people as 'people of safety and simplicity' challenge your attitude? What can you learn from their contentment?
?
When you engage in religious discussions, do you seek truth or victory? Reflect on a recent debate: was your motivation to clarify or to win? How might your love for argument have violated the 'tolerant and easy' spirit of religion that al-Ghazali emphasizes? What concrete change will you make in your next discussion?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Teaching the Elite: The Method of Balances
Ghazali prescribes the method of balances for the elite with three conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's method for teaching the elite: using the 'just balances' and the three prerequisites for a student to benefit from this method.

Core Idea
The elite require the just balances and must possess three qualities: intelligence, freedom from imitation, and trust in the teacher.

Having classified people, al-Ghazali now turns to the elite (khawass). He prescribes a unique method: teaching them the 'just balances' (al-mawazin al-qist), a reference to his logical framework. But not everyone among the elite can benefit equally — they must meet three conditions that ensure they are ready to learn.

Guided Path
1
Read the method for the elite
Read the passage describing how al-Ghazali treats the elite. Note that he teaches them 'the just balances and how to weigh with them'.
This is the method of logical reasoning from Al-Qistas al-Mustaqim.
2
List the three conditions
Identify the three characteristics that the elite student must have: sharp intellect, freedom from blind imitation, and belief in the teacher's expertise.
These are prerequisites for effective learning.
3
Understand the rationale
Reflect on why each condition is necessary. Why must they be free from imitation? Why must they trust the teacher?
These conditions ensure the student can engage with rational argument.

Commentary
This passage is a classificatory and pedagogical exposition by al-Ghazali on how to invite people to the truth. It begins with the premise that people vary in their intellectual capacities and that religion is flexible, so no single approach suits all. He concludes by affirming that this graded approach is enjoined by the Law, citing the principle of addressing people according to their intellects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three conditions do you find most difficult to fulfill in your own pursuit of knowledge? Be honest: is it your intellect, your freedom from blind following, or your trust in a teacher? What will you do about it this week?
?
Consider a belief you hold strongly because you inherited it from family or community. Can you trace it to a rational balance, or is it mere taqlid? If you discovered it was blind imitation, would you have the courage to re-examine it?
?
The third condition requires faith in the teacher's expertise. Is there a teacher in your life whose guidance you resist because of pride or doubt? How might that resistance be blocking your growth? Commit to one step toward softening your heart toward them.

Method of Learning
beginner
7 min
Methods for Commoners and the Disputatious
Ghazali prescribes admonition for commoners and disputation for the disputatious, with Quranic proof and metaphors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's methods for addressing common people (admonition) and the disputatious (disputation), along with the scriptural basis and vivid metaphors illustrating why methods must match the audience.

Core Idea
Commoners need gentle admonition; the disputatious need careful disputation; misapplying methods causes harm.

After discussing the elite, al-Ghazali turns to the other two groups. He prescribes gentle admonition (maw'izah) for common people and disputation (mujadalah) for the disputatious, but only in the best manner. He then cites the Quranic verse commanding these three methods and provides striking metaphors to show why using the wrong method is counterproductive.

Guided Path
1
Read the methods for commoners and disputatious
Read the passage where al-Ghazali says he calls commoners with admonition and the disputatious with disputation. Notice the reference to Quran 16:125.
2
Examine the metaphors
Al-Ghazali uses three metaphors: feeding wisdom to admonition-people like feeding bird meat to an infant; using disputation with the wise like an adult disgusted by human milk; and improper disputation with the disputatious like feeding a Bedouin wheat bread or a townsman dates. Understand each.
These illustrate the harm of mismatched methods.
3
Reflect on the disputatious
Read the description of the disputatious: they have cleverness but are hindered by malice, stubbornness, fanaticism, and imitation. Note how al-Ghazali deals with them gently.
4
Connect to the broader principle
Al-Ghazali concludes with the principle of addressing people according to their intellects. Reflect on how this ties the whole classification together.
This is a key pedagogical maxim.

لأن للغزالى بصدد تصوير الحقيقة وإعلانها رأياً جديراً بالاعتبار ، ذلك أنه أن الناس متفاوتون في الاستعدادات والمدارك . والدين في نظر الغزالى سمح سهل : لا يمكن أن ينظر إلى الناس جميعاً ، مع اختلاف مداركهم واستعداداتهم نظرة واحدة ، فيكلف كليل الذهن فوق طاقته من المباحث النظرية ، أو يحظر على الطلعة المتوثب ، إشباع رغباته ، بالبحث والنظر ولذلك يقول (۱) : الناس ثلاثة أصناف : ۱ - عوام ، وهم أهل السلامة البله ۲ - خواص ، وهم أهل الذكاء والبصيرة - و يتولد بينهم طائفة هم أهل الجدل . أما الخواص فإنى أعالجهم بأن أعلمهم الموازين القسط ، وكيفية الوزن بها ، فيرتفع الخلاف بينهم على قرب ، وهؤلاء قوم اجتمع فيهم ثلاث خصال : إحداها - القريحة النافذة ، والفطنة القوية ، وهذه عطية فطرية ، وغريزة جبلية ، لا يمكن كسبها الثانية - خلو باطنهم من تقليد وتعصب لمذهب موروث مسموع ، فإن المقلد لا يصغى ، والبليد و إن أصغى فلا يفهم . الثالثة – أن يعتقد في أنى من أهل البصيرة بالميزان، ومن لم يؤمن بأنك من أهل الحساب ، لا يمكنه أن يتعلم منك . وأما البله وهم جميع العوام ، وهؤلاء هم الذين ليس لهم فطنة لفهم الحقائق ، فأدعو هؤلاء إلى الله بالموعظة ، كما أدعو أهل البصيرة بالحكمة ، وأدعو أهل الشغب بالمجادلة . وقد جمع الله سبحانه وتعالى هذه الثلاثة في آية واحدة حيث قال : (۱) القسطاس المستقيم ص ٨٦ مطبعة التقدم أدع إلى سبيل ربك بالحكمة والموعظة الحسنة وجادلهم بالتي هي أحسن] . فعلم أن المدعو إلى الله تعالى بالحكمة قوم ، و بالموعظة قوم ، وبالمجادلة قوم ؛ فإن الحكمة إن غذى بها أهل الموعظة أضرت بهم كما تضر بالطفل الرضيع التغذية بلحم الطير ، وأن المجادلة إن استعملت مع أهل الحكمة اشمأزوا منها ، كما يشمئز طبع الرجل القوى من الارتضاع بلبن الآدمى ، وأن من استعمل الجدال مع أهل الجدال لا بالطريق الأحسن ، كما تعلم من القرآن ، كان كمن غذى البدوى بخبز البر ، وهو لم يألف إلا التمر ، أو البلدى بالتمر ، وهو لم يألف إلا البر وأما أهل الجدل فهم طائفة فيهم كياسة ، ترقوا بها عن العوام ، ولكن كياستهم ناقصة ؛ إذ كانت الفطرة كاملة ، ولكن في باطنهم خبث وعناد ، وتعصب وتقليد ؛ فذلك يمنعهم عن إدراك الحق ، وتكون هذه الصفات أكنة على قلو بهم أن يفقهوه وفي آذانهم وقراً . و إنى أدعوهم بالتلطف إلى الحق ، وأعنى بالتلطف ألا أتعصب عليهم ولا أعنفهم ، ولكن أرفق وأجادل بالتي هي أحسن ] . وفى هذا ما يشير إلى أن الغزالى يقدم للناس ألواناً مختلفة من المعرفة ، وإلى أن من الناس طائفة تخفى عليهم الحقيقة لعدم طاقتهم إياها ، لهذا فهو يقف منهم الموقف الذي يرى أن الشرع أمر به ، وهو مخاطبة الناس على قدر عقولهم وكثيراً ما ردد في كتبه ذلك الأثر خاطبوا الناس على قدر عقولهم أتريدون أن يكذب الله ورسوله ؟ ]
Because al-Ghazali, in the context of portraying and declaring the truth, holds an opinion worthy of consideration: that people vary in aptitudes and perceptions. Religion in al-Ghazali's view is tolerant and easy: it is not possible to look at all people, with the difference in their perceptions and aptitudes, with one view, so as to burden the dull-minded beyond his capacity with theoretical investigations, or to prohibit the eager aspirant from satisfying his desires through research and reflection. Hence he says: People are of three classes: 1 - the common people, who are the people of safety and simplicity; 2 - the elite, who are the people of intelligence and insight; and between them arises a group who are the people of disputation. As for the elite, I treat them by teaching them the just balances and the manner of weighing with them, so that disagreement among them will soon cease. These are people in whom three characteristics are combined: first, penetrating intellect and strong acumen, and this is a gifted faculty and innate disposition that cannot be acquired; second, their inner being is free from imitation and fanaticism for a heard inherited madhhab (school), for the imitator does not listen, and the stupid even if he listens does not understand; third, that he believes that I am among the people of insight concerning the balance, and whoever does not have faith that you are among the people of calculation cannot learn from you. As for the simpletons, who are all the common people, these are those who have no acumen to understand the truths, so I call these to God through admonition, just as I call the people of insight through wisdom, and I call the people of contentiousness through disputation. And Allah, exalted and high, has gathered these three in one verse where He said: (1) Al-Qistas al-Mustaqim, p.86, Matba'at al-Taqaddum; 'Call to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good admonition and dispute with them in the way that is best.' So it is known that those called to Allah Most High through wisdom are a people, through admonition are a people, and through disputation are a people. For if wisdom is fed to the people of admonition, it harms them just as feeding a suckling child with bird meat harms him; and if disputation is used with the people of wisdom, they feel disgusted from it, just as the nature of a strong man feels disgust at suckling from human milk; and whoever uses disputation with the people of disputation not in the best way, as you learn from the Quran, is like one who feeds a Bedouin with wheat bread when he is used only to dates, or a townsman with dates when he is used only to wheat. As for the people of disputation, they are a group possessing cleverness by which they have risen above the common people, but their cleverness is deficient; for their innate disposition is complete, but within them is malice, stubbornness, fanaticism, and imitation; that prevents them from perceiving the truth, and these attributes are covers upon their hearts that they should understand it, and in their ears is deafness. And I call them with gentleness to the truth, and I mean by gentleness that I do not act fanatically against them nor treat them harshly, but I am gentle and dispute in the way that is best. And in this is an indication that al-Ghazali presents different colors of knowledge to people, and that among people is a group for whom the truth is hidden due to their lack of ability for it; hence he takes with them the stance that he sees the Law commanded, which is addressing people according to their intellects; and he frequently repeated in his books that effect: 'Address people according to their intellects; do you want that Allah and His messenger should be belied?'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a classificatory and pedagogical exposition by al-Ghazali on how to invite people to the truth. It begins with the premise that people vary in their intellectual capacities and that religion is flexible, so no single approach suits all. Al-Ghazali then divides people into three categories: (1) the common people (al-'awamm), who are simple and safe; (2) the elite (al-khawass), who are intelligent and insightful; and (3) the disputatious (ahl al-jadal), who are clever but flawed by obstinacy. For the elite, he advocates teaching the 'just balances' (al-mawazin al-qist) – a reference to his logical method in Al-Qistas al-Mustaqim – and lists three qualities necessary for them: innate sharpness, freedom from blind imitation, and trust in the teacher's expertise. For the common people, he prescribes gentle admonition (al-maw'izah). For the disputatious, he uses disputation (al-mujadalah) but in the best manner, avoiding harshness. He cites Quran 16:125 as scriptural support for these three methods. Al-Ghazali then employs vivid metaphors: feeding wisdom to those suited only for admonition harms like feeding a baby bird meat; disputation disgusts the wise like an adult disgusted by human milk; and improper disputation with the disputatious is like feeding a Bedouin wheat bread when he is used to dates. He expands on the characteristics of the disputatious: they have some cleverness but are impeded by malice, stubbornness, fanaticism, and imitation, which act as veils on their hearts. He concludes by affirming that this graded approach is enjoined by the Law, citing the principle of addressing people according to their intellects.

Key Terms

أهل الجدل
حكمة
موعظة
جدال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ghazali advocates using disputation with everyone.
Correction: Disputation is only for the disputatious; using it with others is harmful.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, what happens if you use disputation with the people of wisdom?
A) They become happy
B) They feel disgusted
C) They learn quickly
D) They ignore it

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali prescribes specific methods for each group and warns against misapplication; the guiding principle is addressing people according to their intellects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last tried to counsel someone, did you carefully consider their capacity and choose your approach—or did you default to your own comfort zone? Where have you harmed a relationship by feeding wisdom to a heart that needed only simple admonition?
?
Al-Ghazali lists malice, stubbornness, fanaticism, and imitation as inner barriers that block truth. Which of these do you recognise in yourself right now? What is one area of your life where you are holding onto a position out of stubborn habit rather than honest conviction? Will you ask Allah tonight to remove that cover from your heart?
?
Think of someone you know who resists the truth—maybe they argue or become defensive. Have you ever written them off as hopeless? The lesson shows that even the disputatious are called with gentleness. What would it cost you to approach that person this week with genuine gentleness instead of frustration?

The Three Ranks of Madhhab According to al-Ghazali
Al-Ghazali distinguishes three meanings of the term 'madhhab'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'madhhab' in Islamic scholarship has three distinct meanings according to al-Ghazali, and that a complete scholar embodies all three appropriately.

Core Idea
Madhhab is not just a school; it has three ranks.

Al-Ghazali, in his work Mizan al-'Amal, clarifies a fundamental concept often misunderstood. He explains that the word 'madhhab' is not a single, fixed entity but a shared term for three different levels of meaning. This passage helps us avoid conflating personal conviction with pedagogical strategy or polemical defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: the definition, then the three ranks each introduced by a term.
The passage uses 'madhhab' as a homonym (ism mushtarak).
2
Identify the three ranks
List each rank: 1) what one is fanatical about in boasting and disputations, 2) what one follows in instructions and guidance, 3) what one believes in himself from theoretical insights.
Think about how these differ in purpose and audience.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Ghazali says a perfect person has three madhhabs in this regard. Ask: Why would a complete scholar need all three?
Consider the context of teaching, debate, and personal conviction.
4
Connect to the common misunderstanding
Many think madhhab only means legal school. Al-Ghazali shows it is broader. Reflect on how this affects your understanding of scholarly disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل إن الغزالى ليدل على ذلك صراحة فيقول (١) : [ المذهب اسم مشترك لثلاث مراتب : إحداها : ما يتعصب له له في المباهات والمناظرات والأخرى : ما يسار به في التعلمات والإرشادات والثالثة : ما يعتقده المرء في نفسه مما انكشف له من النظريات ولكل كامل ثلاث مذاهب بهذا الاعتبار (۱) ميزان العمل
Rather, al-Ghazali explicitly indicates that by saying: 'Madhhab is a term shared by three ranks: first, what one is fanatical about in boasting and disputations; second, what one follows in instructions and guidance; and third, what one believes in himself from among that which has been revealed to him of theoretical insights. And every perfect person has three madhhabs in this regard.' (1) Mizan al-'Amal.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification. Al-Ghazali states that the word 'madhhab' (often translated as 'school' or 'doctrine') is a homonym (ism mushtarak) covering three distinct meanings. First, the madhhab one fiercely defends in boasting and formal debates – this is the partisan sense. Second, the madhhab one adopts for teaching and guiding others – this is the pedagogical sense. Third, the madhhab that a person holds privately in himself, consisting of truths that have been revealed to him through theoretical investigation – this is the personal conviction. Al-Ghazali concludes that a complete (kamil) person possesses all three types of madhhab, each in its proper context.

مناظرات
تعلمات
إرشادات
نظريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'madhhab' only refers to legal schools.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that 'madhhab' is a broader term covering three distinct meanings: partisan defense, pedagogical instruction, and personal belief. A complete person embodies all three.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, how many ranks of 'madhhab' are there?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali identifies three distinct meanings of 'madhhab'—polemical, pedagogical, and personal—and asserts that a perfect scholar possesses all three.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a position in debate that you did not fully believe in your heart? How does al-Ghazali's distinction between the three madhhabs challenge you to reconcile your public stance with your inner conviction?
?
When you teach or advise others, do you present things as certain that you yourself have not verified? What would it take for you to ensure that your pedagogical madhhab aligns with your personal one?
?
Which of the three ranks do you most rely on in your daily life? Are you neglecting the other two, and what concrete step can you take this week to develop the missing rank(s) in your pursuit of knowledge?
