The Three Methods of Calling to the Truth
Ghazali explains the three methods of da'wa and their appropriate audiences, with analogies and a description of ahl al-jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why different people require different approaches in da'wa and how misapplying methods can be harmful.

Core Idea
Wisdom, admonition, and argument each have their rightful audience.

Ghazali begins by quoting the Quranic command to call to Allah with wisdom, good admonition, and best argument. He then explains that these three methods are not interchangeable; each is suited to a particular type of audience. This lesson explores his classification and the principle of tailoring one's approach to the listener's capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Note the three methods mentioned and the Quranic verse that frames the discussion.
The passage uses analogies to illustrate its points—pay attention to them.
2
Identify the three audiences
Identify the three groups of people mentioned: those who respond to wisdom, those to admonition, and those to argumentation. Understand the characteristics of each group as implied by the analogies.
The passage does not define the groups in detail, but the analogies give clues.
3
Understand the harm of misapplication
Examine the analogies used: feeding wisdom to those needing admonition harms like feeding meat to a baby bird; using argumentation on wisdom-seekers repels them; suboptimal argumentation on argumentation-seekers is like unaccustomed food. Reflect on why the wrong method backfires.
4
Analyze the people of argumentation
Read the description of ahl al-jadal (people of argumentation). Note their cleverness, but also their inner faults that hinder truth. Then see how Ghazali advocates gentleness (talattuf) in dealing with them.
This section highlights the balance between firmness and kindness in da'wa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدع إلى سبيل ربك بالحكمة والموعظة الحسنة وجادلهم بالتي هي أحسن] . فعلم أن المدعو إلى الله تعالى بالحكمة قوم ، و بالموعظة قوم ، وبالمجادلة قوم ؛ فإن الحكمة إن غذى بها أهل الموعظة أضرت بهم كما تضر بالطفل الرضيع التغذية بلحم الطير ، وأن المجادلة إن استعملت مع أهل الحكمة اشمأزوا منها ، كما يشمئز طبع الرجل القوى من الارتضاع بلبن الآدمى ، وأن من استعمل الجدال مع أهل الجدال لا بالطريق الأحسن ، كما تعلم من القرآن ، كان كمن غذى البدوى بخبز البر ، وهو لم يألف إلا التمر ، أو البلدى بالتمر ، وهو لم يألف إلا البر وأما أهل الجدل فهم طائفة فيهم كياسة ، ترقوا بها عن العوام ، ولكن كياستهم ناقصة ؛ إذ كانت الفطرة كاملة ، ولكن في باطنهم خبث وعناد ، وتعصب وتقليد ؛ فذلك يمنعهم عن إدراك الحق ، وتكون هذه الصفات أكنة على قلو بهم أن يفقهوه وفي آذانهم وقراً . و إنى أدعوهم بالتلطف إلى الحق ، وأعنى بالتلطف ألا أتعصب عليهم ولا أعنفهم ، ولكن أرفق وأجادل بالتي هي أحسن ] . وفى هذا ما يشير إلى أن الغزالى يقدم للناس ألواناً مختلفة من المعرفة ، وإلى أن من الناس طائفة تخفى عليهم الحقيقة لعدم طاقتهم إياها ، لهذا فهو يقف منهم الموقف الذي يرى أن الشرع أمر به ، وهو مخاطبة الناس على قدر عقولهم وكثيراً ما ردد في كتبه ذلك الأثر خاطبوا الناس على قدر عقولهم أتريدون أن يكذب الله ورسوله ؟ ]
Call to the way of your Lord with wisdom and good admonition, and argue with them in the best way. So it is known that those called to Allah the Exalted by wisdom are a people, and by admonition are a people, and by argumentation are a people. For if wisdom is fed to the people of admonition, it harms them just as feeding a baby bird meat harms it. And if argumentation is used with the people of wisdom, they recoil from it, just as the nature of a strong man recoils from suckling human milk. And whoever uses argumentation with the people of argumentation not in the best way, as you learn from the Qur'an, is like one who feeds a Bedouin wheat bread when he is not accustomed except to dates, or a city dweller dates when he is not accustomed except to wheat. As for the people of argumentation, they are a group possessing cleverness, rising thereby above the common people, but their cleverness is deficient; for while their natural disposition is complete, inside them is malice, obstinacy, partisanship, and blind following; that prevents them from perceiving the truth, and these qualities become covers on their hearts that they understand it, and in their ears is heaviness. And I call them gently to the truth, and by gentleness I mean that I am not fanatical against them nor harsh with them, but I am kind and argue in the best way. And in this is an indication that al-Ghazali offers people different kinds of knowledge, and that among people is a group for whom the truth is hidden due to their inability to bear it; therefore he adopts the position that the Shari'ah commanded, namely addressing people according to their minds. And he often repeated in his books the report: 'Address people according to their minds; do you want God and His Messenger to be disbelieved?'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the method of da'wa according to Ghazali. It begins with a Quranic verse commanding to call to the way of the Lord with wisdom (hikmah), good admonition (maw'izah hasanah), and arguing in the best way (jidal bi-llati hiya ahsan). Ghazali then explains that people called to Allah are of three types: those who respond to wisdom, those who respond to admonition, and those who respond to argumentation. He warns against using the wrong method for each group: wisdom used on those needing admonition harms them like feeding a baby bird meat; argumentation used on those of wisdom repels them; and using argumentation in a suboptimal way on those suited for argumentation is like feeding someone food they are unaccustomed to. He then describes the people of argumentation (ahl al-jadal): they possess some cleverness that elevates them above commoners, but their cleverness is deficient because their inner nature is tainted by malice, obstinacy, partisanship, and blind following, which prevent them from grasping the truth and make their hearts and ears as if covered. Ghazali says he calls them gently (bi-l-talattuf) to the truth, meaning he avoids fanaticism and harshness, instead being kind and arguing in the best way. The passage concludes with an editorial remark that this shows Ghazali's pedagogical principle of addressing people according to their intellectual capacity, citing the prophetic tradition: 'Address people according to their minds; do you want God and His Messenger to be disbelieved?'

موعظة
مجادلة
جدال
أهل الجدل
التلطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single method of argument works for all audiences.
Correction: Ghazali shows that misapplying a method harms rather than helps, so methods must be tailored.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what happens if wisdom (hikmah) is used with people who are meant for admonition (maw'izah)?
A) They benefit greatly and become wise.
B) It harms them like feeding a baby bird meat.
C) They become more stubborn.
D) They immediately accept the truth.

Key Takeaway
The key to effective da'wa is matching the method to the audience's capacity and disposition, avoiding harm through misapplication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you share your faith, which of these three methods do you instinctively use? Is your choice driven by the other person's need or by your own comfort? How might you be harming instead of helping?
?
Ghazali says ahl al-jadal have cleverness but also malice and obstinacy. Be honest: do you see any of these traits in your own heart when you engage in religious discussions? Will you commit to examining your intentions before arguing?
?
Think of someone you know who is resistant to truth. Have you been using the wrong method with them—maybe arguing when they need admonition, or giving wisdom when they need gentleness? What will you change today to approach them differently?

The Three Meanings of Madhhab According to Ghazali
Ghazali classifies madhhab into three distinct meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's threefold classification of madhhab as fanatical partisanship, pedagogical accommodation, and inner conviction, and why this distinction matters for interpreting theological affiliations.

Core Idea
Madhhab is not a single hat—Ghazali shows it can mean partisanship, teaching strategy, or private belief.

In this passage from Mizan al-'Amal, Ghazali offers a nuanced analysis of the word 'madhhab' (school of thought). Far from being a monolithic label, he argues that the term actually refers to three distinct realities: the inherited partisan identity, the adaptive teaching method, and the innermost conviction. Understanding these layers is essential for reading classical Islamic texts without confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the three-rank classification
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice that Ghazali begins by stating that the term 'madhhab' is shared among three ranks, indicating a deliberate classification.
The word 'مشترك' (shared) is key—this is not a random list but a systematic unpacking.
2
Analyze the first rank: partisanship
Focus on Ghazali's description of the first madhhab: inherited fanaticism from family, teacher, or birthplace, leading to partisanship similar to tribal solidarity. This is what people usually mean by 'so-and-so is Ash'ari' etc.
Ghazali likens this to 'تناصر القبيلة' (tribal solidarity), highlighting its social rather than intellectual basis.
3
Examine the second and third ranks
The second madhhab is adaptive teaching—what the teacher says to students based on their capacity, as in the example of a dull student. The third is private conviction, shared only with those who can understand. Compare how these differ from the first.
The second rank is flexible; the third is hidden. Both contrast with the rigid first rank.

المذهب اسم مشترك لثلاث مراتب : إحداها : ما يتعصب له له فى المباهات والمناظرات والأخرى : ما يسار به في التعلمات والإرشادات والثالثة : ما يعتقده المرء في نفسه مما انكشف له من النظريات ولكل كامل ثلاث مذاهب بهذا الاعتبار فأما المذهب بالمعنى الأول ، فهو نمط الآباء والأجداد ، ومذهب المعلم ومذهب أهل البلد الذى فيه النشوء ، وذلك يختلف بالبلاد والأقطار ، ويختلف بالمعلمين . فمن ولد فى بلد المعتزلة ، أو الأشعرية ، أو الشفعوية ، أو الحنفية ، نفسه منذ صباه ، التعصب له ، والذب عنه ، والذم لما سواه . فيقال هو أشعرى المذهب ، أو معتزلى ، أو شفعوى ، أو حنفى ؛ ومعناه أنه له ، أى ينصر عصابة المتظاهرين بالموالاة ، و يجرى ذلك مجرى تناصر القبيلة [؟] انغرس [؟] بعضهم لبعض [؟] يتعصب [؟] . المذهب الثاني : ما ينطق به في الإرشاد والتعليم، لمن جاء مستفيداً مسترشداً . وهذا لا يتعين على وجه واحد ، بل يختلف بحسب المسترشد ؛ فيناظر كل مسترشد بما يحتمله فهمه ، فإن وقع له مسترشد تركى ، أو هندى ، أو رجل بليد جلف الطبع ، وعلم أنه لو ذكر له أن الله تعالى ليس ذاته في مكان ، وأنه ليس داخل العالم ولا خارجه ، ولا متصلا بالعالم ، ولا منفصلا عنه ؛ لم يلبث أن [؟] الله تعالى [؟] ، ويكذب به ، فينبغى أن يقرر عنده أن الله تعالى على العرش وأنه يرضيه عبادة خلقه ، ويفرح بهم ، و يثيبهم عوضاً وجزاء و إن احتمل أن يذكر له ما هو الحق المبين ، يكشف له . فالمذهب بهذا الاعتبار يتغير ويختلف ويكون مع كل واحد على ما يحتمله فهمه . المذهب الثالث : ما يعتقده الرجل سراً بينه وبين الله عز وجل ، لا يطلع عليه غير الله تعالى ، ولا يذكره إلا مع من هو شريكه في الاطلاع على ما اطلع عليه ، أو بلغ رتبة يقبل الاطلاع عليه ويفهمه ، وذلك بأن يكون مسترشداً ذكياً ] . إلى آخر ما ذكره من الشروط في النص السابق . ومن هذا يظهر أن الغزالى يقول عن الله مثلا للبليد ما لا يقوله للذكى يصور الحقيقة صوراً مختلفة ، على حسب تفاوت الاستعدادات ومعنى ذلك أنه والمدارك
The term 'madhhab' is a name shared by three ranks: first: what one is fanatical about for boasting and debates; second: what one uses in teaching and guidance; third: what one believes in himself from theoretical matters that have been disclosed to him. And every perfect person has three madhhabs in this respect. As for the madhhab in the first sense, it is the pattern of fathers and grandfathers, the madhhab of the teacher, and the madhhab of the town where one grew up; that varies by countries and regions, and varies by teachers. So one who was born in a town of the Mu'tazila, the Ash'ariyya, the Shafi'iyya, or the Hanafiyya becomes fanatical from childhood, defending it, and blaming others. So he is called Ash'ari, Mu'tazili, Shafi'i, or Hanafi; meaning he belongs to it, i.e., he supports the faction pretending to loyalty, and that runs like tribal solidarity [?] they are rooted [?] some of them to others [?] they become fanatical [?]. The second madhhab: what one utters in guidance and teaching to one who comes benefiting and seeking guidance. This is not fixed in one way, but varies according to the seeker; so he debates each seeker according to what his understanding can bear. For if he encounters a Turkish seeker, an Indian, or a coarse dull man, and he knows that if he mentions to him that Allah the Exalted is not a location, that He is not inside the world nor outside, not connected to the world, not separated from it, he would not hesitate to [?] Allah the Exalted [?] and would disbelieve in Him. So he should affirm for him that Allah the Exalted is above the Throne, and that He is pleased by His creation's worship, rejoices in them, and rewards them with compensation and recompense. And if it is possible that he mention to him the clear truth, he may disclose it. Thus the madhhab in this respect changes and varies, and is with each one according to what his understanding can bear. The third madhhab: what a man believes secretly between him and Allah Almighty, no one other than Allah the Exalted is informed of it, and he mentions it only with one who is his partner in the disclosure of what he has been shown, or has reached a rank that accepts disclosure and understands it, and that is if he is an intelligent seeker. Up to the end of the conditions he mentioned in the previous text. And from this it appears that al-Ghazali says about Allah, for example, to the dull person what he does not say to the intelligent one, depicting the truth in different forms according to the disparity of preparedness and perceptions.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the term 'madhhab' (doctrine or way) into three distinct meanings according to Ghazali, drawn from his work Mizan al-'Amal. He states that the word 'madhhab' is shared by three ranks. The first rank is the madhhab of partisanship and boasting: it is the sectarian identity inherited from one's fathers, teachers, or hometown, leading to fanaticism and defense of one's group, similar to tribal solidarity. Thus one is called Ash'ari, Mu'tazili, Shafi'i, or Hanafi, meaning he supports that faction. The second rank is the madhhab used in teaching and guidance: this is not fixed but varies according to the student's capacity. The teacher adapts his presentation to what the student can understand. For example, if a student is dull and would deny Allah if told that He is not in a place, the teacher should instead affirm anthropomorphic notions like 'Allah is above the Throne' to preserve faith. If the student can bear the clear truth, the teacher may disclose it. Thus this madhhab is relative. The third rank is the madhhab of inner conviction: what a person believes secretly between himself and God, only sharing it with those who have reached a similar level of understanding or are intelligent seekers. The passage concludes with an editorial note that Ghazali thereby depicts the truth in different forms according to varying preparedness and perceptions.

Key Terms
مذهب
تعصب
مباهات
مناظرات
إرشاد
تعليم
مسترشد
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One's madhhab is a single fixed identity (e.g., being Ash'ari or Hanafi) that fully defines one's beliefs.
Correction: Ghazali shows that madhhab has three dimensions: inherited partisanship, adaptive teaching, and private conviction. The same person may have different 'madhhabs' in each sense.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, if a teacher encounters a dull student who would reject Allah if told that He is not in a place, what should the teacher do?
A) Insist on the truth even if the student rejects it.
B) Affirm that Allah is above the Throne and similar anthropomorphic statements.
C) Stop teaching the student altogether.
D) Debate the student harshly until he accepts.

Key Takeaway
Madhhab can mean three different things: partisan identity, pedagogical method, or inner belief, and confusing them leads to misunderstanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of Ghazali's three madhhabs dominates your religious identity? Are you more attached to a partisan label (first), a conventional teaching you received (second), or a private conviction you hold (third)? What would it cost you to let go of the first?
?
When you explain Islam to someone with very different background or intelligence, do you adapt your presentation to their capacity, or do you insist on the full truth as you understand it? What fears hold you back from adapting?
?
Is there a belief you hold privately about Allah that you have never shared with anyone because you fear they wouldn't understand or would reject it? If so, are you prepared to keep it secret until you find a 'partner in disclosure'? Does this align with your duty to teach others?

Ghazali's Purpose in the Tahafut and the Skeptic Paradox
Ghazali states his purpose; editor raises skeptic paradox

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali describes his Tahafut as purely negative (falsifying the philosophers) and how this leads to a paradox given his later positive works and his supposed skepticism.

Core Idea
Ghazali's Tahafut is a demolition job, but it raises a puzzle: how can a skeptic write textbooks?

Al-Ghazali begins his Tahafut with a clear statement about his intention: his only goal is to falsify the doctrines of the philosophers, not yet to establish the true doctrine. He promises to write a separate book on the foundations of beliefs. The editor then observes that Ghazali kept his promise, but this very fact creates a paradox: if Ghazali was truly a skeptic about truth (as some have argued), why did he produce such positive works and teach positive courses? In this lesson, we will unpack Ghazali's stated purpose and the puzzle that emerges from it.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's own words
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice that Ghazali distinguishes between two tasks: falsification (تكذيب) and affirmation (إثبات). He only commits to the first in the Tahafut.
The editor's comment after the quote is not Ghazali's own words but an observation.
2
Understand the editor's conclusion
The editor points out that Ghazali did write Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, fulfilling his promise. This shows that the Tahafut should be seen as part of a broader positive project defending a specific kalam doctrine.
Ask yourself: why does the editor say the Tahafut has a 'positive dimension'?
3
Grapple with the paradox
The editor then asks a deep question: if al-Ghazali was a skeptic about truth at this time, how could he write positive works and teach positive lessons? This paradox is the focus of the surrounding discussion.
Think about what implications this has for understanding Ghazali's intellectual state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن لم نلتزم فى هذا الكتاب - يعنى التهافت - إلا تكذيب مذهبهم. وأما إثبات المذهب الحق فسنصف فيه كتاباً بعد الفراغ من هذا إن ساعد التوفيق إن شاء الله نسميه « قواعد العقائد » ونعتنى فيه بالإثبات كما اعتنينا في هذا بالهدم ] وقد وفى الغزالى بما وعد فألف كتابه المشهور « قواعد العقائد » في علم الكلام. وظاهر من ذلك أن الغزالي كان يهدم الفلسفة، لأنها تناقض مذهباً كلامياً معيناً يريد مناصرته . وإذن فينضاف كتاب التهافت ، بمقدار ما فيه من النزعة الإيجابية ، إلى ما ألفه الغزالى فى علم الكلام ، وما قرره في دروسه ، ليتضافر الجميع على تقوية هذا السؤال : كيف يؤلف الشاك فى الحقيقة تأليف إيجابية ، ويدرس تدريساً إيجابيا ؟ !
We did not undertake in this book – meaning the Tahafut – except to falsify their doctrine. As for establishing the true doctrine, we will compose a book on it after finishing this, if success assists, God willing, which we name 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' (Foundations of Beliefs), and we will devote ourselves in it to affirmation as we have devoted ourselves in this [book] to demolition. And al-Ghazali fulfilled what he promised, so he composed his famous book 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' in the science of kalam. And it is apparent from that that al-Ghazali was demolishing philosophy because it contradicts a specific kalam doctrine that he wanted to support. Therefore, the book of the Tahafut, to the extent of its positive tendency, is added to what al-Ghazali composed in the science of kalam and what he established in his lessons, so that all together strengthen this question: how does the skeptic about truth compose positive works and teach positive lessons?
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage begins with al-Ghazali's own statement of intent from the Tahafut: his only aim is to refute the philosophers, not yet to establish the true doctrine, which he postpones to a future book he calls 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id'. The editor then notes that al-Ghazali indeed fulfilled this promise by writing that work, which belongs to the science of kalam. From this, the editor concludes that the Tahafut itself has a positive dimension because it clears the ground for a particular kalam doctrine. But this raises a paradox: if al-Ghazali was in a state of skepticism about the truth during this period (as argued earlier), how could he produce positive writings and teach positive lessons? The passage ends with this unanswered question, setting up the resolution that follows.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Tahafut is entirely negative and destructive.
Correction: The passage shows that Ghazali intended to support a kalam doctrine, and the editor argues the Tahafut has a positive dimension as part of a larger project.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ghazali state his purpose in the Tahafut to be?
A) To prove the truth of philosophy.
B) To demolish the philosophers' doctrine.
C) To write a comprehensive book on kalam.
D) To reconcile philosophy and religion.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's Tahafut is a demolition of philosophy, but it is part of a broader positive project to establish kalam, raising the question of how a skeptic could produce such works.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you identify a falsehood in your life, do you actively work to demolish it, or do you merely acknowledge it without action? What would it take for you to truly 'falsify' that falsehood as Ghazali did with philosophy?
?
Are you living with a contradiction—professing skepticism about some truth while acting with certainty in other areas? Where in your daily life does this inconsistency show itself, and will you commit to resolving it today?
?
Ghazali promised a constructive work after demolition. What is the one positive truth you have neglected to build in your life because you were too focused on tearing down? Write down a concrete step you will take this week to begin that construction.

The Three Meanings of Madhhab According to Ghazali
Ghazali enumerates three meanings of madhhab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's threefold classification of 'madhhab' and how each meaning serves a different function in his thought.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali distinguished between inherited, seeker's, and private conviction as three distinct meanings of madhhab.

The term 'madhhab' can mean different things depending on context. Al-Ghazali, writing during a period of skepticism, clarified that he used the word in three distinct senses. This lesson presents his own classification, which is key to understanding his later works.

Guided Path
1
Read the three meanings
Read the Arabic passage where Ghazali enumerates the three meanings of madhhab. Notice the numbered list and the defining characteristics of each.
The first meaning is based on environment, the second on the seeker's state, the third on personal certainty.
2
Identify the third meaning
The third meaning is the most important for understanding Ghazali's skepticism. It refers to a person's private conviction that they do not disclose publicly.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Ghazali would need to distinguish these three meanings. How does this help resolve apparent contradictions in his writings?
This prepares you for the next lesson on the paradox of skepticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير أن الغزالى قد تكفل بحل هذا الإشكال ، إذ قد حدثنا في النص السابق أن للمذهب ثلاثة معانى :
۱ - مذهب يتعصب له المرء ، لأنه مذهب البلد الذي نشأ فيه ، ومذهب أهله ومعلميه.
۲ – مذهب المسترشدين ، وهو يختلف باختلاف حالهم .
٣ - مذهب يعتقده المرء فى نفسه خاصة ، لا يبوح به إلا لمن اتصف بأمور سبقت الإشارة إليها .
فالغزالى فى حال شكه ، إنما كان يشك فى المذهب بالمعنى الثالث ، لأنه كان يبحث عن الحقيقة التي يدين بها ، ويلقى الله عليها ، ولا يلزم من شكه في المذهب بهذا المعنى ، أن يشك فى المذهب الرسمى ، الذي يتعصب له المرء ، وقد كان مذهب أهل السنة هو مذهب الدولة التي نشأ بين أحضانها ، ومذهب المدارس التي درج فى حجراتها ، ومذهب الأساتذة الذين تعهدوه بالتربية والتعليم إلى فترة بعيدة من عمره . ولذلك فإن كتبه الكلامية كلها مصدرة بمثل هذه الديباجة [ الحمد لله الذي اجتى من صفوة عباده ، عصابة الحق وأهل السنة ] . وواضح أنه ما دام شاكا في الحقيقة في هذه الفترة ، فلا يصح الاعتماد على تأليفه فيها لتصوير آرائه
وأما الفترة الثالثة : التى اهتدى فيها إلى نظرية الكشف الصوفية ، فهى الفترة التى يمكن استمداد تآليفه فيها ، لتصوير المذهب الحق عنده ، لكن ليست كل مؤلفات هذه الفترة تصلح لذلك ، لأن الغزالي لم يتخل في هذه الفترة أيضاً عن مذهبيه بالمعنيين الآخرين. وقد كان الغزالي دقيقاً كل الدقة ، حينما نبه إلى أن له كتباً خاصة ضن بها على الجمهور أودعها [ خالص الحقيقة وصريح المعرفة ] . فهو بذلك قد ساعد الباحثين على أن يفهموه فهماً صحيحاً ، لا لبس فيه ولا غموض ، ومع ذلك لم أر منهم أعطاه يفهم فهماً صحيحاً . و بعد فهذه صفحة مشرقة من تاريخ العلماء الأحرار ، ولعل فيها ما يشجع الباحثين على أن يتخلصوا في بحوثهم من ربقة الجمود والهوى كليهما .
But al-Ghazali undertook to resolve this problem, for he informed us in the previous text that 'madhhab' has three meanings:
1 - A madhhab for which a person is bigoted, because it is the madhhab of the country in which he grew up and the madhhab of his family and teachers.
2 - The madhhab of seekers, which varies according to their state.
3 - A madhhab that a person believes in his own self especially, not disclosing it except to one who possesses qualities previously indicated.
So al-Ghazali in his state of skepticism was only doubting the madhhab in the third meaning, because he was seeking the truth by which he would be religious and meet God, and it does not follow from his doubt about the madhhab in this meaning that he doubts the official madhhab, for which a person is bigoted, and the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna was the madhhab of the state in whose midst he grew up, and the madhhab of the schools in whose rooms he advanced, and the madhhab of the teachers who took charge of his upbringing and education until a distant period of his life. Therefore, all his kalam books begin with a preface like: 'Praise be to God who chose from among His pure servants the party of truth and Ahl al-Sunna.' And it is clear that as long as he was a skeptic about the truth in this period, it is not correct to rely on his compositions in it to depict his views.
As for the third period, in which he was guided to the theory of Sufi unveiling, it is the period from which his works can be derived to depict the true doctrine according to him, but not all writings of this period are suitable for that, because al-Ghazali did not abandon in this period either his two madhhabs in the other two meanings. And al-Ghazali was extremely precise when he alerted that he had special books which he withheld from the general public, in which he deposited 'pure truth and clear knowledge.' Thus he has helped researchers to understand him correctly, without ambiguity or obscurity, yet I have not seen anyone give him the care he deserves in that. After that, this is a bright page from the history of free scholars, and perhaps it contains something to encourage researchers to free themselves in their research from the yoke of both rigidity and caprice.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is the editor's resolution to the paradox of how a skeptic could write positively. It begins by citing al-Ghazali's own classification of 'madhhab' into three types: (1) the madhhab of blind partisanship based on upbringing and environment, (2) the madhhab of seekers which varies with their spiritual state, and (3) the madhhab of personal conviction kept private. The editor explains that al-Ghazali's skepticism was only about the third meaning—his personal conviction—while he remained firmly within the official Sunni madhhab (first meaning) because that was the madhhab of the state, schools, and teachers of his upbringing. Thus his kalam works, such as Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, are all prefaced with praise of Ahl al-Sunna. Therefore, during his skeptical period, his positive works reflect the official madhhab, not his private search. The editor then distinguishes a third period after al-Ghazali adopted Sufi unveiling, during which his works can be used to depict his true doctrine, but with a caveat: even then he did not abandon the first two meanings of madhhab, and he composed esoteric books for an elite audience containing pure truth. The editor notes that al-Ghazali's own precision in distinguishing these periods and works should help scholars understand him correctly, but they have not given it sufficient attention.

Key Terms
المذهب بالمعنى الثالث
المذهب الرسمي
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali's skepticism meant he abandoned all madhhabs.
Correction: He only doubted the third meaning (personal conviction), not the first (official Sunni madhhab).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which meaning of madhhab is based on the country and teachers one grew up with?
A) The first meaning
B) The second meaning
C) The third meaning
D) All three meanings

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali distinguished three meanings of 'madhhab': inherited, seeker's, and personal conviction, and his skepticism concerned only the third.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these three meanings best describes your current attachment to the madhhab you follow? Are you bigoted, seeking, or hiding a private conviction? What does your answer reveal about your sincerity in seeking truth?
?
If you were to be completely honest with yourself, is there a madhhab you believe in privately but are afraid to express? What prevents you from seeking the truth openly, and what would it cost you to change?
?
Al-Ghazali's distinction reveals that a seeker's madhhab changes with growth. Have you allowed your understanding of Islam to evolve, or do you cling to a static version you inherited? What would it take for you to admit you might be wrong?

Resolving the Paradox of Skepticism
Editor explains which meaning Ghazali doubted and why his kalam works are reliable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ghazali's distinction between the three meanings of madhhab resolves the apparent contradiction between his skepticism and his adherence to Sunni doctrine.

Core Idea
Ghazali's skepticism was limited to personal conviction, not the official Sunni madhhab of his society.

How could a skeptic write positive works on Sunni theology? The editor uses Ghazali's own threefold classification to show that his doubt never touched the official madhhab. This lesson explains that resolution and shows that Ghazali's kalam works reflect the state's Sunni doctrine, not his private search.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of skepticism
Read the passage carefully to see which of the three meanings Ghazali doubted. The editor states it was only the third meaning.
The passage says: 'إنما كان يشك في المذهب بالمعنى الثالث'.
2
Understand the official madhhab
The editor explains that the official Sunni madhhab (first meaning) was the madhhab of the state, schools, and teachers. Ghazali did not doubt this.
His kalam books begin with praise of Ahl al-Sunna.
3
Conclude the resolution
Thus, his positive works during the skeptical period reflect the official madhhab, not his personal uncertainty. This resolves the paradox.

غير أن الغزالى قد تكفل بحل هذا الإشكال ، إذ قد حدثنا في النص السابق أن للمذهب ثلاثة معانى :
۱ - مذهب يتعصب له المرء ، لأنه مذهب البلد الذي نشأ فيه ، ومذهب أهله ومعلميه.
۲ – مذهب المسترشدين ، وهو يختلف باختلاف حالهم .
٣ - مذهب يعتقده المرء فى نفسه خاصة ، لا يبوح به إلا لمن اتصف بأمور سبقت الإشارة إليها .
فالغزالى فى حال شكه ، إنما كان يشك فى المذهب بالمعنى الثالث ، لأنه كان يبحث عن الحقيقة التي يدين بها ، ويلقى الله عليها ، ولا يلزم من شكه في المذهب بهذا المعنى ، أن يشك فى المذهب الرسمى ، الذي يتعصب له المرء ، وقد كان مذهب أهل السنة هو مذهب الدولة التي نشأ بين أحضانها ، ومذهب المدارس التي درج فى حجراتها ، ومذهب الأساتذة الذين تعهدوه بالتربية والتعليم إلى فترة بعيدة من عمره . ولذلك فإن كتبه الكلامية كلها مصدرة بمثل هذه الديباجة [ الحمد لله الذي اجتى من صفوة عباده ، عصابة الحق وأهل السنة ] . وواضح أنه ما دام شاكا في الحقيقة في هذه الفترة ، فلا يصح الاعتماد على تأليفه فيها لتصوير آرائه
وأما الفترة الثالثة : التى اهتدى فيها إلى نظرية الكشف الصوفية ، فهى الفترة التى يمكن استمداد تآليفه فيها ، لتصوير المذهب الحق عنده ، لكن ليست كل مؤلفات هذه الفترة تصلح لذلك ، لأن الغزالي لم يتخل في هذه الفترة أيضاً عن مذهبيه بالمعنيين الآخرين. وقد كان الغزالي دقيقاً كل الدقة ، حينما نبه إلى أن له كتباً خاصة ضن بها على الجمهور أودعها [ خالص الحقيقة وصريح المعرفة ] . فهو بذلك قد ساعد الباحثين على أن يفهموه فهماً صحيحاً ، لا لبس فيه ولا غموض ، ومع ذلك لم أر منهم أعطاه يفهم فهماً صحيحاً . و بعد فهذه صفحة مشرقة من تاريخ العلماء الأحرار ، ولعل فيها ما يشجع الباحثين على أن يتخلصوا في بحوثهم من ربقة الجمود والهوى كليهما .
But al-Ghazali undertook to resolve this problem, for he informed us in the previous text that 'madhhab' has three meanings:
1 - A madhhab for which a person is bigoted, because it is the madhhab of the country in which he grew up and the madhhab of his family and teachers.
2 - The madhhab of seekers, which varies according to their state.
3 - A madhhab that a person believes in his own self especially, not disclosing it except to one who possesses qualities previously indicated.
So al-Ghazali in his state of skepticism was only doubting the madhhab in the third meaning, because he was seeking the truth by which he would be religious and meet God, and it does not follow from his doubt about the madhhab in this meaning that he doubts the official madhhab, for which a person is bigoted, and the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna was the madhhab of the state in whose midst he grew up, and the madhhab of the schools in whose rooms he advanced, and the madhhab of the teachers who took charge of his upbringing and education until a distant period of his life. Therefore, all his kalam books begin with a preface like: 'Praise be to God who chose from among His pure servants the party of truth and Ahl al-Sunna.' And it is clear that as long as he was a skeptic about the truth in this period, it is not correct to rely on his compositions in it to depict his views.
As for the third period, in which he was guided to the theory of Sufi unveiling, it is the period from which his works can be derived to depict the true doctrine according to him, but not all writings of this period are suitable for that, because al-Ghazali did not abandon in this period either his two madhhabs in the other two meanings. And al-Ghazali was extremely precise when he alerted that he had special books which he withheld from the general public, in which he deposited 'pure truth and clear knowledge.' Thus he has helped researchers to understand him correctly, without ambiguity or obscurity, yet I have not seen anyone give him the care he deserves in that. After that, this is a bright page from the history of free scholars, and perhaps it contains something to encourage researchers to free themselves in their research from the yoke of both rigidity and caprice.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is the editor's resolution to the paradox of how a skeptic could write positively. It begins by citing al-Ghazali's own classification of 'madhhab' into three types: (1) the madhhab of blind partisanship based on upbringing and environment, (2) the madhhab of seekers which varies with their spiritual state, and (3) the madhhab of personal conviction kept private. The editor explains that al-Ghazali's skepticism was only about the third meaning—his personal conviction—while he remained firmly within the official Sunni madhhab (first meaning) because that was the madhhab of the state, schools, and teachers of his upbringing. Thus his kalam works, such as Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, are all prefaced with praise of Ahl al-Sunna. Therefore, during his skeptical period, his positive works reflect the official madhhab, not his private search. The editor then distinguishes a third period after al-Ghazali adopted Sufi unveiling, during which his works can be used to depict his true doctrine, but with a caveat: even then he did not abandon the first two meanings of madhhab, and he composed esoteric books for an elite audience containing pure truth. The editor notes that al-Ghazali's own precision in distinguishing these periods and works should help scholars understand him correctly, but they have not given it sufficient attention.

Key Terms
المذهب بالمعنى الثالث
المذهب الرسمي
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali's skepticism made him abandon Sunni theology during that period.
Correction: He only doubted his personal belief (third meaning), not the Sunni madhhab of his society, which he continued to publicly uphold.

Checkpoint
According to the editor, what does Ghazali's doubt about the third meaning imply about his adherence to the official Sunni madhhab?
A) It implies he also doubted the official madhhab.
B) It implies he rejected the official madhhab.
C) It does not imply doubt about the official madhhab.
D) It implies he only followed the official madhhab publicly.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's doubt was confined to his personal search for truth, not the official Sunni doctrine he taught and wrote about.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever publicly professed a belief while privately struggling with doubt? How does Ghazali's distinction between personal conviction and official madhhab challenge you to be more honest with yourself and God? Will you bring that hidden struggle into your prayer today?
?
Ghazali's public works remained firmly Sunni even while he sought truth privately. Are your outward actions of worship and teaching fully aligned with the truth you are seeking, or are you merely going through the motions? What is one area where your public and private faith diverge, and what concrete step will you take this week to close that gap?
?
The editor says Ghazali was 'seeking the truth by which he would be religious and meet God.' If you were to die tonight, would you meet God with a truth you have genuinely sought, or with inherited beliefs you never examined? When will you set aside time to honestly re-evaluate your deepest convictions?

The Third Period and Esoteric Works
Editor discusses the third period after Sufi kashf and the esoteric works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of al-Ghazali's third period after Sufi unveiling and the role of his esoteric works in conveying his true doctrine.

Core Idea
Ghazali's true doctrine is found in his esoteric works from the Sufi period, but even then he maintained his public madhhab.

After his period of skepticism, al-Ghazali embraced Sufi unveiling and composed works that reflect his true doctrine. However, he was careful to distinguish between public and private writings. This lesson explores the editor's discussion of that third period and the special books Ghazali reserved for an elite audience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third period
Read the passage about the third period when Ghazali was guided to the theory of Sufi unveiling. This is when his works can depict his true doctrine.
The editor says this period is the one 'التى اهتدى فيها إلى نظرية الكشف الصوفية'.
2
Understand the caveat
Not all works from this period are suitable because Ghazali still adhered to the first two meanings of madhhab. He wrote esoteric books for the elite.
The editor mentions books in which Ghazali deposited 'pure truth and clear knowledge' and withheld from the public.
3
Appreciate Ghazali's precision
Ghazali was extremely precise in indicating which works contain his true views. The editor laments that scholars have not taken this into account.
This shows the importance of distinguishing between exoteric and esoteric writings.

Ghazali's Classification of His Works on Creed
Ghazali classifies his own works on creed and praises al-Iqtisad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali classifies his own works on creed and why he considers al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad superior to formal kalam.

Core Idea
Al-Iqtisad is a superior kalam that knocks on the doors of knowledge, yet remains in the realm of belief.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali guides his readers through his own body of work on creed. He distinguishes between various levels of his writings, pointing out which ones contain proofs of creed and which approach a deeper understanding. This sets the stage for his crucial distinction between belief and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the works mentioned
Read the Arabic and translation to find the names of the works Ghazali lists. Note their sizes and relationships.
He mentions al-Risala al-Qudsiyya, Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id, and al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad.
2
Compare al-Iqtisad with formal kalam
Ghazali claims al-Iqtisad is more thorough and closer to knowledge than formal kalam. Consider what he means by 'formal kalam' and why al-Iqtisad might be superior.
Think about methodology and accessibility.
3
Ask: why does he make this distinction?
Reflect on why Ghazali feels the need to classify his works and assert the uniqueness of al-Iqtisad. This will connect to his broader theological project.
His goal is to move beyond mere dialectic toward inner certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الرسالة القدسية أدلة العقيدة - وتقع في عشرين ورقة – وهي أحد فصول كتاب « قواعد العقائد » من كتاب « الإحياء » . وأما أدلتها مع تحقيق وزيادة تأنق في إيراد الأسئلة والإشكالات، فقد أودعناها كتاب الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد في مقدار مائة ورقة، فهو كتاب مفرد برأسه يحوي لباب علم المتكلمين، ولكنه أبلغ في التحقيق وأقرب إلى قرع أبواب المعرفة من الكلام الرسمي الذي يصادف في كتب المتكلمين، وكل ذلك يرجع إلى الاعتقاد لا إلى المعرفة، فإن المتكلم لا يفارق العامي إلا في كونه عارفاً، وكون العامي معتقداً، بل هو أيضاً عرف مع اعتقاده أدلة الاعتقاد ليؤكد الاعتقاد، ويحرسه من تشويش المبتدعة، ولا تنحل عقدة الاعتقاد إلى انشراح المعرفة. فإن أردت أن تستنشق شيئاً من روائح المعرفة، صادفت منها مقداراً يسيراً مبثوثاً في كتاب الصبر، والشكر، وكتاب المحبة، وباب التوحيد، من أول
And in al-Risala al-Qudsiyya are the proofs of creed - it spans twenty folios – and it is one of the chapters of the book 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' from the book 'al-Ihya'. As for its proofs with verification and additional refinement in presenting questions and problems, we have deposited them in the book 'al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad' in about one hundred folios. It is an independent book containing the core of the science of the mutakallimun, but it is more thorough in verification and closer to knocking on the doors of knowledge than the formal kalam encountered in the books of the mutakallimun. All of that pertains to belief (i'tiqad), not to knowledge (ma'rifa). For the mutakallim does not differ from the layman except in being knowledgeable ('arif), while the layman is a believer (mu'taqid). Rather, he also knows along with his belief the proofs of belief in order to confirm belief and guard it from the confusion of innovators, and the knot of belief is not untied into the expansion of knowledge. If you wish to inhale something from the fragrances of knowledge, you will encounter a small amount scattered in the Book of Patience, Gratitude, the Book of Love, and the Chapter of Tawhid, from the beginning.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a self-classification by Imam al-Ghazali of his own works, distinguishing between the levels of belief (i'tiqad) and knowledge (ma'rifa). He begins by listing his works that contain proofs of creed: al-Risala al-Qudsiyya (a chapter in Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id from Ihya 'Ulum al-Din) and al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad. He claims that al-Iqtisad is superior to conventional kalam books in verification and in leading to knowledge, yet all these works ultimately belong to the realm of belief, not knowledge. Then he explains that the mutakallim (theologian) differs from the layman only in being knowledgeable ('arif) of the proofs, while the layman simply believes. The mutakallim knows the proofs to confirm his belief and protect it from doubts of innovators, but this does not transform the knot (uqda) of belief into the expansion (inshirah) of knowledge. Finally, he directs the seeker of knowledge to find traces of true knowledge scattered in his spiritual works: Kitab al-Sabr, al-Shukr, al-Mahabbah, and Bab al-Tawhid from Ihya. The passage ends abruptly with 'min awwal' (from the beginning), likely continuing with further examples.

Key Terms
الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد (al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad)
الكلام الرسمي (formal kalam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which work does Ghazali claim is more thorough and closer to knowledge than formal kalam?
A) al-Risala al-Qudsiyya
B) Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id
C) al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad
D) Ihya 'Ulum al-Din

Key Takeaway
Ghazali positions his work al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad as a superior form of kalam, more effective than conventional theological dialectic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali distinguishes his work as 'closer to knocking on the doors of knowledge' than conventional kalam. Have you ever settled for a superficial understanding of your creed, or are you actively seeking a deeper verification? What specific belief will you strive to verify more thoroughly starting today?
?
When you study theology, do you treat any book as final, or do you follow Ghazali's approach of seeking refinement? Which source of your current understanding might need to be re-examined, and what will you do to replace passive acceptance with active inquiry?
?
The passage shows Ghazali investing effort to produce a more effective work. Are you willing to invest similar effort in your own learning, even if it means going beyond the standard curriculum? What one step will you take this week to move from formal knowledge toward true understanding?

The Distinction Between Belief (I'tiqad) and Knowledge (Ma'rifa)
Ghazali distinguishes belief from knowledge and describes the mutakallim's role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between belief (i'tiqad) and knowledge (ma'rifa), and the role of the mutakallim versus the layman.

Core Idea
The mutakallim's knowledge of proofs confirms belief but does not transform it into the expansion of knowledge.

After classifying his works, Ghazali clarifies a fundamental epistemological distinction: all of his works on creed, including al-Iqtisad, pertain only to belief (i'tiqad), not to knowledge (ma'rifa). He then explains how the mutakallim (theologian) differs from the layman, and where one might find glimpses of true knowledge. This lesson explores that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim that all works on creed are i'tiqad
Read the opening sentence of this passage portion: 'All of that pertains to belief, not to knowledge.' Consider the weight of this statement: even the refined kalam of al-Iqtisad remains at the level of belief.
Belief here means settled conviction based on proof, but not the direct experiential knowledge Ghazali calls ma'rifa.
2
Compare the mutakallim and the layman
Ghazali says the mutakallim differs from the layman only in being knowledgeable of proofs. Both share belief, but the mutakallim has additional proof-awareness. Ask: What is the purpose of this knowledge if it does not elevate to ma'rifa?
The purpose is to confirm belief and guard against doubts, not to attain the expansion of knowledge.
3
Locate where true knowledge is found
Ghazali directs the seeker to find scattered traces of knowledge in his spiritual works like Kitab al-Sabr, al-Shukr, al-Mahabbah, and Bab al-Tawhid. Note that these are parts of Ihya concerned with inner states, not rational theology.
This hints at his distinction between the science of outward deeds and the science of inward unveiling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الرسالة القدسية أدلة العقيدة - وتقع في عشرين ورقة – وهي أحد فصول كتاب « قواعد العقائد » من كتاب « الإحياء » . وأما أدلتها مع تحقيق وزيادة تأنق في إيراد الأسئلة والإشكالات، فقد أودعناها كتاب الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد في مقدار مائة ورقة، فهو كتاب مفرد برأسه يحوي لباب علم المتكلمين، ولكنه أبلغ في التحقيق وأقرب إلى قرع أبواب المعرفة من الكلام الرسمي الذي يصادف في كتب المتكلمين، وكل ذلك يرجع إلى الاعتقاد لا إلى المعرفة، فإن المتكلم لا يفارق العامي إلا في كونه عارفاً، وكون العامي معتقداً، بل هو أيضاً عرف مع اعتقاده أدلة الاعتقاد ليؤكد الاعتقاد، ويحرسه من تشويش المبتدعة، ولا تنحل عقدة الاعتقاد إلى انشراح المعرفة. فإن أردت أن تستنشق شيئاً من روائح المعرفة، صادفت منها مقداراً يسيراً مبثوثاً في كتاب الصبر، والشكر، وكتاب المحبة، وباب التوحيد، من أول
And in al-Risala al-Qudsiyya are the proofs of creed - it spans twenty folios – and it is one of the chapters of the book 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id' from the book 'al-Ihya'. As for its proofs with verification and additional refinement in presenting questions and problems, we have deposited them in the book 'al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad' in about one hundred folios. It is an independent book containing the core of the science of the mutakallimun, but it is more thorough in verification and closer to knocking on the doors of knowledge than the formal kalam encountered in the books of the mutakallimun. All of that pertains to belief (i'tiqad), not to knowledge (ma'rifa). For the mutakallim does not differ from the layman except in being knowledgeable ('arif), while the layman is a believer (mu'taqid). Rather, he also knows along with his belief the proofs of belief in order to confirm belief and guard it from the confusion of innovators, and the knot of belief is not untied into the expansion of knowledge. If you wish to inhale something from the fragrances of knowledge, you will encounter a small amount scattered in the Book of Patience, Gratitude, the Book of Love, and the Chapter of Tawhid, from the beginning.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a self-classification by Imam al-Ghazali of his own works, distinguishing between the levels of belief (i'tiqad) and knowledge (ma'rifa). He begins by listing his works that contain proofs of creed: al-Risala al-Qudsiyya (a chapter in Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id from Ihya 'Ulum al-Din) and al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad. He claims that al-Iqtisad is superior to conventional kalam books in verification and in leading to knowledge, yet all these works ultimately belong to the realm of belief, not knowledge. Then he explains that the mutakallim (theologian) differs from the layman only in being knowledgeable ('arif) of the proofs, while the layman simply believes. The mutakallim knows the proofs to confirm his belief and protect it from doubts of innovators, but this does not transform the knot (uqda) of belief into the expansion (inshirah) of knowledge. Finally, he directs the seeker of knowledge to find traces of true knowledge scattered in his spiritual works: Kitab al-Sabr, al-Shukr, al-Mahabbah, and Bab al-Tawhid from Ihya. The passage ends abruptly with 'min awwal' (from the beginning), likely continuing with further examples.

Key Terms
الاعتقاد (i'tiqad)
المعرفة (ma'rifa)
المتكلم (mutakallim)
العامي (layman)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad is a work of pure ma'rifa (knowledge) because it is 'closer to knowledge.'
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states: 'All of that pertains to belief (i'tiqad), not to knowledge (ma'rifa).' It remains in the realm of belief, though it is a refined form.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the main difference between the mutakallim and the layman?
A) The mutakallim has belief while the layman has knowledge.
B) The mutakallim is knowledgeable (arif) of the proofs, while the layman is a believer (mu'taqid).
C) The mutakallim has direct knowledge (ma'rifa) while the layman has only belief.
D) The mutakallim and the layman are identical in their level of understanding.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali distinguishes belief (i'tiqad) from knowledge (ma'rifa); even the most refined kalam remains belief, while true knowledge is found in spiritual works.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you assume that simply knowing arguments about God makes you a knower of God? Where is your own expansion of knowledge, or are you content with a well-defended knot?
?
Ghazali says the fragrances of knowledge are scattered in patience, gratitude, love, and tawhid. Have you ever paused your study of proofs to savor those fragrances? What would it take for you to seek them deliberately?
?
If the mutakallim's proofs only confirm belief but do not untie its knot, what is the state of your own belief? Is it a tight knot that protects you, or have you tasted the expansion of ma'rifa? What step can you take today to move beyond the mere confirmation of truth to direct experience?

Ghazali's Concealment of Deeper Sciences
Ghazali justifies concealing four deeper sciences and states the condition for disclosure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's rationale for concealing certain deeper sciences and the conditions under which they may be revealed to qualified individuals.

Core Idea
Ghazali guarded deeper truths because shallow seekers cannot handle them.

Ghazali acknowledges that he wrote about the deeper sciences but did not make them fully public. He explains that most intellects cannot bear these matters, and that the weak — especially those who only pretend to be scholars — would be dazzled. He then sets a condition: only someone who has mastered exoteric religious sciences may receive this knowledge, and it is forbidden to show it to others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the four sciences
The passage lists four sciences that Ghazali concealed. Find each one and note what they are.
These are: Essence, Attributes, Actions, and the Return (eschatology).
3
Understand the condition for disclosure
Ghazali explains who may be shown these sciences. What is the requirement? Why does he forbid showing it to others?
The condition is mastery of exoteric science (ilm al-zahir) and possessing certain qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه العلوم - أعنى علم الذات ، والصفات ، والأفعال ، وعلم المعاد – أودعنا من أوائله ومجامعه القدر الذي رزقنا منه ، مع قصر العمر ، وكثرة الشواغل والآفات ، وقلة الأعوان والرفقاء ، بعض التصانيف ؛ لكنا لم نظهره ، فإنه يكل عنه أكثر الأفهام ، ويستضربه الضعفاء ، وهم أكثر المترسمين (٢) بالعلم بل لا يصلح إظهاره إلا على من أتقن علم الظاهر وحرام على من يقع ذلك الكتاب فى يده أن يظهره إلا على من استجمع هذه الصفات
These sciences—namely, the science of the Essence, the Attributes, the Actions, and the science of the Return—we have deposited in some works from their beginnings and compendiums, according to the measure we were granted, given the shortness of life, the abundance of distractions and afflictions, and the scarcity of helpers and companions. But we did not make them manifest, for most intellects fail to comprehend them, and the weak are dazzled by them—and they are the majority of those who pretend to be scholars (2). Rather, it is not proper to manifest them except to one who has mastered the exoteric science, and it is forbidden for the one into whose hand that book falls to show it except to one who has gathered these qualities.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Ghazali's 'Jawahir al-Quran' in which he discusses why he concealed certain sciences. He specifies the sciences: the science of the Divine Essence, Attributes, Actions, and the Return (eschatology). He says he has deposited some of their beginnings and compendiums in his works, but did not manifest them fully due to constraints and the inability of most people to understand them. He notes that the weak—who are the majority of those who pretend to be scholars—would be dazzled. He then states that it is only proper to manifest these sciences to one who has mastered exoteric ('ilm al-zahir) science, and it is forbidden for the holder of such a book to show it to anyone who does not possess those qualities.

Key Terms
علم الذات
علم الصفات
علم الأفعال
علم المعاد
علم الظاهر
المترسمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali completely abandoned or rejected the deeper sciences of Essence, Attributes, etc.
Correction: Ghazali wrote about them but did not make them manifest to the general public due to their inability to understand.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, to whom is it permissible to reveal the deeper sciences of the Essence, Attributes, Actions, and Return?
A) All Muslims
B) Only the mutakallimun
C) One who has mastered the exoteric science and possesses certain qualities
D) Only the elite among the philosophers

Key Takeaway
Ghazali concealed the sciences of Essence, Attributes, Actions, and Return because most intellects cannot bear them, and only those who have mastered exoteric sciences may receive them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rushed to learn something about God's Essence or Attributes without first grounding yourself in the basics? What is the danger of that for your faith?
?
The passage says that most intellects fail to comprehend these deeper sciences. Do you honestly examine your own capacity, or do you assume you are among the few? How can you know?
?
If you were entrusted with a deep truth about God but forbidden from sharing it except with those qualified, would you obey? What would that obedience cost you?

The Editor's Core Claim Against Tahafut
Editor states his main claim and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's methodological argument that Tahafut al-Falasifa should not be taken as a reliable source for Ghazali's final theological positions.

Core Idea
Tahafut was written for fame, not truth.

The editor opens by asserting a methodological principle: only certain writings can reliably convey an author's true opinions. He then applies this directly to Ghazali's Tahafut, arguing that it does not belong to that trusted category, because Ghazali himself wrote it while seeking status and renown, not to defend the true doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the methodological claim
Read the first sentence carefully. It states that Ghazali's true opinions can only be derived from 'this kind of books' – not from Tahafut. Note the absolutist language.
2
Examine the reason given
The editor immediately provides the reason: Ghazali wrote Tahafut to gain status, fame, and renown, and to support the doctrine that would bring him that, not the true doctrine.
This is the core accusation.
3
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: if this claim is accepted, how does it affect the value of Tahafut as a theological source? The editor is inviting a reassessment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأنت ترى في ذلك أنه لا مطلقاً استمداد آراء الغزالى الخاصة به ، يصح إلا من هذا الصنف من الكتب دون غيره ، وظاهر أن كتاب « التهافت » ليس من بينها ، فلا يصح اعتباره مصوراً لآراء الغزالي وأفكاره الخاصة وأخيراً فقد ألف الغزالى كتاب ( التهافت ) حين كان يطلب الجاه ، والشهرة وبعد الصيت ، فيناصر به المذهب الذى يجلب له كل ذلك ، لا المذهب الحق في ذاته وذلك أن أهل السنة فى تلك الفترة كانوا يضيقون ذرعاً بالمعتزلة والفلاسفة ؛ ولكنهم كانوا واجدين بين صفوفهم من يجرؤ على مناوأة المعتزلة ، والرد عليهم ؛ ولم يكونوا واجدين من يستطيع أن يتقدم إلى الفلاسفة ليطعنهم بسلاح العلم والمعرفة ، حتى يعيش مذهب أهل السنة في طمأنينة وأمان ، فكان المجال فسيحاً لمن يريد أن يتقدم ، لينال من ألقاب الفخاء ما تصبو إليه نفسه ، مما لم ينله أحد قط ، فوجد « أبو حامد » فى هذا مجالاً لإشباع غروره ، فحمل على الفلاسفة حملة عنيفة ، طيرت اسمه في الآفاق ، ورددت في الخافقين ذكره ولسنا نظلم الغزالى حين نقول عنه : إنه فى تلك الفترة التى ألف فيها كتاب ( التهافت » كان يطلب الجاه ، والشهرة ، وبعد الصيت ، فها هو ذا يتحدث عن نفسه في تلك الفترة فيقول : 1 ثم تفكرت في نيتي في التدريس – يعنى حين هم بالخروج من بغداد لتطبيق منهج الصوفية على نفسه فإذا هي غير خالصة لوجه الله تعالى ، بل باعثها ومحركها طلب الجاه ، وانتشار الصيت ، فتيقنت أنى على شفا جرف هار ، وأنى قد أشفيت على النار ، إن لم أشتغل بتلافي الأحوال ] -~- · و يقول في موضع آخر - حين رجع بعد الخلوة ليزاول التدريس بنيسابور: وأنا أعلم أنى وإن رجعت إلى نشر العلم ، فما رجعت ، فإن الرجوع عود إلى ما كان ، وكنت فى ذلك الزمان - يشير إلى أيام بغداد حين ألف كتاب التهافت - أنشر العلم الذى به يكسب الجاه ، وأدعو إليه بقولى ، وعملى ، وكان ذلك قصدى ونيتى ] * يخلص من كل هذا أن كتاب « التهافت » لا يصلح اتخاذه مرجعاً لتصوير أفكار الغزالي ، التي يدين بها ويلقى الله عليها ، بل يجب أن تستمد هذه
Thus you see that absolutely the derivation of Ghazali's own opinions is valid only from this kind of books, not from others, and it is obvious that the book 'al-Tahafut' is not among them, so it is not correct to consider it as a depiction of Ghazali's own opinions and ideas. Finally, Ghazali wrote the book 'al-Tahafut' when he was seeking status, fame, and renown, thereby supporting the doctrine that would bring him all that, not the true doctrine in itself. This is because the Sunnis at that time were becoming impatient with the Mu'tazila and the philosophers, but they did not find among their ranks anyone bold enough to oppose the Mu'tazila and respond to them, and they did not find anyone who could advance against the philosophers to attack them with the weapon of knowledge and learning, so that the Sunni doctrine could live in tranquility and security. So the field was wide for anyone who wanted to come forward to attain titles of glory that his soul aspired to, which no one had ever attained. So Abu Hamid found in this an opportunity to satisfy his vanity, and he launched a violent attack against the philosophers, which made his name fly across the horizons and repeated his mention in the two directions. We are not doing injustice to Ghazali when we say about him: that in that period when he wrote the book 'al-Tahafut' he was seeking status, fame, and renown. Behold, he speaks about himself in that period saying: 'Then I reflected on my intention in teaching – meaning when he intended to leave Baghdad to apply the Sufi method upon himself – and behold it was not pure for the sake of God Almighty, but rather its impetus and mover was seeking status and spread of fame, so I was certain that I was on the brink of a crumbling bank, and that I was on the verge of the Fire if I did not occupy myself with rectifying my state.' And he says in another place – when he returned after the retreat to resume teaching in Nishapur: 'I know that even if I have returned to spreading knowledge, I have not returned; for returning is a return to what was, and in that time – referring to the days of Baghdad when he wrote the book al-Tahafut – I was spreading the knowledge by which status is earned, and calling to it by my words and deeds, and that was my intention and purpose.' The conclusion from all this is that the book 'al-Tahafut' is not suitable to be taken as a reference for depicting the ideas of Ghazali which he believes and meets God with; rather, these must be derived from...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a modern editor's argument about the reliability of Ghazali's 'Tahafut al-Falasifa' as a source for his final theological positions. The editor first states that Ghazali's true opinions can only be derived from certain books, not from 'al-Tahafut'. He then provides a historical context: Sunnis were troubled by Mu'tazila and philosophers but lacked capable defenders; Ghazali saw an opportunity to gain fame and satisfied his vanity by attacking the philosophers. The editor then supports this claim with two direct quotations from Ghazali's own later writings. The first quote is from when Ghazali was leaving Baghdad to adopt Sufism: he reflects that his teaching intention was not pure for God but driven by status and fame. The second quote is from after his retreat, when he returned to teaching in Nishapur: he admits that during the time of writing 'al-Tahafut', he was spreading knowledge that earns status, and that was his intention. The editor concludes that 'al-Tahafut' should not be used as a reference for Ghazali's true beliefs.

Key Terms
الغزالى
التهافت
الجاه
الشهرة
المذهب الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume Tahafut is Ghazali's definitive refutation of philosophy, reflecting his final view.
Correction: This passage argues that Tahafut was an early work motivated by personal ambition, not a reliable statement of his mature theology.

Checkpoint
According to the editor, why is Tahafut not a reliable source for Ghazali's true opinions?
A) Because it was written in a language that was not Arabic
B) Because Ghazali wrote it while seeking fame and status, not to defend the true doctrine
C) Because the book was lost and only fragments remain
D) Because Ghazali was not a philosopher

Key Takeaway
Tahafut al-Falasifa, according to this editor, is not a reliable representation of Ghazali's final beliefs because it was written for personal gain rather than for truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study or teach Islamic knowledge, do you ever catch yourself seeking recognition or admiration rather than sincerely wanting the truth? What would it take for you to purify your intention right now?
?
Have you ever defended a position not because you were convinced it was true, but because it was popular or rewarding? Think of a specific instance—what did you gain, and what did you lose?
?
How do you determine which sources or scholars to trust? If a scholar wrote something for fame, would you be able to detect it? What steps can you take to ensure you are not being misled by your own desire for status when choosing whom to follow?

Historical Context and Ghazali's Self-Critique
Historical context and self-quotes support the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical context that made Tahafut a strategic work, and the evidence from Ghazali's own later writings that supports the editor's claim.

Core Idea
Ghazali himself admitted he wrote for fame.

The editor now provides the historical backdrop: Sunnis were distressed by Mu'tazila and philosophers but lacked capable defenders. Ghazali saw an opportunity to gain glory by attacking the philosophers. Then the editor cites two key passages from Ghazali's later works where Ghazali explicitly admits that during the Tahafut period, his intentions were not pure but driven by a desire for status and fame.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical need
Read the context: Sunnis felt threatened by Mu'tazila and philosophers but had no one to refute the philosophers with knowledge. This created an opportunity for a champion.
2
Read Ghazali's own words
Focus on the two quotes from Ghazali. The first is from when he was about to leave Baghdad for Sufi retreat: he admits his teaching intention was not pure for God but for status and fame. The second is from after his retreat: he acknowledges that during the time he wrote Tahafut, he spread knowledge for worldly gain.
These are direct quotations from Ghazali himself.
3
Connect evidence to the claim
Consider how these quotes support the editor's thesis. Ghazali's own self-critique shows that his earlier scholarly activity, including writing Tahafut, was motivated by a desire for reputation.

We are not doing injustice to Ghazali when we say about him: that in that period when he wrote the book 'al-Tahafut' he was seeking status, fame, and renown. Behold, he speaks about himself in that period saying: 'Then I reflected on my intention in teaching – meaning when he intended to leave Baghdad to apply the Sufi method upon himself – and behold it was not pure for the sake of God Almighty, but rather its impetus and mover was seeking status and spread of fame, so I was certain that I was on the brink of a crumbling bank, and that I was on the verge of the Fire if I did not occupy myself with rectifying my state.' And he says in another place – when he returned after the retreat to resume teaching in Nishapur: 'I know that even if I have returned to spreading knowledge, I have not returned; for returning is a return to what was, and in that time – referring to the days of Baghdad when he wrote the book al-Tahafut – I was spreading the knowledge by which status is earned, and calling to it by my words and deeds, and that was my intention and purpose.' The conclusion from all this is that the book 'al-Tahafut' is not suitable to be taken as a reference for depicting the ideas of Ghazali which he believes and meets God with; rather, these must be derived from...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a modern editor's argument about the reliability of Ghazali's 'Tahafut al-Falasifa' as a source for his final theological positions. The editor first states that Ghazali's true opinions can only be derived from certain books, not from 'al-Tahafut'. He then provides a historical context: Sunnis were troubled by Mu'tazila and philosophers but lacked capable defenders; Ghazali saw an opportunity to gain fame and satisfied his vanity by attacking the philosophers. The editor then supports this claim with two direct quotations from Ghazali's own later writings. The first quote is from when Ghazali was leaving Baghdad to adopt Sufism: he reflects that his teaching intention was not pure for God but driven by status and fame. The second quote is from after his retreat, when he returned to teaching in Nishapur: he admits that during the time of writing 'al-Tahafut', he was spreading knowledge that earns status, and that was his intention. The editor concludes that 'al-Tahafut' should not be used as a reference for Ghazali's true beliefs.

Key Terms
أهل السنة
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة
الجاه
الصيت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali's Tahafut was a purely academic, dispassionate refutation.
Correction: This passage argues it was driven by personal ambition, as Ghazali himself later admitted.

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes Ghazali's own admission about his intentions during the period he wrote Tahafut?
A) He was motivated by a desire to preserve Sunni orthodoxy
B) He was seeking status and spread of fame
C) He was following a direct command from the Caliph
D) He was testing his own philosophical skills

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's own later writings reveal that his Tahafut was a product of impure intentions, casting doubt on its reliability as a source for his final beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in learning or teaching Islamic knowledge, do you ever catch yourself secretly hoping for recognition or praise? What specific action can you take today to redirect that intention solely for Allah?
?
Ghazali, one of the greatest scholars, admitted that his early work was driven by a desire for status and fame. If you were to honestly examine your own motives in studying this lesson, what would you find? Are you ready to confront the possibility that your own intentions may not be pure?
?
If you were to die today and meet Allah with the intentions you had while reading this lesson, would you be content? What would need to change in your heart for you to be able to say, 'I sought Your Face alone'?
