The Second Division: Non-Essential Disputes
Ghazali classifies the second division and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second division of disagreements that do not clash with religious fundamentals, using eclipses as an example.

Core Idea
Not every disagreement undermines faith.

Ghazali continues his classification of disagreements with the philosophers. He now identifies a category of disputes that do not affect the core principles of religion. Using astronomical explanations of eclipses as concrete examples, he shows that some topics can be accepted as established science without threatening faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining the second division
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the definition and the examples.
Notice that Ghazali explicitly says this division does not clash with 'usul al-din'.
2
Identify the two examples given
The lunar eclipse explanation and the solar eclipse explanation are provided. Note the scientific details.
These examples show the kind of knowledge Ghazali considers harmless.
3
Understand why Ghazali does not refute them
He says 'no purpose attaches' and that such matters are not part of verifying prophethood.
This is a key methodological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثانى : ما لا يصدم مذهبهم فيه أصلاً من أصول الدين، وليس من ضرورة تصديق الأنبياء والرسل - صلوات الله عليهم - منازعتهم فيه، كقولهم : إن الكسوف القمرى عبارة عن انمحاء ضوء القمر بتوسط الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، من حيث إنه يقتبس نوره من الشمس، والأرض كرة، والسماء محيط بها من الجوانب، فإذا وقع القمر في ظل الأرض، انقطع عنه نور الشمس؛ وكقولهم : إن كسوف الشمس معناه وقوع جرم القمر بين الناظر وبين الشمس، وذلك عند اجتماعهما في العقدتين على دقيقة واحدة من وهذه الفن أيضاً لسنا نخوض فى إبطاله، إذ لا يتعلق به غرض، ومن ظن أن المناظرة في إبطال هذا من الدين، فقد جنى على الدين وضعف أمره، فإن هذه الأمور تقوم عليها براهين هندسية حسابية، لا يبقى معها ريبة، فمن يطلع عليها، ويتحقق أدلتها، حتى يخبر بسببها عن وقت الكسوفين، وقدرهما، ومدة بقائهما إلى الانجلاء، إذا قيل له : إن هذا على خلاف الشرع، لم يسترب فيه وإنما يسترب فى الشرع، وضرر الشرع ممن ينصره لا بطريقه، أكثر ضرره ممن يطعن فيه بطريقه؛ وهو كما قيل : عدو عاقل خير من صديق جاهل . فإن قيل : فقد قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : « إن الشمس والقمر لآيتان من آيات الله، لا يخسفان لموت أحد ولا لحياته، فإذا رأيتم ذلك فافزعوا إلى ذكر الله والصلاة )، فكيف يلائم هذا ما قالوه ؟، قلنا : وليس في هذا ما يناقض ما قالوه، إذ ليس فيه إلا نفى وقوع الكسوف لموت أحد أو لحياته
The second division: that which does not clash with the principles of religion at all, and it is not necessary for verifying the truth of the prophets and messengers - peace be upon them - to dispute with them about it, such as their saying: The lunar eclipse is the effacement of the moon's light due to the earth's interposition between it and the sun, insofar as it borrows its light from the sun, and the earth is a sphere, and the sky encompasses it from all sides. So when the moon falls into the earth's shadow, the sun's light is cut off from it. And such as their saying: The solar eclipse means the interposition of the moon's body between the observer and the sun, and that occurs when they both meet at the two nodes on exactly one minute. And this art also we do not delve into refuting it, for no purpose attaches to it. And whoever thinks that disputing about refuting this is part of religion has wronged religion and weakened its affairs. For these matters are established by geometrical and arithmetical proofs, concerning which no doubt remains. So whoever studies them and verifies their proofs, so that he can inform about the time of the two eclipses, their extent, and the duration of their remaining until clearing, if it is said to him: This contradicts the Sacred Law, he will not doubt it but rather will doubt the Sacred Law. And the harm to the Sacred Law from one who supports it without its proper method is greater than the harm from one who attacks it through its method. And it is as was said: An intelligent enemy is better than an ignorant friend. For if it is said: The Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Indeed the sun and the moon are two signs from the signs of God; they are not eclipsed for the death of anyone nor for his life. So if you see that, hasten to the remembrance of God and prayer.' How does this fit with what they say? We say: There is nothing in this that contradicts what they say, for it only contains the negation of the eclipse occurring for the death of anyone or his life.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a classification and an argument. Ghazali presents the second division of disagreement: issues that do not affect the fundamentals of religion (usul al-din) and are not necessary for verifying prophethood. He gives the examples of astronomical explanations for lunar and solar eclipses: the lunar eclipse is due to the earth blocking sunlight, and the solar eclipse is due to the moon passing between the earth and sun. He states that he will not refute these claims because they rely on geometrical and arithmetical proofs that are certain. He warns that those who think debating these matters is part of religion are harming it. The harm to religion from an ignorant supporter is greater than from an intelligent enemy. He then addresses an objection: the hadith says the sun and moon are signs of God and are not eclipsed for anyone's death or life. How does that agree with the astronomical explanation? Ghazali answers that the hadith only negates that eclipses occur for a human cause, but does not negate the natural phenomenon itself. Thus, there is no contradiction.

Key Terms
كسوف قمري
كسوف شمسي
أصول الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second division of disagreements according to Ghazali?
A) Disagreements that attack the fundamentals of religion
B) Disagreements that do not clash with religious principles
C) Disagreements about the existence of God
D) Disagreements about prophethood

Key Takeaway
Some disagreements with the philosophers do not affect religion and can be left aside if supported by proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a scientific explanation that seems to conflict with a literal reading of scripture, do you immediately reject it or do you examine whether it truly clashes with the fundamentals of your faith? What would it take for you to accept a natural explanation like Ghazali did with eclipses?
?
Is there any practice or belief in your life that you treat as a non-negotiable part of your religion, but upon reflection might actually be a non-essential matter that you could let go of without weakening your faith? What makes it essential or non-essential according to the standard of this lesson?

The Harm of Ignorant Support and Reconciling Hadith with Science
Ghazali warns against ignorant defenders and reconciles the hadith with science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attacking established science harms religion more than an intelligent critique, and how to reconcile the hadith about eclipses with astronomical explanations.

Core Idea
An intelligent enemy is better than an ignorant friend.

Ghazali now addresses a serious concern: some overzealous defenders of religion attack established scientific knowledge, thereby weakening faith. He warns that such ignorance is more harmful than reasoned opposition. Then he anticipates a hadith objection and shows how it does not contradict the astronomical explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the warning against ignorant supporters

The hadith is from the Prophet (peace be upon him).
3
Understand Ghazali's reconciliation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith 'they are not eclipsed for the death of anyone' disproves the astronomical explanation of eclipses.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that the hadith only negates a superstitious cause (e.g., eclipses happening because of someone's death), not the natural phenomenon described by astronomy.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, who causes more harm to religion?
A) An intelligent enemy who attacks religion properly
B) An ignorant friend who supports religion without proper method
C) A philosopher who denies all religious truths
D) A scholar who debates calmly

Key Takeaway
Supporting religion by denying established knowledge harms faith more than reasoned criticism, and the hadith on eclipses does not conflict with science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a scientific fact that seems to contradict a religious text, do you immediately reject the science or do you pause to reconsider your understanding of the text? Which approach does Ghazali's example with the eclipse hadith call you to adopt, and why might your default reaction be more harmful than you think?
?
Have you ever defended Islam on a matter you did not fully understand, relying on slogans rather than knowledge? How did that feel afterwards? In light of Ghazali's warning, what specific step will you take this week to ensure you don't become an 'ignorant friend' to the religion?
?
The passage says 'An intelligent enemy is better than an ignorant friend.' Who in your life—perhaps even yourself—might be unintentionally harming Islam through zeal without knowledge? What is one concrete change you will make in your approach to defending your faith, starting today?

Ruling on Divine Names: The Linguistic and Fiqh Basis
Ghazali presents the two-stage method for determining the permissibility of using a term for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological principle that the permissibility of applying a term to God depends first on linguistic validity, and if language permits, then on Sharia rulings derived from apparent texts.

Core Idea
Naming God requires both linguistic permission and Sharia approval.

In this passage, Ghazali outlines the criteria for determining whether it is permissible to use a particular term when referring to God. He establishes a two-step process: first, consult the language to see if the word can carry the intended meaning; if yes, then investigate the Sharia to see if using that word is allowed or prohibited based on the apparent meanings of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the sequence Ghazali presents.
The passage is the first part of the original chunk, covering the methodological rule.
2
Identify the two steps
Notice that Ghazali mentions two investigations: linguistic and fiqh. Identify what each step entails.
3
Reflect on the source of rulings
Understand that the prohibition and permission of names come from the apparent meanings (zawahir) of Sharia, not from arbitrary custom.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه ، رجع الكلام فى التعبير باسم الجوهر عن هذا المعنى ، إلى البحث عن اللغة ، وإن سوغت اللغة إطلاقه ، رجع جواز إطلاقه في الشرع ، إلى المباحث الفقهية ، فإن تحريم إطلاق الأسامى وإباحتها يؤخذ مما يدل عليه ظواهر الشرع . ولعلك تقول : هذا إنما ذكره المتكلمون فى الصفات ، ولم يورده الفقهاء في فن الفقه ؛ فلا ينبغى أن تلتبس عليك حقائق الأمور بالعادات والمراسم ، فقد عرفت أنه بحث عن جواز التلفظ بلفظ صدق معناه على المسمى به ، فهو كالبحث عن جواز فعل من الأفعال
Upon it, the discussion about expressing by the name 'jawhar' (substance) for this meaning returns to linguistic investigation; and if language permits its usage, the permissibility of its usage in the Sharia returns to fiqh investigations; for the prohibition of names and their permission is taken from what the apparent meanings of the Sharia indicate. Perhaps you shall say: This was only mentioned by the mutakallimun (theologians) concerning the attributes, and the fuqaha (jurists) did not mention it in the discipline of fiqh; so you should not confuse the realities of things with customs and formalities, for you have known that it is a discussion about the permissibility of uttering a word whose meaning is true of what is named, so it is like the discussion about the permissibility of an action from among actions.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the ruling on applying the term 'jawhar' (substance) to God. Ghazali explains that the debate about using this term for a particular intended meaning (presumably a divine attribute) refers back to linguistic investigation: if the Arabic language allows it, then the Sharia ruling of permissibility or prohibition depends on fiqh, because the prohibition or allowance of names is derived from the apparent texts (zawahir) of the Sharia. Then an objection is raised: the mutakallimun (theologians) discussed this in the context of divine attributes, but the jurists (fuqaha) did not include it in fiqh. Ghazali responds that one should not confuse the realities of things with customary conventions; it is a discussion about the permissibility of uttering a word that truthfully applies to what it names, which is analogous to discussing the permissibility of an action. Thus the ruling on naming is similar to the ruling on actions, and it belongs to fiqh.

Key Terms
الجوهر
اللغة
الشرع
الفقه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a term is linguistically correct, it is automatically permissible in Sharia.
Correction: Ghazali shows that linguistic permission is only the first step; the Sharia ruling must also be derived from apparent texts.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the first step in determining the permissibility of using a term like 'jawhar' for God?
A) Consulting the apparent meanings of Sharia
B) Checking linguistic validity
C) Looking at the practice of the fuqaha
D) Examining the consensus of the mutakallimun

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of using a name for God depends first on linguistic validity and then on Sharia evidence from apparent texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Objection: Is This a Fiqh or Kalam Issue?
Ghazali responds to an objection about the scope of fiqh.

Learning Objective

Naming God is a fiqhi issue because it concerns the permissibility of an utterance.

Defending Eclipse Prayer: Hadith Criticism and Rational Interpretation
Ghazali defends prayer during eclipse and refutes a hadith about God's manifestation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the legitimacy of the eclipse prayer by questioning the authenticity of a hadith and arguing for the priority of rational interpretation.

Core Idea
Authentic hadiths must be reliably transmitted; rational proofs can interpret texts.

Ghazali addresses an objection that a hadith about God's manifestation causing eclipses would undermine the religious command to pray during an eclipse. He refutes the authenticity of that hadith addition and argues that even if authentic, rational interpretation is preferable. This lesson explores his method of defending Islamic law through hadith criticism and rational reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
The passage is dense; take your time to follow the argument.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Ghazali first uses analogy: if prayer is commanded at regular times, why not during eclipse? Then he refutes the hadith by questioning its transmission and allowing interpretation.
The second part is the core of the lesson.
3
Reflect on the broader methodology
Consider how Ghazali balances textual evidence (hadith) with rational certainty. Why does he say interpretation is easier than denying certain matters?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأمر بالصلاة عنده ؛ والشرع الذي يأمر بالصلاة عند الزوال والغروب والطلوع ، من أين يبعد منه أن يأمر عند الكسوف بها استحباباً ؟ فإن قيل : فقد روى أنه قال فى آخر الحديث : « ولكن الله إذا تجلى لشيء خضع له » ، فيدل على أن الكسوف خضوع بسبب التجلى ؛ قلنا هذه الزائدة لم يصح نقلها ، فيجب تكذيب ناقلها ؛ وإنما المروى ما ذكرناه ، كيف ولو كان صحيحاً ، لكان تأويله أهون من مكابرة أمور قطعية ، فكم من ظواهر أولت بالأدلة العقلية التي لا تنتهى فى الوضوح إلى هذا الحد . وأعظم ما يفرح به : ناصر الشرع ، بأن هذا وأمثاله على خلاف الشرع يصرح الملاحدة ، أن فيسهل عليه طريق إبطال الشرع ، إن كان شرطه أمثال ذلك .
And the command to pray according to him; and the Law which commands prayer at noon, sunset, and sunrise, how far-fetched is it that it should command prayer during eclipse as a recommendation? If it is said: It has been narrated that he said at the end of the hadith: 'But when God manifests to something, it submits,' so it indicates that the eclipse is a submission due to manifestation. We say: This addition has not been authentically transmitted, so its transmitter must be rejected; what is narrated is only what we mentioned. Moreover, even if it were authentic, its interpretation would be easier than denying certain matters. How many apparent texts have been interpreted by rational proofs that do not reach this level of clarity? And the greatest joy for the supporter of the Law is that this and its like show that the heretics declare it contrary to the Law, so it becomes easy for him to invalidate the Law if its condition is like that.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument defending the possibility of the Law commanding prayer during an eclipse. The author first argues from analogy: if the Law commands prayer at regular times, why would it not command it during an eclipse as a recommended act? Then he raises an objection: a hadith says that God's manifestation causes submission, implying the eclipse is natural, not a time for prayer. The author refutes this by questioning the authenticity of the hadith addition, stating it is not reliably transmitted and must be rejected. Even if it were authentic, he argues, its interpretation is easier than denying rational certainties. He concludes by noting that such objections actually help the supporter of the Law by showing that heretics reject the Law based on similar weak grounds, making it easy to refute them.

Key Terms
صلاة الكسوف
تجلى
ظواهر
أدلة عقلية
ملاحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All hadiths about divine manifestation are to be taken literally without questioning authenticity.
Correction: Ghazali shows that a hadith addition may be rejected if not reliably transmitted, and even if authentic, rational interpretation is necessary to avoid conflict with certain knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the hadith addition about God's manifestation during eclipse?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) Its transmission is not authentic.
C) It is rationally impossible.
D) It is only metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends the eclipse prayer by rejecting the authenticity of a contradicting hadith and affirming that rational interpretation of texts is always preferable to denying rational certainties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious practice that seems questionable due to a weak hadith, do you investigate its authenticity or simply dismiss it? How will Ghazali's method of verifying transmissions change your approach from now on?

Three Principles of Religion to Refute
Ghazali classifies the types of disputes and focuses on foundational religious principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which three principles of religion Ghazali identifies as crucial to refute in the debate with philosophers, and why focusing on these is a strategic methodological choice.

Core Idea
Ghazali narrows the battlefield to three essential doctrines.

Ghazali now defines the scope of his critique. He lists three foundational beliefs that the philosophers deny: the creation of the world, God's attributes, and bodily resurrection. He insists that refutation must be directed at these core principles, not at peripheral issues. This methodological focus ensures that the debate tackles the heart of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its concise style, then read the literal translation. Notice the three items listed.
2
Identify the three principles
List the three principles Ghazali mentions. Ask yourself: why are these considered 'principles of religion'?
They are: world's origination, attributes of the Creator, and bodily resurrection.
3
Reflect on the methodological point
Ghazali says 'this art and its likes' should be refuted, not other matters. Consider why focusing on these three is strategically important for defending Islamic doctrine.
This avoids wasting effort on peripheral arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث : يتعلق النزاع فيه بأصل من أصول الدين ، كالقول في حدوث العالم ، وصفات الصانع ، وبيان حشر الأجساد والأبدان ؛ وقد أنكروا ذلك جميع فهذا الفن ونظائره هو الذى ينبغى أن يظهر فساد مذهبهم فيه دون ما عداه
The third section: The dispute in it pertains to a principle of religion, such as the statement on the origination of the world, the attributes of the Creator, and the explanation of the resurrection of bodies and persons; and they have denied all of that. So this art and its likes are what should be shown to be invalid in their doctrine, not other than it.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of disputes that are worth engaging. The author identifies three fundamental principles of religion that the philosophers deny: the creation of the world, the attributes of God, and the bodily resurrection. He says the refutation should focus on these core issues and similar ones, not on other matters. This is a strategic methodological point: do not waste effort on peripheral arguments; concentrate on the essentials that define faith.

Ghazali's Dialectical Method: Refuting Philosophers by Adopting Different Schools
Ghazali describes his refutation methodology using multiple schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's unique dialectical strategy in Tahafut al-Falasifa—how he refutes philosophers by adopting various theological schools without committing to any.

Core Idea
Ghazali refutes philosophers by using their own premises—borrowing from any school he needs.

Ghazali introduces his method in this crucial passage. He does not argue from a fixed position but rather adopts the doctrines of other theological schools to expose contradictions in the philosophers' arguments. This approach is both clever and controversial, as it might seem like he is shifting grounds. But as we'll see, it is a powerful dialectical tool.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Familiarize yourself with Ghazali's own words. Notice his statement of purpose and his explicit method.
Pay attention to the phrase 'دخول مطالب منكر' – he enters as a questioner, not a claimant.
2
Identify the three schools mentioned
Ghazali names three theological schools: Mu'tazila, Karramiyya, and Waqifiyya. Think about why he chose these specific schools and what they represent.
Each school has distinct doctrines; using them allows him to test philosophers from multiple angles.
3
Understand the role of the questioner
Ghazali says he enters objection as a 'questioner who demands proof' not as a 'claimant who affirms.' Consider how this stance strengthens his refutation by avoiding commitment to a counter-position.
4
Reflect on the broader strategy
Consider how this method of 'adopting different schools' makes his refutation more powerful and less vulnerable. Does it expose the philosophers' contradictions more effectively?
This is known as dialectical argument from the opponent's premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة ثالثة
ليعلم أن المقصود تنبيه من حسن اعتقاده في الفلاسفة ، وظن أن مسالكهم نقية عن التناقض ، ببيان وجوه تهافتهم ، فلذلك أنا لا أدخل في الاعتراض عليهم إلا دخول مطالب منكر ، لا دخول مدع مثبت ؛ فأبطل عليهم ما اعتقدوه مقطوعاً بإلزامات مختلفة ، فألزمهم تارة مذهب المعتزلة وأخرى مذهب الكرامية ، وطوراً مذهب الواقفية ، ولا أتنهض ذابا عن مذهب مخصوص
Third introduction: Let it be known that the purpose is to alert one who has a good opinion of the philosophers and thinks that their ways are free from contradiction, by exposing the aspects of their inconsistency. Therefore, I do not enter into objection against them except as a questioner who demands proof, not as one who claims and affirms. So I invalidate what they consider certain by various obligatory arguments, and I bind them sometimes with the doctrine of the Mu'tazila, sometimes with the doctrine of the Karramiyya, and sometimes with the doctrine of the Waqifiyya, and I do not stand as a defender of a particular doctrine.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction explaining the author's strategy in refuting the philosophers. He states his goal: to awaken those who have a favorable view of philosophy and think it is free of contradictions. His approach is not to act as a defender of a specific school, but rather as a skeptic who demands proof. He will adopt the doctrines of different Islamic theological schools—Mu'tazila, Karramiyya, and Waqifiyya—to force the philosophers into contradictions. By doing so, he invalidates their claims without committing to any particular school himself. This is a dialectical method using the opponents' own premises against them.

Key Terms
تهافت
ملاحدة
اعتراض
مذهب المعتزلة
مذهب الكرامية
مذهب الواقفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali only uses Ash'ari theology to refute philosophers.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states he adopts the doctrines of Mu'tazila, Karramiyya, and Waqifiyya, not just his own school.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's stated method in refuting the philosophers?
A) Defending the Ash'ari school exclusively.
B) Adopting different schools' doctrines to show contradictions.
C) Refuting all their claims using pure reason alone.
D) Ignoring their arguments and relying on scripture.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes philosophers not by defending a single school, but by adopting various schools' positions to expose internal contradictions in philosophical thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with someone, do you seek to understand their starting point as Ghazali does, or do you rush to defend your own position? What would change if you approached disagreements as a 'questioner' rather than a defender?
?
Ghazali uses doctrines he does not personally hold to expose error. Are you willing to temporarily step outside your own school of thought to see the truth in an argument? How might that affect your humility?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly. Could you honestly adopt an opposing view for the sake of argument to test its consistency? Why does that feel uncomfortable? What does that discomfort reveal about your attachment to being 'right'?

Ghazali's Method: Exposing Philosophers' Contradictions Without Partisanship
Ghazali states his purpose and method for exposing philosophers' contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's strategic method of exposing the philosophers' incoherence by using the doctrines of various Islamic sects against them without defending any single school.

Core Idea
Ghazali unites all Islamic sects against the philosophers by imposing their own doctrines.

Imam Ghazali begins his Tahafut al-Falasifa with a methodological introduction that clarifies his purpose and approach. He is not writing to defend a particular theological school, but to alert those who hold the philosophers in high esteem that their arguments are full of contradictions. In this passage, he explains his strategy of using the positions of different Islamic sects to refute the philosophers, turning all sects into a single confederacy against a common enemy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing Ghazali's description of his purpose and the key terms.
Pay attention to the phrase 'مطالب منكر' (a questioner who denies) — it defines his approach.
2
Identify the key terms
Note the terms like تهافت, مطالب منكر, إلزامات, إلب, أصول الدين. These are central to understanding his method.
The term 'إلب' means a confederacy or united group.
3
Understand the method of imposing doctrines
Ghazali does not defend any madhhab; he uses each madhhab as a weapon. See how he mentions Mu'tazila, Karramiyya, and Waqifiyya.
He imposes their doctrines on the philosophers to expose contradictions.
4
Grasp the strategic unity
Ghazali calls for unity among sects despite their differences, because the philosophers attack the fundamentals of religion (أصول الدين).
The phrase 'فلنتظاهر عليهم' means 'let us unite against them.'
5
Reflect on the closing wisdom
Ghazali ends with a proverb: 'at times of distress, grudges disappear.' This encapsulates his approach of setting aside internal differences.
Think about how this principle applies to broader Islamic unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة ثالثة ليعلم أن المقصود تنبيه من حسن اعتقاده في الفلاسفة ، وظن أن مسالكهم نقية عن التناقض ، ببيان وجوه تهافتهم ، فلذلك أنا لا أدخل في الاعتراض عليهم إلا دخول مطالب منكر ، لا دخول مدع مثبت ؛ فأبطل عليهم ما اعتقدوه مقطوعاً بإلزامات مختلفة ، فألزمهم تارة مذهب المعتزلة (۱) وأخرى مذهب الكرامية (٢ ) ، وطوراً مذهب الواقفية (۳) ، ولا أتنهض ذابا عن مذهب مخصوص بل أجعل جميع الفرق إلباً (۱) واحداً عليهم ، فإن سائر الفرق ربما خالفونا في التفصيل ، وهؤلاء يتعرضون لأصول الدين ، فلنتظاهر عليهم فعند الشدائد تذهب الأحقاد .
Third introduction: Let it be known that the purpose is to alert one who has good belief in the philosophers and thinks that their ways are free from contradiction, by explaining the aspects of their incoherence. Therefore, I do not enter into objection against them except as a questioner who denies, not as a claimant who affirms. So I invalidate what they believe definitively by various impositions. Thus I impose upon them sometimes the doctrine of the Mu'tazila, sometimes the doctrine of the Karramiyya, and sometimes the doctrine of the Waqifiyya, and I do not rise as a defender of a particular doctrine. Rather, I make all the sects a single confederacy against them, for the rest of the sects may differ from us in details, but these [philosophers] attack the fundamentals of religion. So let us unite against them, for at times of distress, grudges disappear.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement by Imam Ghazali in the third introduction of his Tahafut al-Falasifa. He begins by stating the purpose: to alert someone who has a good opinion of the philosophers and believes their arguments are free from contradiction. He will expose the aspects of their incoherence (تهافتهم). Ghazali then explains his method: he will not object to them as someone who affirmatively defends a position, but rather as a questioner who rejects their claims (مطالب منكر, lit. 'a demander who denies'). He will invalidate their certain beliefs by imposing various contradictory positions on them. Specifically, he will sometimes force them into the position of the Mu'tazila, sometimes the Karramiyya, and sometimes the Waqifiyya. He emphasizes that he does not stand up as a defender of any particular school (مذهب مخصوص). Instead, he makes all the different Islamic sects a single confederacy (إلباً واحداً) against the philosophers. The reason: other sects may differ with him in details, but the philosophers attack the very fundamentals of religion (أصول الدين). Therefore, he calls for unity against them, because at times of distress, grudges disappear. This shows Ghazali's strategic approach: he uses the internal disagreements among Islamic sects not to refute them but to turn them against a common enemy.

مطالب منكر
إلزامات
إلب
أصول الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is defending the Mu'tazila or another specific school.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly says he does not defend any particular madhhab; he uses each school's doctrines as a tool to refute the philosophers.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's stated purpose in the third introduction?
A) To defend the Mu'tazila against the philosophers
B) To expose the contradictions of the philosophers without defending any school
C) To prove that all Islamic sects are united
D) To show that the philosophers are correct in their beliefs

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's method is to expose the philosophers' contradictions by imposing the doctrines of various Islamic sects on them, uniting all sects against a common enemy without defending any single school.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own disagreements, do you seek to prove your own school right or to expose falsehood? Ghazali's method is to set aside partisanship for the sake of truth. Can you identify a belief you hold so strongly that you would be unwilling to use any argument (even from an opponent) to defeat a greater error? How might that limit your effectiveness in defending the fundamentals of faith?
?
Ghazali unites all sects against the philosophers, setting aside grudges because the fundamentals are under attack. Is there a common enemy threatening the fundamentals of Islam today that requires you to unite with those you disagree with in details? What grudges do you need to set aside for that cause?

Refutation of Mathematics as a Prerequisite for Theology
Ghazali refutes the claim that mathematics and logic are prerequisites for divine sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the claim that mathematics and logic are prerequisites for understanding divine sciences.

Core Idea
Knowing God does not require knowing arithmetic or geometry.

In this passage, Imam Ghazali exposes a subtle trick used by philosophers to deflect objections. They claim that divine sciences are so deep that one must first master mathematics and logic to even engage with their arguments. Ghazali dismantles this claim by separating arithmetic and geometry, showing their irrelevance to theology, and uses vivid analogies to make his point clear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' trick
Read the beginning of the passage to understand the tactic Ghazali is refuting: that philosophers claim divine sciences require prior study of mathematics and logic.
Notice how this trick intimidates the questioner into silence.
2
Analyze the refutation of arithmetic
Ghazali argues that arithmetic (discrete quantity) has no connection to theology. He compares it to medicine requiring grammar, which is obviously absurd.
This shows that demanding arithmetic is an unreasonable prerequisite.
3
Analyze the refutation of geometry
Even if we accept the geometric descriptions of the heavens, Ghazali says they do not affect theological inquiry. Their conclusions are irrelevant to proving God's attributes.
Geometry deals with continuous quantity and describes the physical world, not the divine.
4
Reflect on the analogies
Ghazali gives two analogies: knowing a house requires a builder does not require knowing its number of beams, and knowing an onion is created does not require knowing its layers. Consider why these analogies are effective.
Both analogies illustrate that essential knowledge does not depend on detailed mathematical information.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة رابعة
من عظائم حيل هؤلاء فى الاستدراج - إذا أورد عليهم إشكال في معرض الحجاج - قولهم : إن هذه العلوم الإلهية غامضة خفية ، وهي أعصى العلوم على الأفهام الذكية ، ولا يتوصل إلى معرفة الجواب عن هذه الإشكالات إلا بتقديم الرياضيات والمنطقيات ؛ فمن يقلدهم في كفرهم ، إن خطر له إشكال على مذهبهم ، يحسن الظن بهم ويقول : لا شك فى أن علومهم مشتملة على حله ، وإنما يعسر على دركه ، لأنى لم أحكم المنطقيات ، ولم أحصل الرياضيات فنتمول : أما الرياضيات التى هى نظر فى الكم المنفصل – وهو الحساب – فلا تعلق للإلهيات به ، وقول القائل : إن فهم الإلهيات يحتاج إليها ؛ خرق ، كقول القائل : إن الطب والنحو واللغة يحتاج إليها ، أو الحساب يحتاج إلى الطب؛ وأما الهندسيات التي هي نظر في الكم المتصل فيرجع حاصله إلى بيان أن السموات وما تحتها إلى المركز ، كرى الشكل ، وبيان عدد طبقاتها ، وبيان عدد الأكر المتحركة فى الأفلاك ، وبيان مقدار حركاتها ؛ فلنسلم لهم جميع جدلاً أو اعتقاداً فلا يحتاجون إلى إقامة البراهين عليه – ولا يقدح ذلك في شيء من النظر الإلهى ، وهو كقول القائل : العلم بأن هذا البيت حصل بصنع بناء عالم مريد قادر حي ، يفتقر إلى أن يعرف أن البيت مسدس مثمن ، وأن يعرف عدد جذوعه ، وعدد لبناته ، وهو هذيان لا يخفى فساده ، وكقول القائل : لا يعرف كون هذه البصلة حادثة ، ما لم يعرف عدد طبقاتها
Fourth Introduction. Among the great tricks of these people in drawing others in—when an objection is raised against them in the context of argumentation—is their saying: 'These divine sciences are obscure and hidden, and they are the most difficult of sciences for intelligent minds, and one cannot reach knowledge of the answer to these objections except by first studying mathematics and logic.' So whoever imitates them in their unbelief, if an objection to their doctrine occurs to him, he thinks well of them and says: 'There is no doubt that their sciences contain its solution, but it is difficult for me to grasp because I have not mastered logic nor acquired mathematics.' So we say: As for mathematics which is the study of discrete quantity—that is arithmetic—there is no connection of divine sciences with it. And the statement of one who says that understanding divine sciences requires it is absurd, like the statement of one who says that medicine, grammar, and language require it, or that arithmetic requires medicine. As for geometry, which is the study of continuous quantity, its outcome reduces to showing that the heavens and what is beneath them to the center are spherical in shape, and showing the number of their layers, and the number of moving spheres in the orbs, and the magnitude of their motions. So let us concede all of that to them, either dialectically or as a belief—they do not need to establish proofs for it—and that does not invalidate anything of divine inquiry. And it is like the statement of one who says: Knowledge that this house came about by the craftsmanship of a knowing, willing, powerful, living builder requires knowing that the house is hexagonal, octagonal, and knowing the number of its beams and its bricks, which is nonsense whose corruption is not hidden. And like the statement of one who says: One cannot know that this onion is originated unless he knows the number of its layers.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a common philosophical tactic. Ghazali identifies a trick: when philosophers are challenged, they claim that divine sciences are extremely difficult and cannot be understood without prior training in mathematics and logic. This intimidates the questioner, who then doubts his own ability and refrains from objecting. Ghazali counters by separating arithmetic (discrete quantity) and geometry (continuous quantity). Arithmetic, he argues, has no relevance to theology; demanding it is as absurd as saying medicine needs grammar or that arithmetic needs medicine. Geometry, though describing the spherical heavens and their motions, is irrelevant to proving divine truths; conceding these findings does not affect theological inquiry. He gives two analogies: knowing a house was built by a wise builder does not require knowing its exact shape or number of beams; knowing an onion is created does not require knowing its number of layers. Both analogies show that knowledge of God's existence and attributes does not depend on mathematical details.

المنطقيات
الكم المنفصل
الكم المتصل
الهندسيات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali rejects all mathematics, but he only rejects its necessity for theology.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny the validity of mathematics; he argues it is not a prerequisite for understanding divine matters.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' trick that Ghazali refutes in this passage?
A) They claim that divine sciences are too difficult for anyone to understand
B) They claim that mathematics and logic are prerequisites for understanding divine sciences
C) They claim that only they have access to divine sciences
D) They claim that divine sciences are unnecessary

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that theological truths do not depend on mathematical knowledge; mathematics is irrelevant to proving God's existence and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Think of an area where you have trusted experts simply because they seemed more knowledgeable. Is there a truth from this passage that calls you to re-evaluate that trust and rely more directly on the clear signs of God's creation?

Ghazali Exposes the Philosophers' Deceit: Mathematics as a False Prerequisite
Ghazali exposes the fourth introduction: the philosophers' trick of requiring mathematics before divine science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that mathematics is necessary for divine science, and why this claim is a deceitful tactic.

Core Idea
Mathematics is irrelevant to divine science; requiring it is a trick to avoid discussion.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a cunning argument used by philosophers to ward off objections. They claim that divine sciences are so obscure that only those who have mastered mathematics and logic can grasp the answers. Ghazali dismantles this claim by showing that mathematics has no actual connection to divine science, using simple and powerful analogies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of Ghazali's argument come through before interpreting.
Notice the rhetorical structure: Ghazali first describes the trick, then refutes it point by point.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Pinpoint the exact claim: that divine sciences are hidden and require prior study of mathematics and logic. Understand why this is a 'deceit' according to Ghazali.
3
Separate arithmetic from geometry
Ghazali treats arithmetic (discrete quantity) and geometry (continuous quantity) separately. Observe the different ways he refutes each.
Arithmetic is dismissed as having no connection; geometry's results are granted but shown to be irrelevant.
4
Explore the analogies
Ghazali uses three analogies: the house, the onion, and the pomegranate. For each, ask: why does the analogy work? How does it expose the foolishness of the philosophers' requirement?
These analogies drive home the point that knowing a thing's existence does not require knowing its detailed attributes.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why Ghazali calls this a 'great trick'. How does this refutation protect the student of kalam from being misled?
This connects to the learning objective and prepares for future lessons on logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة رابعة
من عظائم حيل هؤلاء فى الاستدراج - إذا أورد عليهم إشكال في معرض الحجاج - قولهم : إن هذه العلوم الإلهية غامضة خفية ، وهي أعصى العلوم على الأفهام الذكية ، ولا يتوصل إلى معرفة الجواب عن هذه الإشكالات إلا بتقديم الرياضيات والمنطقيات ؛ فمن يقلدهم في كفرهم ، إن خطر له إشكال على مذهبهم ، يحسن الظن بهم ويقول : لا شك فى أن علومهم مشتملة على حله ، وإنما يعسر على دركه ، لأنى لم أحكم المنطقيات ، ولم أحصل الرياضيات فنتمول : أما الرياضيات التى هى نظر فى الكم المنفصل – وهو الحساب – فلا تعلق للإلهيات به ، وقول القائل : إن فهم الإلهيات يحتاج إليها ؛ خرق ، كقول القائل : إن الطب والنحو واللغة يحتاج إليها ، أو الحساب يحتاج إلى الطب ؛ وأما الهندسيات التي هي نظر في الكم المتصل فيرجع حاصله إلى بيان أن السموات وما تحتها إلى المركز ، كرى الشكل ، وبيان عدد طبقاتها ، وبيان عدد الأكر المتحركة فى الأفلاك ، وبيان مقدار حركاتها ؛ فلنسلم لهم جميع جدلاً أو اعتقاداً فلا يحتاجون إلى إقامة البراهين عليه – ولا يقدح ذلك في شيء من النظر الإلهى ، وهو كقول القائل : العلم بأن هذا البيت حصل بصنع بناء عالم مريد قادر حي ، يفتقر إلى أن يعرف أن البيت مسدس مثمن ، وأن يعرف عدد جذوعه ، وعدد لبناته ، وهو هذيان لا يخفى فساده ، وكقول القائل : لا يعرف كون هذه البصلة حادثة ، ما لم يعرف عدد طبقاتها ، ولا يعرف كون هذه الرمانة حادثة ، ما لم يعرف عدد حباتها وهو هجر من الكلام مستغث عند كل عاقل
A fourth introduction: Among the great tricks of these people in deceit: when an objection is raised against them in the context of argument, they say: 'These divine sciences are obscure and hidden, and they are the most resistant of sciences to intelligent minds. The knowledge of the answer to these objections is not attained except by prior study of mathematics and logics.' So whoever imitates them in their disbelief, if an objection occurs to him against their doctrine, he thinks well of them and says: 'There is no doubt that their sciences include its solution, but it is difficult for me to grasp because I have not mastered the logics nor acquired the mathematics.' So we say: As for mathematics, which is investigation of discrete quantity – that is arithmetic – there is no connection of the divine sciences to it. The statement of the one who says that understanding the divine sciences needs it is nonsense, like someone who says that medicine, grammar, and language need it, or arithmetic needs medicine. As for geometry, which is investigation of continuous quantity, its result reduces to stating that the heavens and what is beneath them to the center are spherical, stating their layers, stating the number of revolving spheres, and stating the amount of their motions. So we grant them all, either dialectically or as a belief, so they do not need to establish proofs of it – and that does not harm anything of divine science. It is like someone who says: The knowledge that this house came about by the work of a builder who is knowing, willing, powerful, and alive depends on knowing that the house is hexagonal or octagonal, and knowing the number of its beams and its bricks – and that is nonsense whose corruption is not hidden. And like someone who says: One does not know that this onion is originated unless he knows the number of its layers, nor does one know that this pomegranate is originated unless he knows the number of its seeds. That is idle talk, rejected by every rational person.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that mathematics is a prerequisite for divine science. Ghazali begins by describing the fourth introduction: the philosophers' deceitful tactic of claiming that divine sciences are extremely obscure and that only those who have mastered mathematics and logic can understand them. He then argues that arithmetic (discrete quantity) has no connection to divine science; it is like saying medicine needs grammar. Geometry (continuous quantity) yields only astronomical facts which Ghazali grants but asserts they do not affect divine science. He uses analogies: knowing a house exists does not require knowing its architectural details, and knowing an onion or pomegranate is created does not require counting its layers or seeds. The refutation is complete and establishes that mathematical prerequisites are irrelevant.

Correction: Ghazali shows that mathematics has no bearing on divine science; it is a deceitful obstacle, not a genuine prerequisite.

Mathematics is not a prerequisite for divine science; requiring it is a rhetorical trick to avoid discussion.

Logic: A Shared Science and Ghazali's Refutation Plan
Ghazali concedes logic's necessity, reveals it is kalam's 'Kitab al-Nazar', and plans to refute philosophers using their own terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's concession of logic's necessity, his claim that it is not exclusive to philosophers but known to mutakallimun as 'Kitab al-Nazar', and his strategic plan to refute philosophers using their own logical terminology and standards.

Core Idea
Ghazali uses the philosophers' own tool of logic to prove them inconsistent in divine sciences.

In this passage, Ghazali makes a crucial strategic move: he concedes the value of logic but immediately denies the philosophers' monopoly over it. He reveals that what they call 'logic' is simply the mutakallimun's 'Kitab al-Nazar' under a different name. He then announces his plan to adopt their exact terminology and rules in a special section of his book, precisely to demonstrate that they themselves cannot meet their own standards in metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the rhetorical structure: concession, then redefinition, then strategic plan.
Pay attention to the list of alternative names Ghazali gives for logic.
2
Identify Ghazali's concession
Ghazali begins by affirming the philosophers' statement about logic. What does he concede? Why is this important for his credibility?
3
Understand the redefinition
Ghazali lists three names mutakallimun use for logic. How does this strip the philosophers of exclusivity?
The names: Kitab al-Nazar, Kitab al-Jadal, Madarik al-Uqool.
4
Analyze the refutation strategy
Ghazali plans to use the philosophers' own logical terms and conditions. What specific logical works does he mention (Isagoge, Categories)?
He will show they fail their own criteria in divine sciences.
5
Consider the placement
Why does Ghazali postpone this logical section to the end? What is the role of his earlier work 'Mi'yaar al-'Ilm'?
Some readers can skip it; others need a background in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم قولهم : إن المنطقيات لابد من أحكامها هو صحيح ، ولكن المنطق ليس مخصوصاً بهم ، وإنما هو الأصل الذي نسميه في فن « الكلام » « كتاب النظر » فغير وا عبارته إلى المنطق تهويلا ، وقد نسميه ( كتاب الجدل » وقد نسميه مدارك العقول » فإذا سمع المتكايس المستضعف ، اسم المنطق ، ظن أنه فن غريب لا يعرفه المتكلمون ، ولا يطلع عليه إلا الفلاسفة ، ونحن لدفع هذا الخيال واستئصال هذه الحيلة فى الإضلال ، نرى أن نفرد القول في « مدارك العقول » في هذا الكتاب ، ونهجر فيه ألفاظ المتكلمين والأصوليين ، بل نوردها بعبارات المنطقيين ، ونصبها في قوالبهم ، ونقتفى آثارهم لفظاً لفظاً ونناظرهم في هذا الكتاب بلغتهم - أعنى بعباراتهم في المنطق - ونوضح أن ما شرطوه في صحة مادة القياس في قسم البرهان من المنطق ، وما شرطوه في صورته في كتاب القياس وما وضعوه من الأوضاع فى « إيساغوجي » و « قاطيغور ياس » التي هي من أجزاء المنطق ومقدماته ، لم يتمكنوا من الوفاء بشيء منه فى علومهم الإلهية . ولكننا نرى أن نورد ( مدارك العقول » فى آخر الكتاب ، فإنه كالآ لة لدرك مقصود الكتاب ، ولكن رب ناظر يستغنى عنه في الفهم ، فنؤخره حتى يعرض عنه من لا يحتاج إليه ، ومن لايفهم ألفاظنا في آحاد المسائل في الرد عليهم ، فينبغي أن يبتدئ أولا بحفظ كتاب ( معيار العلم ) الذي هو الملقب بالمنطق عندهم .
Yes, their statement that the logical matters must be mastered is correct. But logic is not exclusive to them; rather, it is the principle that we call in the art of Kalam 'Book of Investigation' ('Kitab al-Nazar'). They merely changed its wording to logic to intimidate. We have also called it 'Book of Dialectic' and 'Perceptions of Intellects' ('Madarik al-Uqool'). So when the cunning weak person hears the name 'logic', he thinks it is a strange art unknown to the mutakallimun and known only to philosophers. To refute this illusion and uproot this trick of misguidance, we think it best to devote a discussion to 'Perceptions of Intellects' in this book, and to abandon in it the terminology of the mutakallimun and usuliyyun; rather we will present it in the expressions of the logicians, and cast it in their molds, and follow their traces word for word, and debate them in this book in their language - that is, using their expressions in logic. And we will clarify that what they have stipulated regarding the correctness of the matter of the syllogism in the demonstrative section of logic, and what they stipulated regarding its form in the Book of Syllogism, and the rules they laid down in Isagoge and Categories, which are parts of logic and its premises, they were not able to fulfill any of it in their divine sciences. But we think it best to present the 'Perceptions of Intellects' at the end of the book, for it is like the tool for attaining the aim of the book. However, there may be a reader who dispenses with it in understanding, so we postpone it until those who do not need it may disregard it; and whoever does not understand our expressions in the individual issues in refuting them should first study the book 'Mi'yaar al-'Ilm' (Standard of Knowledge), which is called 'Logic' among them.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a concession and a strategic plan. Ghazali first concedes the necessity of logic, but immediately strips the philosophers of their supposed monopoly: logic is actually the same discipline that mutakallimun call 'Kitab al-Nazar' (Book of Investigation). The philosophers merely renamed it to 'logic' to intimidate. He then outlines his strategy: he will compose a section called 'Madaarik al-Uqool' (Perceptions of Intellects) using only the terminology of the logicians, following their rules exactly, and then demonstrate that they themselves fail to meet their own conditions for demonstrative syllogisms in their divine sciences. He plans to place this section at the end of the book because some readers can dispense with it; those who need it should first study his earlier work 'Mi'yaar al-'Ilm', which covers logic in their terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is against logic and rejects its use in theology.
Correction: Ghazali affirms that logical reasoning is necessary and that it is the same as the mutakallimun's 'Kitab al-Nazar'. His objection is only to the philosophers' claim of exclusivity and their use of logic to support false conclusions in divine sciences.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's main purpose in writing the 'Madaarik al-Uqool' section using the philosophers' logical terminology?
A) To prove that logic is useless for divine science.
B) To show that the philosophers fail to meet their own conditions for demonstrative syllogisms in divine sciences.
C) To translate Kalam works into Greek logical terms.
D) To discourage readers from studying logic.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali strategically concedes the necessity of logic but denies its exclusivity to philosophers, planning to refute them by using their own logical standards to show they fail in divine sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely encountered the term 'logic' with suspicion. Has that suspicion ever stopped you from learning a tool that could help you defend your faith? Will you now take the step to study it as Ghazali did?
?
If you were a student in Ghazali's time, would you be among those who dismiss logic as foreign, or among those who learn it to refute falsehood? Which category do you belong to today?
?
Ghazali calls logic the 'Perceptions of Intellects'. Are you using your intellect to perceive truth, or are you relying on blind imitation? What intellectual tool are you neglecting that could bring you closer to certainty?
